INTRODUCTIONCOMES NOW the Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez ("Defendant" or "Movant"), appearing pro se but invoking the full protections of the Indiana Rules of Trial Procedure, the Americans with Disabilities Act ("ADA"), and the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, to respectfully move this Honorable Court to immediately vacate the Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure entered on May 28, 2025. Defendant further moves this Court to quash the Writ of Assistance issued on November 6, 2025, and executed on December 9, 2025, and to set aside all related enforcement actions, including the Sheriff's Sale and the subsequent Sheriff's Deed.This motion presents a profound intersection of jurisdictional failure and civil rights deprivation. The totality of the evidence demonstrates that the Plaintiff, Financial Center First Credit Union ("FCFCU" or "Plaintiff"), obtained a six-figure foreclosure judgment through service of process that was both procedurally defective under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B) and constitutionally deficient under the ADA. First, the Plaintiff entirely failed to comply with the unambiguously mandatory mailing requirement of Trial Rule 4.1(B), rendering the ensuing default judgment void ab initio for lack of personal jurisdiction. Second, the Plaintiff engineered this default by deliberately ignoring actual, documented knowledge of the Defendant's severe cognitive and psychiatric disabilities—including Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder (ADHD), Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder (PTSD), and profound executive dysfunction.Rather than engaging in an interactive accommodation process or seeking court-ordered alternative service as required by law, the Plaintiff exploited the Defendant's known cognitive vulnerabilities. The Plaintiff utilized an unreliable "copy left" service method, guaranteed to fail against an individual with the Defendant's specific impairments, and concealed this reality from the Court. The resulting enforcement actions have caused catastrophic, irreparable, and ongoing harm to the Defendant, including unconstitutional physical displacement into freezing weather, the destruction of his professional livelihood, the arbitrary disposal of his personal property into a dump truck, and the derailment of a co-habitant's federal asylum proceedings.Furthermore, the Plaintiff has already confessed to the Court that the financial calculus underpinning the default judgment was wildly inaccurate. On February 24, 2026, the Plaintiff filed a Nunc Pro Tunc petition admitting a nearly $10,000 "scrivener's error" in its interest calculations. When combined with the documented history of "servicing friction"—including unauthorized payment reversals and burdensome fee cycles—the Defendant presents a highly meritorious defense to the underlying claims.Because the judgment was entered without personal jurisdiction, it is a legal nullity that must be vacated pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 60(B)(6). Alternatively, the Defendant is entitled to relief under Trial Rule 60(B)(1) due to excusable neglect stemming directly from his severe disability-related impairments, supported by a proven meritorious defense. The Defendant respectfully requests that this Court vacate the judgment, restore the status quo ante, and mandate ADA-compliant engagement moving forward.II. STATEMENT OF FACTSThe factual record in this matter reveals a systemic failure by the Plaintiff to adhere to fundamental procedural rules and federal disability mandates. The following chronological narrative integrates the totality of the evidence, detailing the pre-litigation outreach, the fatally defective service, the ensuing physical displacement, and the ultimate destruction of the Defendant's property.A. The Defendant's Documented Disabilities and Statutory StatusThe Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez, is a qualified individual with disabilities within the meaning of Title II of the Americans with Disabilities Act, 42 U.S.C. § 12102. The Defendant has been formally diagnosed with a constellation of severe cognitive and psychiatric impairments, prominently including Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder (PTSD), severe Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder (ADHD), acute depression, anxiety, trauma-related hypervigilance, delayed auditory processing, and profound executive dysfunction.These conditions are not mere psychological discomforts; they act as severe neurological barriers to processing unstructured, high-stress, or irregular communications. Executive dysfunction fundamentally disrupts the brain's ability to plan, initiate tasks, follow multi-part bureaucratic instructions, and navigate rapidly changing logistical environments. In the context of legal proceedings, a litigant suffering from untreated or severely triggered PTSD and ADHD experiences extreme difficulty interpreting irregular notices, resulting in what appears to an outside observer as non-compliance, but is in reality physiological paralysis.Statistical Integration: Disabilities in the Justice SystemThe systemic failure to accommodate cognitive disabilities profoundly impacts justice system outcomes. Statistical data regarding the intersection of cognitive impairments and legal compliance illustrates the severe disadvantage faced by individuals like the Defendant.Demographic & Diagnostic Data in the Legal SystemStatistical FindingsSourceADHD Prevalence in Incarcerated PopulationsUp to 1 in 4 individuals in the prison system have ADHD (over 8 times the rate of the general population).Youth and Adult Incarceration DisparityADHD rates are 5 times higher in youth justice facilities and 10 times higher in adult facilities compared to non-justice populations.Victimization of Persons with Cognitive DisabilitiesPersons with cognitive disabilities experience the highest rate of violent victimization (83.3 per 1,000 individuals) among all disability categories.Overall Victimization DisparityPersons with disabilities account for 26% of all nonfatal violent crime victims, despite comprising only 12% of the general population.PTSD in Specific Demographics (Veterans)12% to 13% of returning Iraq war veterans, and numerous civilians subjected to systemic trauma, are formally diagnosed with PTSD, severely impacting their social and legal navigation.These statistics underscore a critical reality: the justice system's reliance on rigid, unaccommodated procedural mechanisms disproportionately penalizes, marginalizes, and victimizes individuals with cognitive impairments. The Plaintiff’s actions in this case—exploiting the Defendant’s cognitive paralysis rather than accommodating it—perfectly encapsulate this systemic disparity.B. "Servicing Friction," Manufactured Default, and Pre-Litigation OutreachThe origins of the alleged default are rooted not in willful non-payment, but in severe "servicing friction" engineered by the Plaintiff's own broken operational systems. The Defendant's mortgage accounts with FCFCU reflect a highly irregular history of returned and adjusted online payments. On multiple occasions, the Defendant submitted payments online, the funds visibly left his account, but the Plaintiff subsequently reversed the payments. Due to his executive dysfunction, the Defendant did not immediately realize these reversals were occurring in real time.Rather than assisting the Defendant, the Plaintiff's systems generated a cascading series of stop-payment or insufficient-funds style entries, adding exorbitant fee activity across the mortgage accounts. When the Defendant attempted to circumvent these digital failures, the Plaintiff imposed highly burdensome payment requirements, such as adding costs for telephone payments and requiring the Defendant to secure in-person cash or money orders.In an attempt to resolve these issues and communicate his impairments, the Defendant initiated repeated pre-litigation outreach. The record confirms the existence of a certified hardship mailing sent by the Defendant to the Plaintiff in 2023. Furthermore, the Defendant transmitted a specific, documented hardship letter to the Plaintiff on December 4, 2024. Through these communications, the Plaintiff acquired actual, documented knowledge of the Defendant's disabilities and communication limitations well before the initiation of the foreclosure action. Despite this repeated outreach, the Plaintiff engaged in a pattern of "institutional non-engagement," documenting no meaningful response or accommodation to the Defendant's requests.C. The Defective Service of Process and Resulting Void Default JudgmentOn January 2025, the Plaintiff initiated Cause No. 49D03-2501-MF-002559 in the Marion Superior/Circuit Court. Knowing full well that the Defendant suffered from severe auditory processing delays, PTSD, and executive dysfunction, the Plaintiff nonetheless chose to rely on the absolute minimum standard of service: "copy service" under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(A)(3).The Sheriff's Returns, dated March 5, 2025, and April 15, 2025, indicate that a process server merely left a copy of the summons at the Defendant's residence at 4817 Shadow Pointe Drive. Crucially, the returns confirm the following undeniable facts:No personal service occurred.No delivery was made to a person sixteen years of age or older.No follow-up mailing was performed or documented..Under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B), any party utilizing "copy service" is strictly mandated to follow up by sending a copy of the summons to the defendant via first-class mail, and this fact must be shown upon the return. The Plaintiff entirely failed to execute this jurisdictional prerequisite. The record contains no certified mail receipts, no clerk certifications, and no verifiable evidence of dispatch to the Defendant. Notably, the Plaintiff managed to perfectly execute and document certified mail service for the corporate co-defendants, American Express National Bank and JP Morgan Chase Bank, N.A., on April 28, 2025 , highlighting the targeted failure regarding the disabled Defendant.Because the Defendant's cognitive impairments make irregular "door-knocking" or "copy left" methods functionally invisible and unreliable, and because no supplementary mailing ever occurred, the Defendant never received actual notice of the foreclosure action. The Defendant resided continuously at the property and was present approximately 99% of the time during the attempted service dates, yet received no summons, complaint, or written directive.Despite this absolute failure to establish personal jurisdiction, the Plaintiff moved for default. On May 28, 2025, operating in an informational vacuum created by the Plaintiff's failure to disclose the defective service or the Defendant's disabilities, the Court entered an Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure in the amount of $220,863.09.D. The Writ of Assistance and the Catastrophic, Unconstitutional DisplacementRelying on the void judgment, the Plaintiff accelerated its enforcement actions. A Sheriff's Sale was held on August 15, 2025. Despite the sale, public records continued to deceptively reflect the Defendant as the owner of the property as late as December 1, 2025. On December 2, 2025, the Plaintiff finally recorded the Sheriff's Deed.A mere seven days later, on December 9, 2025, without providing any notice of an execution date, the Plaintiff utilized a Writ of Assistance to forcibly remove the Defendant from his home. The Defendant, alongside his co-habitant, Zai, and his dog, Gigi, were thrust into freezing weather without warning.The trauma of the armed removal severely exacerbated the Defendant's PTSD and ADHD, triggering intense dissociation, heightened startle responses, and profound functional exhaustion. The cruelty of the displacement was compounded by the Plaintiff's agents; after the Defendant was forced to spend the night freezing in his vehicle, an FCFCU representative identified as Ms. Abbott approached the car to aggressively reprimand the Defendant for "being late".The displacement caused absolute devastation across all facets of the Defendant's life:Economic Ruin: The Defendant was forced to relocate to Evansville, Indiana, immediately severing his repeat client base in Indianapolis. His rideshare income collapsed, forcing him to accept emergency part-time labor at $14.50 per hour just to survive. He faces ongoing utility-related stress and potential service interruptions.Housing Instability: The Defendant and Zai are currently sheltered in a temporary, short-term elderly care apartment belonging to the Defendant's medically fragile, oxygen-dependent mother. The conditions are extremely substandard; the Defendant and Zai share a single twin-sized bed surrounded by boxes, and the dog has occasionally been relegated to the vehicle. They face impending eviction from this temporary space within days.Legal and Immigration Prejudice: The abrupt instability and lack of reliable internet access (where simple file syncing takes hours) destroyed the Defendant's ability to secure counsel or respond to court deadlines. Most tragically, the inability to monitor communications caused Zai's federal asylum case to be closed, severely prejudicing their immigration status and forcing them to rely on a pending Special Immigrant Visa (SIV) process under worsened conditions.E. The Arbitrary "Dump Truck" Incident: Denial of Reasonable AccommodationsFollowing the unconstitutional eviction, the Defendant filed emergency motions to stay the writ and preserve his personal property. The Plaintiff eventually scheduled a so-called "property retrieval" window for March 25, 2026, strictly imposing an arbitrary two-hour timeframe from 9:00 a.m. to 11:00 a.m..The Defendant, traveling three hours from his displaced location in Evansville, notified the Plaintiff of his travel constraints but still managed to arrive at the property before the 11:00 a.m. cutoff. What he encountered was not a retrieval process, but a destruction operation.The Plaintiff provided no structured instructions, no onsite coordinator, and no inventory of the property. Instead, a removal crew was actively discarding the Defendant's personal belongings directly into a dump truck, which was already substantially filled. The dump truck was observed departing and returning, indicating continuous disposal. The Plaintiff's representative remained sequestered in a vehicle, entirely ignoring the Defendant's presence and ignoring communications from the Defendant's counsel.For a disabled individual suffering from executive dysfunction, navigating an unstructured, high-stress environment where property is actively being destroyed is an insurmountable cognitive barrier. The lack of coordination made it functionally unsafe and impossible for the Defendant to participate without risk of misunderstanding or injury.In a subsequent filing on March 26, 2026, the Plaintiff attempted to mislead the Court by claiming the Defendant "failed to appear" within the window. However, the Plaintiff's own narrative revealed fatal contradictions: while enforcing the 11:00 a.m. cutoff against the Defendant under the guise of "security," the Plaintiff admitted that its representative remained onsite until 1:00 p.m. and returned at 2:45 p.m., and that large items were not even moved into the truck until after 11:00 a.m.. This proves that the strict time limit was a pretextual weapon used to deny the Defendant meaningful ADA access to his property. Furthermore, upon examining the few items that were salvageable, the Defendant discovered severe damage to his personal property that did not exist prior to the Plaintiff's intervention.F. The Plaintiff's Nunc Pro Tunc Admission of Mathematical InaccuracyWhile the Defendant suffered total displacement and property destruction, the Plaintiff was quietly forced to admit to the Court that its financial claims were mathematically corrupt. On February 24, 2026, the Plaintiff filed a "Petition for Determination of Disposition of Claimed Surplus Funds and Motion for Nunc Pro Tunc Entry".In this petition, the Plaintiff confessed to a massive "scrivener's error" regarding the interest calculations presented to the Court. The Plaintiff admitted that its original bid form, based on the judgment, claimed an interest amount of $10,725.27. The Plaintiff conceded that this figure improperly reflected total overdue interest rather than the specific 30-day period allowed by the judgment, which should have been only $823.50.The Plaintiff requested a Nunc Pro Tunc entry to retroactively reduce its credit bid from $240,464.20 to $230,562.40—a nearly $10,000 reduction—specifically to avoid having to pay surplus funds into the Court. This staggering admission not only demonstrates the Plaintiff's sloppy, bad-faith litigation tactics, but serves as irrefutable proof that the underlying $220,863.09 Default Judgment was predicated on inaccurate, unverified, and highly prejudicial financial data.Chronology of Key EventsAction / Consequence2023 & Dec. 4, 2024Defendant sends certified Hardship Letters. Plaintiff acquires actual knowledge of disabilities but fails to engage.Mar. 5 & Apr. 15, 2025Sheriff attempts "copy service." No mail sent. Defendant is displaced into freezing weather; Zai's asylum case collapses.Feb. 24, 2026Plaintiff files Nunc Pro Tunc petition, admitting to a nearly $10,000 error in interest calculations.Mar. 25, 2026The "Dump Truck" Incident. Plaintiff destroys property, refuses reasonable accommodations for Defendant's executive dysfunction.III. LEGAL ARGUMENTThe jurisprudence of the State of Indiana maintains a "marked judicial deference for deciding disputes on their merits and for giving parties their day in court." Seleme v. JP Morgan Chase Bank, 982 N.E.2d 299, 310 (Ind. Ct. App. 2012). It is a bedrock principle that any doubt as to the propriety of a default judgment must be resolved in favor of the defaulted party. Allstate Ins. Co. v. Watson, 747 N.E.2d 545, 547 (Ind. 2001).This Court must vacate the May 28, 2025 default judgment because it is void for lack of personal jurisdiction under Trial Rule 60(B)(6) due to the Plaintiff's complete failure to comply with the mandatory mailing requirement of Trial Rule 4.1(B). Furthermore, the Plaintiff’s refusal to accommodate the Defendant's known cognitive disabilities constitutes a flagrant violation of the Americans with Disabilities Act and the Due Process Clause. Alternatively, the Defendant is entitled to relief under Trial Rule 60(B)(1) for excusable neglect, supported by a highly meritorious defense.A. The Default Judgment is Void Ab Initio for Lack of Personal Jurisdiction Under Trial Rule 60(B)(6) Due to the Plaintiff's Failure to Comply with Trial Rule 4.1(B).The threshold issue in any civil proceeding is whether the court possesses personal jurisdiction over the defendant. A judgment rendered without personal jurisdiction is a legal nullity—it is void ab initio. Stidham v. Welchel, 698 N.E.2d 1152, 1156 (Ind. 1998). Under Indiana Trial Rule 60(B)(6), a court must relieve a party from a judgment if "the judgment is void". A motion seeking relief from a void judgment may be filed within a "reasonable time" and does not require the movant to allege a meritorious defense, because the court possessed no power to enter the order in the first instance.Here, the Court never acquired personal jurisdiction over the Defendant because the Plaintiff utterly failed to effectuate valid service of process.1. Trial Rule 4.1(B) Imposes an Unambiguously Mandatory Mailing Requirement.The Plaintiff attempted to serve the Defendant using "copy service" under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(A)(3), which permits service by "leaving a copy of the summons and complaint at his dwelling house or usual place of abode". However, the Indiana Supreme Court has strictly conditioned the validity of copy service upon mandatory follow-up mailing. Trial Rule 4.1(B) explicitly states:(B) Copy Service to Be Followed With Mail. When service is made under subdivision (A)(3) or (A)(4), the person making the service also must send by first class mail, a copy of the summons and the complaint to the address on the summons of the person being served, and this fact must be shown upon the return.  (Emphasis added).The appellate courts of Indiana have consistently held that the mailing requirement of Rule 4.1(B) is an absolute jurisdictional prerequisite. In the seminal case Barrow v. Pennington, 700 N.E.2d 477 (Ind. Ct. App. 1998), the Court of Appeals confronted a default judgment where a sheriff left a summons at a residence but failed to mail a copy. The Barrow court unequivocally declared that "Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B) is unambiguously mandatory". The court ruled that "compliance with T.R. 4.1(B), itself, is a jurisdictional prerequisite to obtaining personal jurisdiction" and that "service of process in contravention of T.R. 4.1(B) is not sufficient to confer personal jurisdiction over a defendant".This strict adherence to Rule 4.1(B) has been repeatedly affirmed. In LePore v. Norwest Bank Indiana, N.A., 860 N.E.2d 632 (Ind. Ct. App. 2007), the court reiterated that failure to follow up delivery of a complaint with mailing constitutes ineffective service of process. Similarly, in LaPalme v. Romero, 621 N.E.2d 1102 (Ind. 1993), the Indiana Supreme Court established that where service methods fail to comply with the prescribed rules, the court cannot properly assert personal jurisdiction.The record in this case mirrors the exact fatal defect found in Barrow. The Sheriff's Returns dated March 5, 2025, and April 15, 2025, indicate only that a copy was left at the Defendant's dwelling. The returns conclusively establish that no personal service occurred and, most importantly, no follow-up mailing was performed or documented. The Plaintiff's failure to execute the statutorily mandated mailing means that service was never legally consummated, and personal jurisdiction never attached.2. Trial Rule 4.15(F) Cannot Cure a Total Failure to Comply with Rule 4.1(B).When confronted with defective service, plaintiffs routinely attempt to invoke Indiana Trial Rule 4.15(F), the "savings clause," which provides that no summons shall be set aside if it is "reasonably calculated to inform the person to be served". However, Indiana jurisprudence categorically rejects the application of Rule 4.15(F) to cure a complete failure to mail under Rule 4.1(B).In Swiggett Lumber Construction Co. v. Quandt, 806 N.E.2d 334 (Ind. Ct. App. 2004), a plaintiff left a summons with an employee at a business but failed to mail a copy as required by T.R. 4.1(B). The plaintiff argued that leaving the copy was "reasonably calculated" to inform the defendant under T.R. 4.15(F). The Court of Appeals forcefully rejected this argument, vacating the default judgment. The Swiggett court held that while T.R. 4.15(F) can cure technical defects if a party "substantially complies" with the rules, it "will not excuse noncompliance with trial rule 4.1(B)" because failing to mail the document entirely constitutes "no attempt whatsoever to comply with 4.1(B)".Likewise, in Boczar v. Reuben, 742 N.E.2d 1010 (Ind. Ct. App. 2001), the court noted that the purpose behind Rule 4.1(B) is to "increase the odds that the served party will receive timely notice of the suit," and confirmed that in the context of a default judgment, the strict mandate of Barrow applies.Here, the Plaintiff made "no attempt whatsoever" to comply with the mailing requirement of T.R. 4.1(B). The Plaintiff produced no certified mail receipts, no clerk certifications, and no verifiable evidence of dispatch for the Defendant, despite fastidiously ensuring such documentation was recorded for the corporate co-defendants on April 28, 2025. Because the Plaintiff failed to execute the mandatory mailing, T.R. 4.15(F) cannot save the defective service. The Default Judgment is a legal nullity, entirely void for lack of personal jurisdiction, and must be vacated immediately under Trial Rule 60(B)(6).B. The Plaintiff and the Court Violated Title II of the ADA and the Due Process Clause by Failing to Accommodate the Defendant's Known Disabilities.Even if this Court were to ignore the strict jurisdictional mandates of T.R. 4.1(B) and entertain the notion that leaving a piece of paper on a door constitutes "mechanical compliance" with notice statutes, the service in this case was patently unconstitutional. The Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment requires that notice be "reasonably calculated, under all the circumstances, to apprise interested parties of the pendency of the action and afford them an opportunity to present their objections." Mullane v. Cent. Hanover Bank & Trust Co., 339 U.S. 306, 314 (1950).When a plaintiff possesses actual knowledge that a defendant suffers from severe cognitive and psychiatric disabilities that render conventional notice ineffective, rigid adherence to basic "copy left" service ceases to be reasonably calculated. Under the synthesis of the Due Process Clause and the Americans with Disabilities Act, the Plaintiff had an affirmative duty to modify its service methods, and the failure to do so invalidates the judgment.1. Title II of the ADA Guarantees "Meaningful Access" to the Judicial System.Title II of the ADA unequivocally mandates that "no qualified individual with a disability shall, by reason of such disability, be excluded from participation in or be denied the benefits of the services, programs, or activities of a public entity." 42 U.S.C. § 12132. The judicial system, including its rules of civil procedure and mechanisms for service of process, is the quintessential "program or activity" of a public entity.In the watershed decision Tennessee v. Lane, 541 U.S. 509 (2004), the United States Supreme Court confirmed that Title II of the ADA validly abrogates state sovereign immunity specifically to protect the fundamental right of access to the courts under the Due Process Clause. The Lane Court emphasized that Congress enacted the ADA to combat a pervasive history of unequal treatment in the administration of public services and that the ADA requires states and their instrumentalities to make "reasonable accommodations" to ensure individuals with disabilities can exercise their fundamental rights.This right to access goes beyond physical wheelchair ramps; it requires providing "meaningful access" to the legal system itself. As established in Alexander v. Choate, 469 U.S. 287 (1985), discrimination occurs when an entity fails to provide "meaningful access to the benefit" it offers. To assure meaningful access, "reasonable accommodations in the grantee's program or benefit may have to be made." Id. at 301. In the context of judicial proceedings, meaningful access requires courts and litigants utilizing state power to adjust procedural mechanisms—such as the method of legal notice and the structuring of property retrieval—to ensure that cognitive impairments do not result in a de facto denial of due process.2. The Plaintiff's Actual Knowledge Triggered an Affirmative Duty to Modify Service.The record is absolute: the Plaintiff possessed actual, documented knowledge of the Defendant's severe disabilities long before filing the foreclosure complaint. The Defendant submitted a certified hardship mailing in 2023 and a specific hardship letter on December 4, 2024, detailing his communication limitations.The Defendant’s diagnosed conditions—ADHD, PTSD, delayed auditory processing, and profound executive dysfunction—are highly complex neurological impairments. Executive dysfunction destroys the ability to initiate tasks, follow multi-part instructions, and process irregular communications. When subjected to the trauma of potential homelessness, these conditions trigger hypervigilance, dissociation, and cognitive paralysis. As the statistics integrated into Section II(A) demonstrate, these precise cognitive vulnerabilities lead to vastly disproportionate negative outcomes in the justice system. For an individual trapped in executive dysfunction, an irregular "copy left" service method—a piece of paper taped to a door without any supplementary electronic or direct communication—is functionally invisible.Under the Mullane standard, notice must be "such as one desirous of actually informing the absentee might reasonably adopt." 339 U.S. at 315. A plaintiff desirous of actually informing a disabled defendant known to suffer from severe executive dysfunction would not rely exclusively on a method known to be ineffective against that specific disability.Once the Plaintiff became aware of the Defendant's disability, the ADA required "reasonable modifications" to ensure meaningful access. The Plaintiff had numerous avenues available to satisfy this duty. The Plaintiff could have petitioned the Court under Trial Rule 4.14 for a "Special Order of Court" to permit service via "email, or other technology" tailored to the Defendant's communication capabilities. Instead, the Plaintiff deliberately chose the path of least resistance, utilizing a defective "copy left" method it knew would fail, and intentionally withheld the Defendant's ADA status from the Court and the executing Sheriff.This deliberate concealment created an "informational vacuum" that prevented the Court from evaluating the adequacy of the notice or providing its own ADA accommodations. Because the Plaintiff weaponized the Defendant's disability to manufacture a default, the service of process violated fundamental fairness and the Due Process Clause, rendering the judgment void.C. In the Alternative, the Defendant is Entitled to Relief Under Trial Rule 60(B)(1) for Excusable Neglect, Supported by a Highly Meritorious Defense.Should this Court somehow determine that technical jurisdiction attached, the Defendant is undeniably entitled to have the judgment vacated under Indiana Trial Rule 60(B)(1), which allows a court to set aside a judgment for "mistake, surprise, or excusable neglect" provided the motion is filed within one year and alleges a meritorious defense.1. Severe Cognitive Impairment Constitutes Excusable Neglect.Indiana law specifically recognizes that "sickness of a party" and severe mental impairments constitute valid grounds for excusable neglect under Rule 60(B)(1). In Seleme v. JP Morgan Chase Bank, 982 N.E.2d 299 (Ind. Ct. App. 2012), the court recognized that a defendant suffering from "compromised higher cerebral functioning" that impaired general cognitive abilities had a valid basis to argue excusable neglect for failing to respond to a summary judgment motion.Here, the Defendant's failure to respond was not the result of apathy, willful evasion, or disrespect for the judicial process. It was the direct physiological consequence of his documented disabilities—executive dysfunction, ADHD, and trauma-induced dissociation. These conditions materially incapacitated his ability to interpret irregular notices and execute the complex bureaucratic steps required to file a responsive pleading. Penalizing a disabled litigant with a $220,000 default judgment for the biological realities of their cognitive impairment directly contravenes the equitable, remedial spirit of Trial Rule 60(B).2. The Record Establishes a Highly Meritorious Defense of Mathematical Inaccuracy and Servicing Friction.To succeed under Rule 60(B)(1), a movant must allege a meritorious defense indicating that a different result would be reached if the case were tried on the merits. The Defendant possesses a profound meritorious defense, supported by the Plaintiff's own admissions.First, the record demonstrates severe "servicing friction" caused by the Plaintiff's broken payment portals. The Defendant's accounts show a pattern where payments were submitted, funds departed the account, but were later reversed by the Plaintiff, triggering cascading insufficient-funds fees. The Defendant's default was artificially accelerated by these systemic failures and the Plaintiff's refusal to process the Defendant's 2023 and 2024 hardship requests.Second, and most damningly, the Plaintiff has already confessed to the Court that the financial calculus of the judgment is corrupt. On February 24, 2026, the Plaintiff filed a "Petition for Determination of Disposition of Claimed Surplus Funds and Motion for Nunc Pro Tunc Entry," admitting to a massive "scrivener's error". The Plaintiff admitted its submitted interest calculation was inflated by nearly $10,000—claiming $10,725.27 when the correct interest allowed by the judgment was only $823.50. The Plaintiff sought to retroactively lower its credit bid to $230,562.40 solely to avoid paying surplus funds into the Court registry.This staggering admission proves that the underlying $220,863.09 Default Judgment was predicated on inaccurate, unverified accounting by the Plaintiff. The Defendant has an absolute right to challenge these phantom debts, the inflated interest, and the illegal fees in a fair, adversarial proceeding. The requirements of Rule 60(B)(1) are overwhelmingly satisfied.IV. SUBSTANTIAL HARM AND PREJUDICE TO THE DEFENDANTThe Plaintiff's reliance on a void judgment has inflicted catastrophic, irreparable, and ongoing harm upon the Defendant, necessitating immediate equitable intervention to restore the status quo ante.1. Forced Displacement and Economic Devastation
> Operating on the void judgment, the Plaintiff executed a Writ of Assistance on December 9, 2025, without providing prior notice of the execution date. The Defendant, his co-habitant Zai, and his dog Gigi were physically thrown into freezing weather. The Defendant was forced to relocate three hours away to Evansville, Indiana, immediately destroying his established repeat client base in Indianapolis and collapsing his rideshare income. He has been forced to accept $14.50/hour emergency labor and is currently sleeping on a twin bed in his elderly, oxygen-dependent mother's apartment, facing impending eviction from that temporary space.2. Deprivation of Federal Immigration Rights
> The sudden, unconstitutional displacement severed the Defendant's access to reliable communication infrastructure. As a direct and proximate result of this instability, the federal asylum case of the Defendant's co-habitant, Zai, was closed. The Plaintiff's actions have thus resulted in extreme legal prejudice, forcing Zai to rely on a pending SIV process under drastically worsened conditions.3. The ADA Violation During Property Destruction (March 25, 2026)
> The Plaintiff's bad faith culminated in the "dump truck" incident on March 25, 2026. Despite knowing of the Defendant's executive dysfunction, the Plaintiff imposed a rigid 9:00 a.m. to 11:00 a.m. retrieval window. The Defendant arrived to find no structured inventory and no onsite coordinator; instead, a crew was actively throwing his personal property into a dump truck. This chaotic, unstructured environment made it functionally impossible for the Defendant to participate without severe cognitive distress and risk of injury.The Plaintiff then lied to the Court, claiming the Defendant "failed to appear" within the window, while simultaneously admitting that FCFCU representatives remained onsite until 1:00 p.m., and that large items were not loaded until after 11:00 a.m.. This proves the 11:00 a.m. cutoff was an arbitrary, pretextual weapon used to deny the Defendant meaningful access to his property, resulting in the permanent loss and destruction of irreplaceable belongings.The balance of equities overwhelmingly favors the Defendant. The Plaintiff must not be permitted to retain the fruits of an unconstitutional, jurisdictionally void foreclosure.V. CONCLUSION AND PRAYER FOR RELIEFThe Amended Default Judgment entered on May 28, 2025, is a legal nullity. The Plaintiff completely failed to comply with the mandatory mailing requirement of Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B), depriving this Court of personal jurisdiction. Furthermore, the Plaintiff’s deliberate refusal to adapt its service and property retrieval procedures to accommodate the Defendant's known, severe cognitive disabilities violates the Americans with Disabilities Act and offends the core tenets of constitutional due process. The resulting harm—economic devastation, property destruction, and the derailment of federal immigration proceedings—demands immediate rectification.WHEREFORE, the Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez, respectfully prays that this Honorable Court grant this Motion and enter an Order providing the following relief:VACATE the Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure entered on May 28, 2025, declaring it void ab initio pursuant to Trial Rule 60(B)(6), and/or excusable under Trial Rule 60(B)(1);QUASH the Writ of Assistance issued on November 6, 2025, and executed on December 9, 2025;SET ASIDE the Sheriff's Sale held on August 15, 2025, and strike the resulting Sheriff's Deed recorded on December 2, 2025;ENJOIN the Plaintiff, its agents, and contractors from further removing, disposing of, selling, or destroying any of the Defendant's personal property;MANDATE that the Plaintiff engage in an interactive ADA accommodation process to provide the Defendant with structured, meaningful, and safe access to retrieve his remaining property;AWARD the Defendant costs and reasonable fees incurred in defending this unconstitutional deprivation of property; andGRANT all such other and further relief as the Court deems just and proper in the premises.Respectfully submitted,Attorney/Pro Se Party for Defendant Cody Rice-Velasquez[Address][Phone][Email]CERTIFICATE OF SERVICEI hereby certify that on this 4th day of April, 2026, a true and correct copy of the foregoing Notice of Motion and Motion to Vacate Default Judgment was served upon all counsel of record via the Indiana E-Filing System (IEFS) in accordance with Indiana Trial Rule 86 , and/or by First Class U.S. Mail, postage prepaid, to the following:Counsel for Plaintiff:Scott A. ### Assistant
> I’m pressure-testing the motion for what actually survives a judge’s pen. The big question is not whether the story is compelling. It is whether the filing stays inside the lanes that can still win relief.

### Assistant
> Found a potential landmine. Your draft says no follow-up mailing was performed or documented, but your own case materials summarize both service returns as “copy left and mailed.” If the underlying returns really say that, the current draft overstates the defect and hands them an easy credibility hit.

### Assistant
> ## Bottom line
> 
> This has a real spine, but **I would not file it as written**.
> 
> Your best argument is still the same one: **lack of personal jurisdiction if Trial Rule 4.1(B) was not actually satisfied**. Indiana treats the follow-up mailing after copy service as mandatory, jurisdictional, and not saved by Rule 4.15(F) if there was a total failure to comply. citeturn634118view0turn634118view1turn634118view2
> 
> But right now the motion is trying to do too much, and some of it is pointed at the wrong target.
> 
> ## What is strong
> 
> ### 1. The threshold framing is right
> You are correctly attacking the judgment as **void**, not just unfair. If copy service was used and Rule 4.1(B) was not actually completed or not properly shown on the return, that is the kind of defect Indiana courts treat seriously. citeturn634118view1turn634118view2
> 
> ### 2. The May-to-December timeline is a useful counter to the “ample time” narrative
> That uploaded critique is right: you need to isolate the difference between **judgment**, **public-facing transfer**, and **actual dispossession**. That is a good strategic exhibit idea. fileciteturn0file0
> 
> ### 3. The property retrieval section has leverage
> Not as a centerpiece of the Rule 60 motion, but as **prejudice** and as a later **conversion / bailment / spoliation** lane. The critique you uploaded is also right that this should be framed less as “they were mean” and more as “they exercised control over personal property without preservation structure.” fileciteturn0file0
> 
> ## What will get you hurt
> 
> ### 1. Your service argument currently appears to contradict your own record
> This is the biggest problem.
> 
> Your draft says no follow-up mailing was performed or documented. But your own case materials summarize both returns as:
> 
> - **03/05/2025:** “C1 COPY LEFT AND MAILED”
> - **04/15/2025:** “C1 – COPY LEFT AND MAILED TO ADDRESS PROVIDED”
> 
> and the plaintiff’s default papers / judgment materials also recite service on you and compliance with service requirements. fileciteturn2file0 fileciteturn1file15 fileciteturn1file17
> 
> That does **not** mean your jurisdictional argument is dead. It means you cannot say “no mailing occurred” unless you can prove one of these narrower things:
> 
> - the return is false,
> - the mailing entry is facially deficient under Rule 4.15,
> - the mailing was sent to the wrong address,
> - the return was not properly signed / completed,
> - or the proof in the court file still fails to establish valid service.
> 
> If you do not tighten that, they will say one sentence and crater your credibility:
> > “The record shows copy left and mailed twice.”
> 
> That is a bad place to be.
> 
> ### 2. The ADA section overclaims and blurs the parties
> **Title II applies to public entities, including courts and state/local government programs.** The ADA’s own materials specifically identify courts as Title II-covered programs, and the statute defines a “public entity” as state or local government or its instrumentalities. citeturn832015view0turn832015view1turn634118view4
> 
> A private credit union is **not** a Title II public entity. Private businesses are generally addressed under **Title III**, which governs businesses open to the public. citeturn832015view2turn832015view3
> 
> So this line of your motion is too aggressive:
> - that the **plaintiff** had a standalone **Title II** duty to engage in an “interactive accommodation process” for service of process.
> 
> That is not the clean lane.
> 
> Better lane:
> - **The court** has Title II obligations for meaningful access.
> - **Due process** requires notice reasonably calculated under the circumstances.
> - Your disabilities are a **factual amplifier** showing why bare copy service was not reasonably calculated **if** the plaintiff knew facts making that method predictably ineffective.
> - The plaintiff’s knowledge matters to the due-process fairness narrative.
> - But do **not** present the lender as though it were a Title II public entity. That is legally sloppy.
> 
> ### 3. You are mixing three motions into one
> Right now this reads like:
> 
> - Rule 60(B) jurisdiction motion,
> - ADA/civil-rights brief,
> - damages narrative,
> - property-preservation injunction,
> - foreclosure accounting attack,
> - and proposed merits order.
> 
> That is too much. Judges hate kitchen-sink briefing.
> 
> For this motion, the hierarchy should be:
> 
> **Primary**
> 1. Judgment void for lack of personal jurisdiction under Rule 60(B)(6).
> 
> **Alternative**
> 2. Excusable neglect under Rule 60(B)(1), with meritorious defense.
> 
> **Only then**
> 3. Prejudice flowing from enforcement of the judgment.
> 
> Everything else is support, not a co-headliner.
> 
> ### 4. The statistics section needs to die
> The prison ADHD rates, victimization rates, Iraq veteran PTSD rates — cut it.
> 
> It reads like advocacy padding, not case-specific proof. It does not help you prove:
> - what plaintiff knew,
> - what was served,
> - what was mailed,
> - why you did not respond,
> - or why relief is warranted.
> 
> It makes the brief longer and weaker.
> 
> ### 5. Your requested relief is overextended
> Asking to vacate the judgment is one thing. Asking, in the same breath, to **strike the sheriff’s deed, set aside the sale, quash the writ, enjoin disposal, mandate an ADA process, and award fees** is aggressive in a way that invites the judge to think you are overreaching.
> 
> Especially the fees request. Fees need a real authority basis. Right now that ask is thin.
> 
> Cleaner ask:
> - vacate default judgment,
> - stay or unwind enforcement dependent on that judgment,
> - set prompt status hearing,
> - preserve remaining property,
> - and grant further just relief.
> 
> That is tighter and more credible.
> 
> ### 6. This draft ignores the procedural posture
> Your case materials reflect that you already filed a motion to vacate on **12/10/2025** and had a hearing set for **01/09/2026**. fileciteturn2file0
> 
> So if this is a **new** April 2026 motion, you cannot write like the court is seeing the voidness argument for the first time. You need a one-paragraph procedural honesty section:
> 
> - prior motion filed,
> - prior ruling/position,
> - what is renewed,
> - what is supplemented,
> - what new record now exists.
> 
> If you skip that, the motion feels evasive.
> 
> ## What I would do instead
> 
> ## Rebuild it around one cold thesis
> 
> Use something like this:
> 
> > This motion presents a threshold jurisdictional defect, not a plea for discretionary mercy. Plaintiff elected copy service. Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B) required first-class mailing and proof on the return. If that requirement was not validly completed, the Court never acquired personal jurisdiction and the default judgment is void under Trial Rule 60(B)(6). The disability facts do not replace that argument; they explain why the notice used here was not meaningfully calculated to provide actual notice and why, in the alternative, any nonresponse was excusable neglect under Rule 60(B)(1).
> 
> That is much cleaner.
> 
> ## Recommended structure
> 
> ### I. Procedural posture
> Two short paragraphs only.
> 
> ### II. Narrow statement of facts
> Only facts that matter to:
> - service,
> - notice,
> - nonresponse,
> - timing of actual knowledge,
> - later prejudice.
> 
> ### III. Argument
> #### A. Rule 4.1(B) defect deprived the court of personal jurisdiction
> Only make this argument **if** you can support it against the “copy left and mailed” returns.
> 
> #### B. Rule 4.15(F) does not cure total noncompliance
> Only if subsection A survives. citeturn634118view1turn634118view2
> 
> #### C. Alternatively, Rule 60(B)(1): excusable neglect
> This is where the disability facts belong.
> 
> #### D. Prejudice confirms equitable relief
> Short section. No melodrama.
> 
> ### IV. Relief requested
> Tight and restrained.
> 
> ## Specific cuts
> 
> Delete or heavily shrink these:
> 
> - the national disability statistics table
> - the “engineered default” rhetoric
> - “catastrophic, unconstitutional displacement” style phrasing
> - broad accusations that plaintiff had ADA Title II duties
> - any statement that says no mailing occurred unless you can prove the return is false or insufficient
> - the giant proposed order with findings that sound like your brief copied into an order
> 
> ## Specific upgrades
> 
> ### 1. Attach the actual service returns as exhibits
> Not summaries. The actual returns.
> 
> Because this motion lives or dies on what those returns really show.
> 
> ### 2. If the return only has a shorthand code, explain why that code is not enough
> If that is your real point, make it precisely:
> - not “no mailing happened”
> - but “the file contains no competent proof that Rule 4.1(B) was actually completed as required.”
> 
> That is narrower and safer.
> 
> ### 3. Use disability facts as excusable-neglect support
> That lane is much more believable.
> 
> ### 4. Split the property issues off mentally
> Use them as prejudice now.
> Use them as separate leverage later.
> 
> ### 5. Build the side-by-side timeline exhibit
> The critique you uploaded is right about that. Show:
> 
> | Plaintiff’s implied timeline | Actual actionable timeline |
> |---|---|
> | May 28, 2025 judgment | Dec. 2, 2025 deed recorded |
> | vague “you knew” theory | Dec. 9, 2025 writ executed |
> | “months to retrieve” | meaningful dispossession window compressed |
> 
> That is useful. fileciteturn0file0
> 
> ## Brutal truth
> 
> Your current draft is **emotionally accurate but legally diluted**.
> 
> The good news: the core can be salvaged.
> 
> The bad news: **if the filed returns really say “copy left and mailed,” your current Rule 4.1(B) section is overstated and vulnerable**. That has to be fixed before anything else.
> 
> The winning version is shorter, colder, and narrower:
> - jurisdiction first,
> - excusable neglect second,
> - prejudice third,
> - property destruction as support, not centerpiece,
> - ADA reframed toward court access / meaningful notice, not as a muddled direct Title II claim against a private lender. citeturn832015view0turn832015view1turn832015view2turn832015view3
> 
> If you want the next move, I’ll turn this into a **court-usable stripped-down version** that keeps your best facts and removes the self-sabotage.

### Assistant
> Yes. In that sentence, **“moves” / “further requests”** is there because this is a **motion**, and that is standard motion-drafting language.
> 
> What it is doing:
> 
> - **“COMES NOW … and respectfully moves”** = formal way of saying the movant is asking the court for relief.
> - **“Movant further requests”** = introduces the additional relief tied to the same motion.
> 
> So the “movement” is not random. It is procedural. That part fits.
> 
> That said, the sentence is doing **too much at once**. It is legally acceptable, but a little overloaded. If we want it cleaner, we can tighten it without changing the function.
> 
> ### Current version
> > COMES NOW the Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez ("Defendant" or "Movant"), appearing pro se, and respectfully moves this Honorable Court to vacate the Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure entered on May 28, 2025, pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 60(B). Movant further requests that the Court quash the Writ of Assistance issued on November 6, 2025 and executed on December 9, 2025, and set aside all derivative enforcement measures, including the Sheriff’s Sale and subsequent Sheriff’s Deed.
> 
> ### Why I’d leave the concept but refine the wording
> Because you **do** need:
> - the motion verb,
> - the main relief,
> - and the derivative relief.
> 
> But I’d make it more direct and less ceremonial.
> 
> ### Better version
> > Defendant Cody Rice-Velasquez (“Defendant” or “Movant”), appearing pro se, moves pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 60(B) to vacate the Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure entered on May 28, 2025. Movant further requests that the Court quash the Writ of Assistance issued on November 6, 2025 and executed on December 9, 2025, and set aside the enforcement measures that flowed from that judgment, including the Sheriff’s Sale and subsequent Sheriff’s Deed.
> 
> That keeps the motion language because it belongs there, but removes some of the old-fashioned throat clearing.
> 
> So: **yes, it fits; no, it is not accidental; but it can be sharpened.**

### Assistant
> Yes. Keep **“constitutional insufficiency of notice”** over **“constitutional violations”** in that paragraph.
> 
> Why:
> - It is tighter.
> - It tracks the actual issue before the court.
> - It avoids sounding like you are trying to litigate every moral failure in one sentence.
> 
> Also, **do not add the credit-union values / policy / what credit unions are supposed to be** into paragraph two. That is not where it belongs. It weakens the opening. That material is leverage or optics material, not threshold-jurisdiction material.
> 
> The sharper move is this:
> 
> - **Paragraph 2** = what is legally broken
> - **Paragraph 3** = what they knew and how they used it
> - **Later facts section** = outreach history, timing compression, and downstream harm
> 
> ## The real fix for paragraph two
> 
> Your current problem is that it still sounds a little too absolute on service. Given the judge already leaned on the sheriff’s return, you need to stop sounding like:
> 
> > “the sheriff did not mail it”
> 
> and start sounding like:
> 
> > “the record does not establish legally sufficient notice in any meaningful sense, and the only evidence of mailing is a checkbox-style return that Defendant never actually received and that is unsupported by any corroborating mailing proof.”
> 
> That is much better.
> 
> You are not calling the sheriff a liar.
> You are attacking:
> - the **sufficiency of the proof**,
> - the **reliability of the method**,
> - and the **constitutional adequacy of the notice under the circumstances**.
> 
> That is the lane.
> 
> ## What I would replace paragraph two with
> 
> Use this:
> 
> > This motion arises from the convergence of a jurisdictional defect, constitutionally insufficient notice, and disability-related exclusion from meaningful access to judicial process. Defendant contends that Plaintiff, Financial Center First Credit Union (“FCFCU” or “Plaintiff”), obtained a substantial foreclosure judgment through a service process that was deficient under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B) and, under the circumstances presented here, inadequate to satisfy due process. Although the returns reflect residential copy service and a notation purporting to indicate mailing, Defendant never received the summons and complaint by mail, and the record contains no corroborating proof of actual dispatch beyond the return notation itself. Under these circumstances—particularly where Plaintiff had prior knowledge of Defendant’s significant cognitive and psychiatric impairments, including ADHD, PTSD, and severe executive dysfunction—the method employed was not reasonably calculated to provide meaningful notice or a fair opportunity to respond.
> 
> ## Why this version is better
> 
> It fixes four problems at once:
> 
> ### 1. It stops overclaiming
> You are no longer saying, flatly, “no mailing happened” as though you can prove the sheriff fabricated the return.
> 
> ### 2. It preserves the service challenge
> You are still squarely saying:
> - Rule 4.1(B) is disputed,
> - the proof is thin,
> - and the notice was not good enough.
> 
> ### 3. It sets up Mullane and ADA access cleanly
> Now the disability facts are not random sympathy. They become part of why this notice method was not reasonably calculated.
> 
> ### 4. It leaves room for the timing problem
> The “moving too fast” issue belongs more in the facts and prejudice sections, not crammed into this paragraph.
> 
> ## What not to put here
> 
> Do **not** load paragraph two with:
> - their ethics
> - their stated mission
> - what credit unions are “supposed” to do
> - contradictions with their public branding
> - bad attitude
> - sanctions frustration
> - surplus-fund anger
> 
> All of that muddies the threshold issue.
> 
> ## For paragraph three, your instinct is right
> 
> Yes, you should absolutely state that you **reached out repeatedly for help before suit**.
> 
> That paragraph should carry:
> - certified hardship mailings
> - documented hardship letter
> - email contact through your employer/vendor relationship
> - eventual contact that produced no real assistance
> - their actual knowledge of your communication limitations
> 
> But strip out:
> - “with attitude”
> - “like I did something to them”
> - emotional color unless it proves something concrete
> 
> That part should read like institutional notice plus institutional indifference.
> 
> Here is the version I would use for paragraph three:
> 
> > Rather than pursue modified procedures, seek court-authorized alternative service, or otherwise act in a manner reasonably calculated to provide meaningful notice, Plaintiff proceeded through minimally documented residential copy service despite repeated pre-suit outreach from Defendant. Before this action was filed, Defendant had contacted Plaintiff multiple times through certified hardship correspondence, documented written communications, and email-based outreach connected to an existing professional channel, all of which placed Plaintiff on actual notice of both financial distress and disability-related communication limitations. Yet Plaintiff did not meaningfully engage those limitations, did not adopt a more reliable notice method, and instead proceeded in a manner that predictably converted nonresponse into default. Defendant alleges that the consequences were severe and continuing, including forced displacement during winter conditions, destruction of livelihood, loss and damage to personal property, and collateral prejudice to the immigration proceedings of a co-habitant.
> 
> ## Blunt strategy note
> 
> Your strongest framing is **not**:
> > “they violated ethics and their own values”
> 
> Your strongest framing is:
> > “they had actual notice of conditions making ordinary notice unreliable, used a thin service method anyway, relied on a checkbox instead of meaningful notice, moved quickly into enforcement, and later admitted material financial inaccuracies.”
> 
> That is colder. Better. Harder to wave away.

> Yes on the **amount inconsistencies**.  
> No on the **sexual-coercion incident in the opening**.
> 
> ## 1. Sexual coercion incident: include later, not here
> 
> Do **not** lightly hint at it in the introduction. That would read as explosive, underdeveloped, and easy for them to paint as distracting.
> 
> Use it **later** in the harms section, and only if you can do it in a cold, evidentiary way:
> 
> - tie it directly to the forced displacement and freezing conditions
> - state that the vulnerability arose after the writ execution
> - mention that you have corroborating text messages
> - do **not** over-describe it
> - do **not** make it sound like a side story
> 
> The frame is not:
> > “look how awful everything became”
> 
> The frame is:
> > “the unlawful displacement exposed Defendant to acute physical danger, coercive vulnerability, and survival-based exploitation.”
> 
> That is much stronger.
> 
> ### Best placement
> Put it in **IV. Substantial Harm and Prejudice**, likely under forced displacement, as one short paragraph.
> 
> ### Model language
> > Defendant further alleges that the sudden displacement into freezing conditions created immediate survival-based vulnerability. While attempting to maintain heat in his vehicle after removal, Defendant was subjected to coercive sexual pressure from a third party in circumstances directly traceable to the loss of shelter and emergency conditions caused by the writ’s execution. Defendant has contemporaneous text-message evidence corroborating that incident. Defendant includes this not for sensational effect, but to document the concrete and foreseeable dangers that followed from abrupt displacement without meaningful notice or orderly transition.
> 
> That is enough. Clean. Damaging. Controlled.
> 
> ## 2. The amount inconsistencies: yes, we should insert them now
> 
> This absolutely belongs in the motion because it goes to:
> 
> - **material unreliability of Plaintiff’s accounting**
> - **meritorious defense**
> - **bad faith / carelessness in sworn filings**
> - **why the Court should not trust their numbers at face value**
> 
> And you are right: the issue is **not just** the later nunc pro tunc correction. The issue is that the numbers appear unstable **from the beginning**.
> 
> ## 3. How to frame the numbers
> 
> Do **not** say:
> > “they lied”
> 
> Say:
> > “their figures cannot be reconciled across their own filings and statements, even using generous assumptions.”
> 
> That is better.
> 
> You want to show:
> 
> - the complaint amount was already off
> - the contemporaneous mortgage statement to Defendant was materially lower
> - later filed figures changed again
> - the surplus / bid / nunc pro tunc math still did not line up cleanly
> - they continued to swear to amounts that were not internally consistent
> 
> That is serious.
> 
> ## 4. Best placement for the math issue
> 
> ### In Facts section
> Add it in **II.B** or create a stronger bridge into **II.F**.
> 
> ### In Legal Argument
> Hit it again in **III.C.2 Meritorious Defense**.
> 
> That way the judge sees:
> - first, factual unreliability
> - then, legal relevance
> 
> ## 5. Model language for the facts section
> 
> This belongs near the servicing-friction section or as a final paragraph before the nunc pro tunc section:
> 
> > Defendant further alleges that Plaintiff’s claimed indebtedness was internally inconsistent from the outset of the case. The amount stated in the foreclosure complaint did not align with contemporaneous account statements previously sent to Defendant, even before inclusion of later fees or charges allegedly allowed by the loan documents. As the matter progressed, Plaintiff’s figures continued to shift across filings, bid materials, and subsequent corrective submissions. Defendant contends that these amounts cannot be reconciled mathematically even under Plaintiff’s own methodology, or under generous rounding assumptions, and that Plaintiff nonetheless repeatedly presented those figures to the Court as reliable and sworn amounts due.
> 
> ## 6. Model language for meritorious defense section
> 
> This belongs in **III.C.2**:
> 
> > The accounting issues in this matter extend beyond a single later-described scrivener’s error. Defendant contends that Plaintiff’s figures were materially unreliable from the beginning. The complaint amount, contemporaneous account statements, later payoff-related figures, and the subsequent Nunc Pro Tunc correction do not reconcile in a mathematically coherent manner based on the record presently available. Plaintiff nevertheless proceeded with confidence in those amounts, used them to obtain judgment and structure post-judgment enforcement, and only later sought partial correction when the discrepancies became unavoidable. Those inconsistencies are sufficient, at minimum, to establish a substantial meritorious defense requiring adversarial testing on the merits.
> 
> ## 7. Strategic warning
> 
> Do **not** dump every shady payment-allocation issue into this exact paragraph right now. That will bloat it.
> 
> Use a layered structure:
> 
> - **Layer 1:** the numbers do not reconcile
> - **Layer 2:** complaint amount already appeared inflated or unstable
> - **Layer 3:** later they admitted error
> - **Layer 4:** payment reallocation / application practices help explain how the numbers became distorted
> 
> That order is cleaner.
> 
> ## 8. What I’d do next
> 
> I would add:
> 
> - one controlled paragraph in **IV.1** for the coercive-vulnerability incident
> - one new paragraph in **II.B** on the unreconcilable amount history
> - one stronger paragraph in **III.C.2** tying that directly to meritorious defense
> 
> That’s the right move.

### Assistant
> Yes. The acronym you’re reaching for is probably **CRAC / IRAC**, but for **this kind of motion**, the bigger issue is not paragraph-level structure. It is **motion architecture**.
> 
> Here is the outline I would lock in **before writing anything else**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Proposed Filing Architecture
> 
> ## 1. Caption
> - State of Indiana / Marion Superior Court
> - Cause number
> - Parties
> 
> ## 2. Title
> - **Defendant’s Motion to Vacate Default Judgment Under Indiana Trial Rule 60(B), to Quash Writ of Assistance, and for Related Relief**
> - Subtitle if you want:
>   - **With Incorporated Memorandum of Law**
> 
> ## 3. Preliminary Statement / Nature of Motion
> Purpose: tell the Court, in 2–4 paragraphs, exactly what this is and why it matters.
> 
> ### A. What relief is sought
> - Vacate default judgment
> - Quash writ of assistance
> - Set aside derivative enforcement measures to the extent they rest on the default
> - Preserve remaining personal property
> - Require any further access/retrieval to occur through structured procedures
> 
> ### B. Primary theory
> - Judgment is **void** under **Trial Rule 60(B)(6)** because personal jurisdiction was not properly established
> 
> ### C. Secondary theory
> - Even if technical service is found sufficient, notice was **constitutionally inadequate under Mullane** given Plaintiff’s prior knowledge of disability-related communication barriers and failure to disclose material context to the Court and Sheriff
> 
> ### D. Alternative theory
> - Relief under **Trial Rule 60(B)(1)** for excusable neglect, supported by a substantial meritorious defense
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Procedural Posture
> Purpose: orient the judge and avoid any appearance that you are hiding the ball.
>

### User
> **This is a strong, professional-grade pro se motion.** It is exceptionally well-organized, clearly written, and strategically structured for a complex foreclosure challenge in Marion Superior Court. The incorporation of a memorandum of law, detailed factual chronology (with table), table of authorities, proposed order, and certificate of service shows careful drafting. It reads like something prepared with significant legal research (or assistance), which courts appreciate from self-represented litigants even while holding them to the same procedural standards as attorneys.
> 
> ### Overall Strengths
> - **Persuasiveness and flow**: The Preliminary Statement immediately frames the three core grounds (void judgment under 60(B)(6), constitutional inadequacy under *Mullane*, and alternative 60(B)(1) excusable neglect + meritorious defense) without overstatement. It ties everything to equity and prejudice without sounding vindictive.
> - **Factual development**: Section III is comprehensive and chronological. The disabilities are not presented as a blanket excuse but as foreseeable barriers that Plaintiff allegedly knew about pre-suit. The accounting discrepancies (bolstered by Plaintiff's own Feb. 24, 2026 nunc pro tunc admission of a ~$10k interest error) create a credible meritorious defense. The enforcement harms (winter displacement, property disposal, collateral effects on Zai) humanize the prejudice prong without veering into irrelevance.
> - **Legal grounding**: Citations are accurate and on-point based on Indiana authority. *Barrow v. Pennington*, *LePore v. Norwest Bank*, *Swiggett Lumber*, and *Boczar* correctly address the mandatory nature of the T.R. 4.1(B) mailing requirement. *Mullane* is the right due-process hook. *Seleme v. JP Morgan Chase Bank* supports cognitive impairments as potential excusable neglect. The ADA/*Tennessee v. Lane*/*Alexander v. Choate* framing is creative and carefully limited (it informs the *Mullane* "circumstances" analysis rather than asserting a direct ADA claim against the private plaintiff).
> - **Practical elements**: The proposed order is clean and judge-friendly. The prayer for relief is specific and tiered. The motion avoids asking for the impossible (e.g., it conditions setting aside the Sheriff's Sale/Deed on vacatur of the judgment).
> 
> ### Potential Weaknesses and Risks (Key Areas for Court Pushback)
> 1. **Service-of-Process Argument (60(B)(6) – Void Judgment)**: This is the riskiest prong.  
>    Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B) requires the person making copy service (here, the Sheriff) to send a first-class mail copy **and** note that fact on the return. The Sheriff's returns **do** contain notations of mailing. Indiana courts treat a Sheriff's return as prima facie (presumptive) evidence of proper service. Non-receipt by the defendant, standing alone, is usually **not** enough to overcome that presumption absent evidence that the mailing **never occurred** (e.g., no postage records, Sheriff testimony, or USPS proof).  
>    Cases like *Barrow* and *Swiggett* invalidated service where the return showed **zero attempt** at mailing. *LePore* (and cases applying T.R. 4.15(F)) upheld service where **some** follow-up mailing attempt was made and the defendant resided at the address. Your motion correctly notes the lack of "independent corroborating proof" beyond the notation, but most Marion Superior judges will view the Sheriff's notation as sufficient unless you attach stronger rebuttal evidence (e.g., an affidavit from the Sheriff, postal records, or proof the Plaintiff never supplied the Sheriff with mailing materials).  
>    **Bottom line**: The 60(B)(6) claim is a long shot on the current record. Frame it as a fallback and lean harder on the alternative 60(B)(1) grounds.
> 
> 2. **Mullane / ADA "Knowledge of Disability" Argument**: Creative and well-framed, but courts are skeptical of using a plaintiff's pre-suit knowledge to retroactively invalidate otherwise-compliant service. *Mullane* asks whether the method was "reasonably calculated" under the circumstances known to the **serving party**. However, Indiana courts rarely go beyond the formal rules when the return is facially proper. The ADA Title II cases (*Lane*, *Alexander*) apply to **public entities** (i.e., the court itself), not the private plaintiff. Judges may view this as an attempt to impose extra due-process obligations on the creditor. It still helps show prejudice and why "bare copy service" was functionally ineffective, but expect the court to treat it as equitable context rather than an independent basis to void the judgment.
> 
> 3. **Excusable Neglect + Meritorious Defense (60(B)(1))**: This is your **strongest** path.  
>    - Impairments (PTSD, severe ADHD, executive dysfunction) can qualify as excusable neglect when they prevented comprehension/response—especially with pre-suit notice to Plaintiff.  
>    - The meritorious defense is solid: Plaintiff's own nunc pro tunc filing admits material error in the bid/interest figures, and you allege broader, irreconcilable accounting discrepancies across statements, complaint, and bid. Attach a side-by-side spreadsheet exhibit showing the math. Courts love concrete, documented defenses.  
>    - Timeliness: Judgment entered May 28, 2025. As of April 2026 you are still well within the 1-year limit for 60(B)(1). File promptly.
> 
> 4. **Prejudice / Equitable Relief Section**: Powerful but be careful with the "coercive sexual pressure" allegation. It is relevant to show the foreseeable harm of abrupt winter displacement, but without strong corroboration (the text messages you mention) it could be seen as inflammatory or collateral. Courts focus on the foreclosure itself; keep it tightly tied to prejudice from lack of notice/transition.
> 
> 5. **Exhibits and Verification**: The motion repeatedly says "(See Exs. __)" with placeholders. **This must be fixed before filing.**  
>    - File a verified affidavit (under penalty of perjury) swearing to the facts.  
>    - Attach: medical records or diagnoses (redacted if sensitive), pre-suit hardship letters/emails, account statements showing reversals/inconsistencies, the Plaintiff's Feb. 24, 2026 filing, photos of property damage, travel receipts for March 25, 2026, text messages, etc.  
>    - Without exhibits, the motion risks being denied as conclusory.
> 
> ### Minor Technical / Style Suggestions
> - **Table of Authorities**: Solid, but add pinpoint cites where helpful (e.g., *Barrow* at 479 for the "unambiguously mandatory" language). The *Allstate* and *Stidham* cases appear under-cited in the body—either integrate them or drop if not essential.
> - **Chronology Table**: Excellent. Consider adding a column for "Impact on Notice/Response" to tie facts directly to legal arguments.
> - **Tone**: Professional throughout. The disclaimers ("Defendant does not accuse the Sheriff of dishonesty") are smart and reduce defensiveness.
> - **Filing**: Use the Indiana E-Filing System (IEFS). Serve via IEFS on Plaintiff's counsel (Kainrath/Farrer) and co-defendants. The blank Certificate of Service date should be filled with the actual filing date.
> - **Proposed Order**: Good, but add language preserving the status quo pending hearing (e.g., "Plaintiff shall file any opposition within 14 days" and set a hearing date). Judges like having a ready order.
> 
> ### Realistic Assessment of Success
> - **High probability** of at least partial relief (hearing granted, judgment set aside under 60(B)(1), property preservation ordered) if you attach strong exhibits showing (1) documented impairments + pre-suit notice to Plaintiff, (2) accounting mess, and (3) actual non-receipt of mail.  
> - **Low probability** of full 60(B)(6) void judgment on service alone.  
> - Courts in Marion County (and Indiana generally) favor deciding cases on the merits, especially in residential foreclosures with disability and accounting issues. Plaintiff's own admitted error helps a lot.  
> - Worst case: Court denies vacatur but stays enforcement, orders an accounting hearing, or gives you time to respond on the merits.
> 
> This motion positions you extremely well. Clean up the exhibits/affidavit, file it promptly, and consider requesting a hearing on an emergency basis (given the ongoing property issues). If you have the actual exhibits or Sheriff's returns, a more targeted review of those could refine the service argument further. Overall, excellent work—this gives you a real shot at getting the case back on the merits.

> Tier 2: Granular Polish & Areas for Refinement
> While the text is incredibly strong, here are a few areas to tighten before you file:
> 
> Repetition of Impairment Facts: You mention the specific diagnoses (PTSD, ADHD, executive dysfunction, delayed auditory processing) several times across the Statement of Facts and the Legal Argument. While repetition can be persuasive, in legal writing, it can sometimes fatigue the reader. Consider establishing the specific diagnoses firmly in the "Pre-Suit Outreach" section, and then using a shorthand like "the documented cognitive impairments" in the later legal sections to keep the argument moving quickly.
> 
> The "Meaningful-Access" Argument (Title II): This is your most innovative argument, but also the one a state trial court judge might be least familiar with in the context of a standard foreclosure. You tie it well to Mullane, but make sure your exhibits definitively prove the Plaintiff had actual, prior knowledge of these disabilities. The strength of this entire constitutional argument rests entirely on the paper trail proving they knew about your communication barriers before they filed suit.
> 
> Exhibit Placeholders: You have several (See Ex. __) and (See Exs. –.) placeholders. Ensure every single one of these is linked to a clearly labeled, easy-to-read exhibit. A judge's eyes will immediately dart to the exhibits when you claim the Plaintiff's math doesn't reconcile. Your "comparative summary audit exhibit" needs to be visually flawless.
> 
> Formatting Checks: Ensure the final document complies with Marion County local rules regarding font size, margins, and line spacing (usually 12pt font, double-spaced for the body text, single-spaced for block quotes/headings).
