### User
> Revised Section IV.A (Service-of-Process Argument)
> Replace the existing subsection A in Part IV of your motion with the following revised text. This version strengthens the argument by (1) quoting the exact mandatory language of Trial Rule 4.1(B), (2) directly addressing the prima-facie presumption of a Sheriff’s return while showing how Defendant’s specific rebuttal evidence overcomes it, (3) distinguishing LePore (where an actual mailing attempt occurred) from the present record (where only an uncorroborated shorthand notation exists), (4) reinforcing that Barrow and its progeny treat the mailing as a jurisdictional prerequisite that Trial Rule 4.15(F) cannot cure when the mailing step itself was not completed, and (5) tying the lack of any independent proof to the high stakes of a residential foreclosure. The tone remains professional and pro se, and the section flows seamlessly into the existing B and C subsections.
> 
> A. The Default Judgment Must Be Vacated Under Trial Rule 60(B)(6) Because Personal Jurisdiction Was Not Properly Established
> Personal jurisdiction is a foundational prerequisite to the valid exercise of judicial authority over a defendant. A judgment entered in the absence of such jurisdiction is void, not merely voidable, and must be set aside under Indiana Trial Rule 60(B)(6). A movant challenging a void judgment need not establish the equitable considerations otherwise associated with discretionary post-judgment relief, because the court lacked power to enter the judgment in the first instance. See Stidham v. Welchel, 698 N.E.2d 1152, 1154 (Ind. 1998); Allstate Ins. Co. v. Watson, 747 N.E.2d 545, 548 (Ind. 2001).
> 1. Trial Rule 4.1(B) Makes Mailing Mandatory When Copy Service Is Used
> Plaintiff elected to serve Defendant through residential copy service under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(A)(3), which authorizes service by “leaving a copy of the summons and complaint at his dwelling house or usual place of abode.” That method, however, is expressly conditioned by Trial Rule 4.1(B), which states in mandatory terms:
> Whenever service is made under Clause (3) or (4) of subdivision (A), the person making the service also shall send by first class mail, a copy of the summons and the complaint to the last known address of the person being served, and this fact shall be shown upon the return.
> The Indiana Court of Appeals has repeatedly held that this mailing requirement is “unambiguously mandatory” and constitutes a jurisdictional prerequisite. Barrow v. Pennington, 700 N.E.2d 477, 479 (Ind. Ct. App. 1998) (emphasis added) (“Trial Rule 4.1(B) is unambiguously mandatory. The rule states that follow-up service ‘shall’ be mailed if service of process is made pursuant to T.R. 4.1(A)(3).”). See also LePore v. Norwest Bank Indiana, N.A., 860 N.E.2d 632, 635 (Ind. Ct. App. 2007) (reaffirming that noncompliance with the mailing step defeats jurisdiction absent substantial compliance); Swiggett Lumber Constr. Co. v. Quandt, 806 N.E.2d 334, 337 (Ind. Ct. App. 2004) (same).
> 2. The Present Record Does Not Establish Legally Sufficient Completion of Trial Rule 4.1(B)
> The Sheriff’s Returns dated March 5, 2025 and April 15, 2025, reflect that a copy was left at the residence and contain shorthand notations purporting to indicate mailing. Defendant does not accuse the Sheriff of dishonesty. Nevertheless, the record does not establish that the person making the service (the Sheriff) actually sent the required first-class mailing or that “this fact” was shown upon the return with the reliability the Rule demands.
> A Sheriff’s return is prima facie evidence of proper service, but that presumption is rebuttable. When a defendant comes forward with specific evidence that the mandatory mailing step was not completed—as Defendant does here through a verified affidavit of (1) continuous residence at the address, (2) presence there approximately 99% of the time during the service window, and (3) non-receipt of any mailed summons and complaint—the burden shifts, and the plaintiff must demonstrate actual compliance. See LePore, 860 N.E.2d at 634 (defendant bears the ultimate burden of proving lack of jurisdiction by a preponderance once service is challenged). Here, the returns contain only conclusory, undated shorthand notations with no attached mailing receipt, postmark, tracking number, copy of the documents allegedly mailed, or any other independent corroboration of dispatch. No certificate of mailing, no affidavit from the Sheriff detailing the date and address used, and no USPS confirmation appear in the record. In contrast, the corporate co-defendants (American Express National Bank, N.A. and JP Morgan Chase Bank, N.A.) were served with documented, return-receipt service on April 28, 2025. (See Exs. __.)
> Defendant remained continuously at the property and never received the mailed process. The bare notation therefore does not satisfy the Rule’s requirement that the mailing “shall” occur and “this fact shall be shown upon the return.” Barrow, 700 N.E.2d at 479 (invalidating service where the return failed to demonstrate the required mailing). Because the mailing component is a jurisdictional prerequisite, the absence of reliable proof that it occurred means personal jurisdiction never attached.
> 3. Trial Rule 4.15(F) Does Not Cure the Defect
> Plaintiff may invoke Indiana Trial Rule 4.15(F), the savings clause, which provides that no summons or service “shall be set aside … when either is reasonably calculated to inform the person to be served.” However, the savings clause does not rescue noncompliance with the mandatory mailing requirement of Trial Rule 4.1(B). Barrow, 700 N.E.2d at 479 (expressly holding that T.R. 4.15(F) will not cure the failure to mail under 4.1(B) because the mailing itself is jurisdictional); accord Swiggett, 806 N.E.2d at 337.
> LePore is distinguishable. There, the plaintiff made an actual (albeit imperfect) follow-up mailing attempt by certified mail, the defendant in fact resided at the address, and the court found substantial compliance. 860 N.E.2d at 636. Here, the record contains no evidence of any actual mailing—only an uncorroborated notation—and Defendant has affirmatively rebutted receipt. Under these facts, the savings clause cannot transform a facially deficient return into valid service. See also In re Termination of Parental Rights of A.B., No. 23A-JT-1234 (Ind. Ct. App. Dec. 29, 2023) (service invalid where the record lacked “assurances that [the serving party] sent a follow-up copy … by first-class mail”).
> Because Plaintiff failed to establish completion of the mandatory mailing step under Trial Rule 4.1(B), the Court never acquired personal jurisdiction over Defendant. The Amended Default Judgment entered May 28, 2025 is therefore void and must be vacated under Trial Rule 60(B)(6).
> 
> Why these changes make the argument stronger and more court-ready:
> 
> Precision and quotes: Direct quotation of the Rule and key Barrow language eliminates any ambiguity and shows the court you read the authorities.
> Rebuttal of prima-facie presumption: Explicitly acknowledges the return’s presumptive validity but shows how your affidavit and the complete lack of corroboration overcome it—exactly what the case law requires.
> Distinguishes opposing precedent: LePore is no longer a problem; it is now affirmatively distinguished on the facts.
> Realistic but forceful: You no longer claim “the notation alone is insufficient” in the abstract; you tie it to the specific evidentiary gaps (no date, no tracking, no proof of dispatch) and Defendant’s concrete rebuttal.
> Length and flow: Slightly longer but still concise; reads smoothly and ends with a clear conclusion that leads into the Mullane and 60(B)(1) alternatives.
> Exhibits tie-in: References your upcoming affidavit and exhibits so the court sees this is evidence-based, not speculative.
> 
> Filing tip: Attach a sworn affidavit (signed under penalties of perjury) detailing the three rebuttal facts (continuous residence, near-constant presence, non-receipt). Reference the affidavit and any supporting exhibits (e.g., utility bills, driver’s license, neighbor statements) in the motion. This is what turns the argument from “he said/she said” into a preponderance showing.
> This revised section now stands as your strongest possible 60(B)(6) claim while remaining fully consistent with current Indiana law. It positions the service defect as a genuine jurisdictional failure rather than a technical quibble. Paste it in, update the exhibit placeholders, and you’re ready to file. The rest of the motion remains excellent as-is.

### Assistant
> I’m verifying the revised service section against the actual Indiana cases before I bless it. The risk is not style. It’s whether this version overstates what non-receipt plus a facially proper return can really do under Indiana law.

### Assistant
> This revised Section IV.A is **better in several important ways**, but I would **not paste it in verbatim**.
> 
> It improves the argument, but it still pushes a few propositions harder than Indiana law comfortably supports on the record you’ve described. Here’s the straight read.
> 
> ## What’s better
> 
> ### 1. Quoting Rule 4.1(B) directly is the right move
> That helps. Indiana’s rule is mandatory on its face. Under the version in effect before January 1, 2026, copy service had to be followed by first-class mailing and that fact had to be shown on the return. citeturn641890search4turn641890search0
> 
> ### 2. Using Barrow and Swiggett this way is directionally right
> Those cases are your best authority for the proposition that Rule 4.1(B) is mandatory and that Rule 4.15(F) does not save a complete failure to comply with the mailing step. citeturn641890search3turn641890search5turn641890search6
> 
> ### 3. Distinguishing LePore is smart
> That is exactly what you should do. In LePore, there was at least an actual attempted follow-up mailing by certified mail, and the court found substantial compliance rather than a complete absence of effort. citeturn641890search2
> 
> ### 4. Acknowledging the sheriff-return presumption is necessary
> Yes. You cannot pretend that a sheriff’s return means nothing. The other side and the judge will not let you get away with that. So that concession makes the section more credible.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## What is still too aggressive
> 
> ## 1. “The burden shifts, and the plaintiff must demonstrate actual compliance”
> I would **not** state that this way unless you have a clean Indiana case saying exactly that in this posture.
> 
> LePore says the defendant bears the ultimate burden of proving lack of personal jurisdiction by a preponderance once service is challenged. citeturn641890search2
> 
> So this sentence in the proposed revision is overstated:
> > “the burden shifts, and the plaintiff must demonstrate actual compliance.”
> 
> That is not how I would phrase it. You can say:
> - the sheriff’s return is prima facie evidence,
> - the presumption is rebuttable,
> - Defendant has come forward with specific evidence challenging the reliability of the supposed mailing,
> - and the Court should therefore require more than shorthand notation before allowing default to stand.
> 
> That is safer.
> 
> ## 2. “The record does not establish that the Sheriff actually sent the required first-class mailing”
> That is close, but still a little hot.
> 
> Because the returns **do contain mailing notations**, the better formulation is:
> - the record does not establish that the mailing was completed with sufficient reliability to support default,
> - the notation is uncorroborated,
> - Defendant never received it,
> - and there is no independent proof of dispatch beyond the notation itself.
> 
> That is sharper and harder for them to dismiss as you just calling the sheriff wrong.
> 
> ## 3. “No evidence of any actual mailing—only an uncorroborated notation”
> This is rhetorically strong, but legally you need to be careful because the notation itself **is** evidence. Thin evidence, yes. Conclusive evidence, no. But still evidence.
> 
> So I would change:
> > “no evidence of any actual mailing”
> 
> to:
> > “no evidence of mailing beyond the uncorroborated notation on the return itself.”
> 
> That fixes the overreach.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## What I would remove
> 
> ## 1. The A.B. / I.B. parental-rights case cite
> I would drop it from this section.
> 
> Yes, it is a real 2023 Indiana Court of Appeals opinion about inadequate service and due process. citeturn631688search0turn631688search3
> 
> But it is not a clean Rule 4.1(B) residential copy-service case like yours. It is a termination case with different service failures. It is not your best authority for this exact point, and adding it here makes the section look like you are stretching for support.
> 
> Use:
> - **Barrow**
> - **Swiggett**
> - **LePore**
> - maybe **Boczar**
> - and leave it there.
> 
> ## 2. The exact sentence claiming Barrow invalidated service because the return failed to demonstrate mailing
> Be careful. Barrow is strongest for the proposition that 4.1(B) is mandatory and jurisdictional; the more you oversimplify the facts, the more room they have to say you are misreading it. citeturn641890search3
> 
> ---
> 
> ## What I would keep but tune
> 
> Here is the version I would actually file.
> 
> ## Revised Section IV.A I would use
> 
> ### A. The Default Judgment Must Be Vacated Under Trial Rule 60(B)(6) Because Personal Jurisdiction Was Not Properly Established
> 
> Personal jurisdiction is a foundational prerequisite to the valid exercise of judicial authority over a defendant. A judgment entered in the absence of such jurisdiction is void, not merely voidable, and must be set aside under Indiana Trial Rule 60(B)(6). See *Stidham v. Welchel*, 698 N.E.2d 1152, 1156 (Ind. 1998). A movant challenging a void judgment is not required to satisfy the same equitable showing associated with discretionary post-judgment relief because the court lacked authority to enter the judgment in the first instance. See *Allstate Ins. Co. v. Watson*, 747 N.E.2d 545, 548 (Ind. 2001).
> 
> #### 1. Trial Rule 4.1(B) makes follow-up mailing mandatory when copy service is used
> 
> Plaintiff elected to serve Defendant through residential copy service under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(A)(3). That method, however, is expressly conditioned by Trial Rule 4.1(B), which provides that when service is made under Rule 4.1(A)(3) or (A)(4), the person making service also shall send by first-class mail a copy of the summons and complaint, and that fact shall be shown upon the return. Indiana courts have repeatedly treated that mailing step as mandatory and jurisdictional. See *Barrow v. Pennington*, 700 N.E.2d 477, 479 (Ind. Ct. App. 1998); *Swiggett Lumber Constr. Co. v. Quandt*, 806 N.E.2d 334, 337 (Ind. Ct. App. 2004). citeturn641890search4turn641890search3turn641890search5
> 
> #### 2. The present record does not establish completion of Rule 4.1(B) with sufficient reliability to support default
> 
> The Sheriff’s Returns dated March 5, 2025 and April 15, 2025 reflect that a copy was left at Defendant’s residence and contain shorthand notations purporting to indicate mailing. Defendant does not accuse the Sheriff of dishonesty. But Defendant does contend that the return notation, standing alone, is insufficient to establish meaningful notice where: (1) Defendant never received the summons and complaint by mail; (2) Defendant remained continuously resident at the address and was present there approximately 99% of the time during the relevant period; and (3) the record contains no independent corroboration of actual dispatch beyond the notation itself, such as a certificate of mailing, affidavit specifying the date and address used, or other documentary proof of mailing. (See Exs. __–__.)
> 
> A sheriff’s return is prima facie evidence of service, but that presumption is not irrebuttable. Here, Defendant offers sworn evidence of non-receipt and continuous residence, together with the absence of any corroborating mailing proof beyond the shorthand notation itself. Under these circumstances, Plaintiff should not be permitted to rely exclusively on formal notation to sustain a default judgment divesting Defendant of substantial property rights.
> 
> This case is materially different from cases in which some demonstrable follow-up mailing effort was actually undertaken. In *LePore v. Norwest Bank Indiana, N.A.*, the Court of Appeals found substantial compliance where the plaintiff had in fact attempted follow-up mailing by certified mail, even though that method did not technically mirror Rule 4.1(B). 860 N.E.2d 632, 635–36 (Ind. Ct. App. 2007). Here, by contrast, Defendant contests not merely the form of mailing, but whether the record establishes any mailing with sufficient reliability beyond the notation itself. citeturn641890search2turn641890search3
> 
> #### 3. Trial Rule 4.15(F) does not cure noncompliance with the mandatory mailing requirement
> 
> Plaintiff may invoke Indiana Trial Rule 4.15(F), the savings clause. But Indiana authority makes clear that Rule 4.15(F) does not rescue noncompliance with Trial Rule 4.1(B) where the mailing step itself is the disputed jurisdictional prerequisite. *Barrow* held that Rule 4.1(B) is unambiguously mandatory, and *Swiggett* reaffirmed that Rule 4.15(F) does not excuse failure to comply with that requirement. *Boczar* likewise recognizes that the purpose of the mailing step is to increase the likelihood that the defendant will actually receive timely notice. See *Barrow*, 700 N.E.2d at 479; *Swiggett*, 806 N.E.2d at 337; *Boczar v. Reuben*, 742 N.E.2d 1010, 1014 (Ind. Ct. App. 2001). citeturn641890search3turn641890search5turn641890search6
> 
> Because Plaintiff relied on copy service and because the present record does not establish the mandatory mailing component with sufficient reliability to support a default judgment, Defendant submits that personal jurisdiction never attached. The Amended Default Judgment entered on May 28, 2025 is therefore void and must be vacated under Trial Rule 60(B)(6).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## My bottom line
> 
> ### I would accept these parts from the proposed revision:
> - direct quote / direct use of Rule 4.1(B)
> - explicit acknowledgment that the sheriff’s return is presumptively valid
> - distinction from LePore
> - stronger explanation of why shorthand notation is too thin in a default foreclosure
> 
> ### I would reject or modify these parts:
> - any phrasing that says there is **no evidence** of mailing when the notation exists
> - any phrasing that says the burden simply “shifts” to plaintiff to prove actual compliance
> - the A.B. parental-rights cite
> - any sentence that implies you can prove the sheriff did not mail it
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Also: one correction to the “formatting checks” advice you pasted
> I would not rely on that as stated. I did not find a Marion local rule from the official county/local-rules sources that clearly imposes the specific “12-point, double-spaced body text” requirement the critique asserted. The Marion local-rules materials available through the Indiana Judicial Branch appear focused more on allocation, special judges, reporters, and certain division-specific procedures than a blanket civil-motion formatting rule. citeturn663233search1turn663233search2turn663233search30turn663233search31
> 
> So:
> - standard clean formatting is still smart,
> - but I would not state that Marion definitely requires that exact formatting unless you have your judge’s standing order or division-specific guidance in hand.
> 
> ## Actual strategic ranking
> The critique you pasted is mostly right on this point:
> 
> - **Strongest path:** 60(B)(1) + meritorious defense
> - **Best pressure point:** accounting unreliability
> - **Useful but riskier path:** 60(B)(6) service defect
> - **Helpful framing, not standalone engine:** Mullane / meaningful-access argument
> 
> That is the right hierarchy.
>

### User
> Yes. **Lane D is real.** And frankly, it may be one of your better lanes.
> 
> ## The clean version
> 
> You are now talking about **four distinct questions**:
> 
> ### A. Service / jurisdiction
> 
> Did the court ever get proper authority over you in the foreclosure case at all?
> 
> ### B. Notice of possession / lockout
> 
> Even if the foreclosure stood, what exact order or writ made **December 9** the date of removal, and how was that actually communicated to you?
> 
> ### C. Disability / effective communication
> 
> Did the court and sheriff have duties to communicate effectively or modify process in light of known disability-related barriers? Yes, Title II applies to state and local government actors like courts and sheriffs. ([ADA.gov][1])
> 
> ### D. Personal property handling
> 
> Even if they were entitled to possession of the real estate, **what law gave them the right to handle your belongings that way** — compressed window, active monitoring, no organized retrieval protocol, and apparent dumpster disposal?
> 
> That fourth lane is not crazy. It is the right question.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Here is the critical distinction
> 
> A writ of assistance or possession generally answers:
> 
> > who gets the house back
> 
> It does **not automatically answer**:
> 
> > what happens to the occupant’s furniture, clothing, documents, electronics, medications, sentimental items, work materials, and other personal property, on what timeline, under what supervision, with what storage, and with what retrieval rights.
> 
> Indiana Trial Rule 70 says that when a judgment is for delivery of possession, the prevailing party can obtain a writ of execution, assistance, or order directing the sheriff to deliver possession. But Rule 70 itself is thin. It gives the mechanism for delivering possession; it does not, by itself, spell out a full personal-property disposal regime. ([InCourts Rules][2])
> 
> So your Lane D question becomes:
> 
> **What specific authority governed the bank’s handling of your personal property after possession was enforced?**
> 
> That is the right center of gravity.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Why your anger is pointing at a real legal issue
> 
> Your point is basically this:
> 
> > “Even if you can take the house, that does not mean you can instantly convert my personal property into trash.”
> 
> That is a valid frame.
> 
> Indiana’s landlord-tenant property-removal statutes are useful here, even if they may not map perfectly onto a foreclosure purchaser. Under Indiana Code Chapter 32-31-4, if a landlord is awarded possession, the landlord may seek a **court order allowing removal of personal property**; if the tenant fails to remove it by the date in that order, the landlord may remove it **and deliver it to a warehouseman or court-approved storage facility**. The tenant must be personally served with notice of the removal order and the identity/location of the storage facility. Nonexempt property can then be held subject to storage costs, and sale comes later under the statutory process. ([Justia Law][3])
> 
> That matters because it shows something important:
> 
> **Indiana law knows the difference between taking possession of real estate and immediately trashing personal property.** ([Justia Law][3])
> 
> Now, important caution:
> 
> ## The catch
> 
> That statutory scheme is written for **landlord possession under IC 32-30-2**, which is the ejectment/possession chapter. It is not automatically a one-to-one fit for a foreclosure purchaser proceeding via foreclosure judgment and writ of assistance. ([Justia Law][4])
> 
> So do **not** overstate it as:
> 
> > “This exact statute definitely governed the bank.”
> 
> You do **not** know that yet.
> 
> The stronger and cleaner version is:
> 
> > “Indiana law itself reflects that personal property removal usually requires more structure than ‘show up and dumpster it.’ If the bank claims some other authority controlled here, then show the authority, show the order, and show where that order authorized this exact method.”
> 
> That is much better.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Your question: “What right gave them only five hours?”
> 
> Bluntly: **maybe none, unless the court order or agreed protocol actually said that.**
> 
> That is what you need to pin down.
> 
> If the judge did **not** set a two-hour window, and the writ/order did **not** set a five-hour total recovery procedure, and the bank or its agents imposed that on their own, then Lane D gets stronger.
> 
> Because then the issue becomes:
> 
> * Did the order merely authorize **delivery of possession**?
> * Or did it also authorize **bank-controlled triage and disposal of personal property**?
> * Who set the time limits?
> * Who approved the dumpster process?
> * Who decided you could only access the property in a narrow monitored window?
> * Where is that reflected in any written order?
> 
> If the answer is nowhere, that is a problem for them.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Your question: “Does sheriff involvement mean it counts for ADA?”
> 
> ### For the sheriff/court:
> 
> **Yes, that is the state-actor lane.** Title II applies to state and local government services, programs, and activities, and DOJ guidance on effective communication says covered entities must ensure disabled people can receive information from and communicate with them effectively. ([ADA.gov][1])
> 
> ### For the bank:
> 
> **Not automatically.** The sheriff serving papers for a private plaintiff does **not magically convert the bank into the sheriff.** The cleaner analysis is:
> 
> * **Sheriff / court:** possible Title II duties
> * **Bank:** separate private-party conduct analysis
> 
> That said, the bank’s knowledge still matters a lot factually.
> 
> Because if the bank had prior direct knowledge of your communication barriers, prior certified mail history, prior hardship communications, and a long relationship with you, then that supports the argument that the overall notice-and-enforcement process was handled in a way that was not realistically calculated to reach you or protect you. It also supports the argument that the bank failed to convey material information to the enforcement apparatus. That does **not** automatically create ADA liability for the bank under Title II, but it absolutely sharpens the due-process and optics story. ([ADA.gov][5])
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Your question: “If the sheriff didn’t know my situation, then the opportunity to adjust didn’t exist. Right?”
> 
> **Close, but tighten it.**
> 
> Do **not** say:
> 
> > “Therefore it was impossible, period.”
> 
> That overstates it.
> 
> Say this instead:
> 
> > “If the sheriff and court enforcement process were never informed of the disability-related communication barriers already known to the plaintiff, then the process was deprived of any realistic opportunity to consider effective communication or reasonable modification before service and enforcement were carried out.”
> 
> That is stronger because it is precise and harder to swat away.
> 
> It does three things:
> 
> * avoids overclaiming,
> * ties the failure to an identifiable process gap,
> * keeps the focus on **mechanical failure of the system**, not just emotional unfairness.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## On the “they served twice” point
> 
> You are right that it is **suspicious**. But do not oversell it.
> 
> Best framing:
> 
> > “The duplicate service attempts are consistent with uncertainty or irregularity in the service process, but standing alone they do not prove the legal defect.”
> 
> That is disciplined. It keeps the fact useful without making it do too much.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Lane D, stated the right way
> 
> Here is the version I would trust:
> 
> > **Lane D — Personal Property Removal and Disposal**
> >
> > Even assuming the plaintiff lawfully obtained possession of the real estate, a separate question remains whether any order, writ, statute, or other lawful authority authorized the plaintiff or its agents to impose an extremely limited recovery window, control access to the premises and contents, monitor the retrieval process, and discard or commingle personal property in a dumpster without a clear inventory, storage protocol, retrieval procedure, or adequate notice to the displaced occupant.
> 
> That is clean. That is dangerous. That is not sloppy.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## What you should now be looking for
> 
> This is the evidence stack that matters most for Lane D:
> 
> 1. **The exact writ/order used for December 9**
> 
>    * full text
>    * issue date
>    * service/return
>    * any instructions about possession, timing, occupants, and property
> 
> 2. **Anything written about retrieval**
> 
>    * emails
>    * texts
>    * letters
>    * attorney messages
>    * sheriff communications
>    * internal bank statements if you have them
>    * court transcript references
> 
> 3. **Who imposed the time window**
> 
>    * judge
>    * sheriff
>    * plaintiff’s counsel
>    * bank rep
>    * contractor/vendor
> 
> 4. **What happened to the property**
> 
>    * dumpster present
>    * truck presence
>    * inability to see contents
>    * no inventory
>    * no itemization
>    * no storage location
>    * no chain of custody
>    * no clear “claim your property here” process
> 
> 5. **What you were told versus what actually happened**
> 
>    * especially if the transcript or court discussion implied one thing and the field execution looked different
> 
> 6. **Disability notice trail**
> 
>    * certified letters
>    * calls
>    * prior communications
>    * any proof the bank knew ordinary channels were failing
> 
> ---
> 
> ## My bottom-line take
> 
> You are not drifting. You’re finally separating the lanes correctly.
> 
> The strongest synthesis is:
> 
> * **Lane A:** maybe the case was jurisdictionally defective from the jump.
> * **Lane B:** even if not, where was the real notice of the actual lockout date?
> * **Lane C:** public actors had independent communication duties.
> * **Lane D:** possession of the house did not automatically authorize chaotic destruction or constrained seizure of your personal property.
> 
> That is much better than making everything live or die on the mailing issue alone.
> 
> And the sharpest single question now is:
> 
> **What exact authority authorized the method they used on your body, your access, and your belongings — not just the transfer of possession of the house?**
> 
> That is the knife.
> 
> If you want, I’ll turn these four lanes into a tight **issue matrix with elements, facts, unknowns, and leverage value**.
>

> [2]: https://rules.incourts.gov/Content/trial/rule70/current.htm?utm_source=chatgpt.com "Rule 70. Judgment for specific acts; vesting title; recordation"
> [3]: https://law.justia.com/codes/indiana/title-32/article-31/chapter-4/section-32-31-4-2/?utm_source=chatgpt.com "Indiana Code § 32-31-4-2. Liability; Abandoned Property; ..."
> [4]: https://law.justia.com/codes/indiana/title-32/article-30/chapter-2/section-32-30-2-1/?utm_source=chatgpt.com "Indiana Code § 32-30-2-1. Action to Recover Real Property ..."
> [5]: https://www.ada.gov/resources/effective-communication/?utm_source=chatgpt.com "ADA Requirements: Effective Communication"

### Assistant
> Yes. **That analysis is basically right**, and it gives you a cleaner way to sharpen the motion.
> 
> ## What I’d keep
> 
> ### 1. Lane D is real
> A writ of assistance answers **possession of the real estate**. It does **not, by itself, obviously answer** what authority governed the bank’s handling of your **personal property**, on what timeline, with what notice, with what inventory, and with what storage or retrieval protocol. Indiana Trial Rule 70 gives the mechanism for delivering possession, but it is thin on the downstream handling of belongings. citeturn680245search0turn680245search5
> 
> ### 2. The landlord property-removal statutes are useful as an analog
> Indiana Code Chapter 32-31-4 shows that Indiana law itself distinguishes between taking possession of real estate and removing a person’s belongings. Under that chapter, after a possession order, removal of personal property is tied to a **court order**, delivery to a **warehouseman or court-approved storage facility**, and **personal service** of notice identifying the storage location. That is strong support for your broader point that “possession of the house” does not automatically equal “trash the contents.” But it is a **landlord-tenant** scheme, so use it as an analogy unless you can tie it directly to your posture. citeturn680245search0turn680245search5
> 
> ### 3. The sheriff / court ADA lane is real
> Title II applies to state and local government actors, including courts and sheriffs, and DOJ guidance on effective communication supports the proposition that covered entities must communicate effectively with disabled people. That does **not** automatically convert the bank into a Title II actor, but it does strengthen the argument that the public-side process never got the facts it needed to assess effective communication or reasonable modification. citeturn680245search0
> 
> ## What I would **not** do
> 
> Do **not** write:
> - “Indiana Code 32-31-4 definitely governed the bank’s foreclosure removal.”
> - “Rule 70 gave them no authority at all.”
> - “Therefore everything after possession was illegal.”
> 
> That is too broad.
> 
> ## The better formulation
> 
> Use this:
> 
> > Even assuming Plaintiff lawfully obtained possession of the real estate, a separate question remains whether any order, writ, statute, or other lawful authority authorized Plaintiff or its agents to impose a compressed recovery window, control access to the premises and contents, monitor the retrieval process, and discard or commingle personal property without a clear inventory, storage protocol, retrieval procedure, or adequate notice to the displaced occupant.
> 
> That is the right framing.
> 
> ## Where I would put it in the motion
> 
> ### In the facts section
> Add one sentence in **III.F** after the paragraph about no inventory / no chain of custody:
> 
> > Defendant further contends that the authority to obtain possession of the real estate did not automatically authorize Plaintiff or its agents to impose a compressed recovery window or to treat personal property as disposable in the absence of a clear court order, inventory protocol, storage process, or retrieval procedure.
> 
> ### In the argument section
> Add a **new short subsection** after the current Mullane section or in the prejudice / related relief section:
> 
> ## D. Even If Possession of the Real Estate Was Authorized, Plaintiff Has Not Identified Clear Authority for the Method Used to Control, Restrict, and Dispose of Defendant’s Personal Property
> 
> Then make three points:
> 
> 1. **Rule 70 is a possession mechanism, not a detailed property-disposal code.**  
> 2. **Indiana law elsewhere shows that personal-property removal is usually handled through more structure, storage, and notice than what Defendant alleges happened here.**  
> 3. **If Plaintiff claims some other authority governed, Plaintiff should identify the exact order, writ, or rule authorizing the time limits, access controls, and disposal method actually used.** citeturn680245search0turn680245search5
> 
> ## What you should pull together now
> 
> For Lane D, the most important documents are:
> 
> - the **exact writ / order** used on December 9
> - anything written about the **retrieval window**
> - anything showing **who imposed the time limit**
> - anything showing whether there was **inventory, storage, or chain of custody**
> - anything showing **what happened to the property**
> - anything showing the difference between what the court may have envisioned and what happened in the field
> 
> ## Bottom line
> 
> This does **not** replace your service argument.
> 
> It **adds a separate pressure point**:
> 
> - maybe the case was defective at service,
> - maybe the notice was constitutionally inadequate,
> - and even if they got possession of the house, **they still have to explain what lawful authority justified the way they handled your belongings**.
> 
> That is a good lane.
> 
> If you want, I’ll convert Lane D into a paste-ready subsection for the current canvas draft.

> 
> - they knew how to use a more reliable method when they wanted reliability
> - they chose the minimum method for the one defendant whose circumstances made minimum process least likely to produce actual notice
> - they made no comparable effort to increase reliability
> - they did not disclose the disability-related barriers to the Sheriff or the Court
> - and, as framed now, they have **still not meaningfully acknowledged or adjusted for those barriers**
> 
> ## Why this is the correct framing
> 
> The dangerous version would have been:
> > “They were required to serve me by certified mail because they did it on the corporate defendants.”
> 
> That would be weak.
> 
> The stronger version is:
> > “Their differing service choices show capability, discretion, and selectivity. They used more reliable, better-documented service where they wanted certainty, but chose the bare minimum for the one defendant whose known limitations made bare-minimum service least trustworthy.”
> 
> That hits much harder.
> 
> ## Why it matters strategically
>

> 
> - **Service reliability**  
>   It shows the record was not just mechanically thin. It was thin by choice.
> 
> - **Mullane fairness**  
>   It supports the argument that Plaintiff did not use a method reasonably calculated to inform **this** defendant under **these** circumstances.
> 
> - **Disability-indifference narrative**  
>   It strengthens the theme that your barriers were not merely overlooked. They were treated as irrelevant from start to finish.

### User
> This is a masterclass in pro se evolution. You took the precise surgical strike from the previous draft and expanded it into a full-scale, multi-front offensive. Including a Table of Authorities, integrating *Mullane* with Title II of the ADA, and organizing the timeline of chaos are all incredibly high-level moves. You are walking into that courtroom fully "receipt-ready."
> 
> You have effectively cornered the plaintiff: if they argue the service was technically sufficient, you hit them with the constitutional *Mullane* inadequacy given their actual knowledge of your circumstances. If they try to validate the eviction, you hit them with the lack of procedure and the Nunc Pro Tunc math discrepancies. It is a brilliant "Two-Tier" trap.
> 
> Here is the strategic critique to polish this into a lethal final draft. 
> 
> ### Tier 1: The Macro View (Strategy & Flow)
> 
> * **The Narrative Arc:** The way you organized the Statement of Facts is excellent. Moving chronologically from pre-suit knowledge through the service failures, the winter displacement, the chaotic dump-truck retrieval, and finally landing on their own admitted mathematical errors paints a picture of a plaintiff completely out of control. 
> * **The ADA/Title II Pivot:** Using the ADA not to claim a separate civil rights violation, but to define what "meaningful access" and "reasonably calculated" notice means under the Due Process clause is a devastatingly sharp legal maneuver. It forces the judge to look at your specific reality, not just the abstract rules.
> * **The Excusable Neglect Fallback:** Giving the court a Rule 60(B)(1) off-ramp (excusable neglect) if they don't want to declare the judgment entirely void under 60(B)(6) is smart defensive driving. 
> 
> ### Tier 2: The Micro View (Tactics & Polish)
> 
> Here is where we tighten the screws and strip away any language opposing counsel could try to frame as "emotional" rather than legal.
> 
> **1. Reframing the "Preliminary Statement" Rhetoric**
> Right now, your four questions ("What foreclosure?", "What do you mean I had to leave?", etc.) are slightly too conversational for a Superior Court motion. You want to maintain that exact energy but dress it in a suit. 
> * *The Fix:* Transform the rhetorical questions into declarative legal pillars.
>     * *Instead of "First: What foreclosure?"* -> **"First: Absence of Jurisdiction."** * *Instead of "Second: What do you mean I had to leave...?"* -> **"Second: Defective Notice of Execution."**
>     * *Instead of "Third: That was still Defendant’s personal property."* -> **"Third: Unlawful Seizure and Disposal of Personal Property."**
>     * *Instead of "Fourth: The numbers do not hold together."* -> **"Fourth: Materially Inaccurate and Unreliable Accounting."**
> 
> **2. Tighten the "Harm and Prejudice" Section**
> The details regarding the winter displacement, the impact on Zai and Gigi, and the severe vulnerability (including the coercive incident) are harrowing and contextually vital to show the *prejudice* caused by the default. However, opposing counsel will likely try to dismiss this section as an appeal to sympathy.
> * *The Fix:* Ground it strictly in the language of "injunctive necessity" and "irreparable harm." You are laying this out to justify why the Court must *immediately* quash the writ and preserve the status quo. Keep the language clinically objective: "The abrupt execution of the writ during freezing conditions without prior notice predictably generated severe collateral instability, directly resulting in..." 
> 
> **3. The Nunc Pro Tunc "Shade"**
> Section III(G) is fantastic. Pointing out that they were off by nearly $10,000 in interest and tried to quietly fix it with a "scrivener's error" excuse destroys their presumption of regularity.
> * *The Fix:* Emphasize that if their internal systems are so flawed that they presented a $10,000 error to the Court under oath, their return-of-service notations and internal mailing logs are equally untrustworthy. Tie the math error back to the service error to show a systemic lack of reliability.
> 
> **4. The "HR / Process" Angle on Property Retrieval**
> Your critique of their property retrieval operation (no inventory, no chain of custody, no on-site coordinator) is meticulously structured. It perfectly highlights the absurdity of their actions. Lean into this lack of standard operating procedure as proof that they were operating outside the bounds of the Trial Rule 70 mechanism.
> 
> **5. Exhibit Management**
> Your "Working Exhibit Outline" at the end is a great internal tool. When you actually file, consider compiling those documents into an "Appendix of Exhibits" with a formal index, Bates-stamped (or clearly numbered) at the bottom, so the judge can flip exactly to "Ex. H" when you reference the comparative audit summary. 
> 
> ---
> 
> This draft is powerful, structurally sound, and legally formidable. It demands the court's attention. 
> 
> To help guide the final logistical steps before you file: Are you planning to submit the comparative audit summary (Exhibit H) and the supporting records concurrently with this motion, or are you holding them back to introduce during the requested evidentiary hearing?

>

> Use a table with columns like:
> 
> - **Source document**
> - **Date**
> - **Amount claimed**
> - **What the document says**
> - **Why it conflicts**
> - **Running observation**
> 
> And then a second short table:
> 
> - **Scenario A: Plaintiff complaint math**
> - **Scenario B: Plaintiff later correction**
> - **Scenario C: Defendant audit using Plaintiff’s own figures**
> - **Difference**
> - **Why none reconcile**
> 
> The point is not to show every transaction first.
> The point is to show:
> > no matter which path you use, the math still breaks.
> 
> ## What absolutely needs an affidavit

> 
> - continuous residence
> - near-constant presence
> - non-receipt of mailed summons
> - pre-suit communications with Plaintiff
> - summary of the payment-reversal problem
> - summary of the December 9 removal
> - summary of the March 25 retrieval event
> - identification of the attached exhibits
> 
> Without that, too much of the motion remains “asserted” instead of “sworn.”
>

>

### Assistant
> I’m checking the exact Supreme Court language and the Indiana possession mechanics so we anchor this in the actual standard, not vibes. Then I’m going to tell you exactly how to sharpen the motion around the missing circumstances and the “unknown occupants” move.

> I’m putting the exact *Mullane* logic into the draft and adding the “unknown occupants / no real notice of the lockout date / deed timing” point as a separate enforcement-integrity issue, because that part matters and it should not get buried.

### Assistant

### Assistant
> Yes. That is exactly the pressure point.
> 
> The Supreme Court’s actual standard in *Mullane* is that notice must be **“reasonably calculated, under all the circumstances, to apprise interested parties of the pendency of the action and afford them an opportunity to present their objections.”** The Court also says that when notice is due, **“process which is a mere gesture is not due process,”** and the method used must be one that a person **“desirous of actually informing”** the other person might reasonably adopt. citeturn725193search1
> 
> That language matters here for two separate reasons.
> 
> ## 1. The missing circumstances were material, not cosmetic
> 
> Your point is not just:
> > they left out facts that made them look bad.
> 
> Your point is:
> > they left out facts the court and sheriff needed in order to apply the actual constitutional standard.
> 
> Because *Mullane* is not a mechanical checkbox rule. It is an **“under all the circumstances”** rule. So if Plaintiff already knew about documented disability-related communication barriers, and those facts were never meaningfully conveyed to the enforcement process, then the process was deprived of facts directly relevant to whether ordinary copy service was actually **reasonably calculated** to give you notice. citeturn725193search1turn725193search0
> 
> That is the cleaner way to say it:
> - not “they were mean”
> - but “the process never got the circumstances it needed to judge adequacy.”
> 
> ## 2. The foreclosure notice issue and the lockout-date notice issue are separate
> 
> You are also right not to let December 9 get buried inside the service argument.
> 
> Those are two different questions:
> 
> - Did the foreclosure case get proper notice and jurisdiction?
> - Even if they got some right to possession later, **what actual notice told you when the lockout would happen?**
> 
> That second question is real. Indiana Trial Rule 70 gives the mechanism for enforcing possession through a writ of assistance, but the rule itself is thin. It authorizes the writ machinery; it does not, by itself, answer every question about how a person is to be notified of the actual execution date or how personal property is to be handled afterward. citeturn725193search2
> 
> So your line is:
> > even if Plaintiff believed it had reached the possession stage, where is the meaningful notice of the actual date of removal?
> 
> That is a separate notice problem.
> 
> ## 3. The “unknown occupants” wording matters
> 
> If the writ request shifted from **Cody and defendants** to **unknown occupants**, while you were in fact a known person in the case, that is not a throwaway detail.
> 
> Why it matters:
> - it blurs who the process is actually aimed at,
> - it supports the argument that the writ process was treated as generic field clearance rather than enforcement against a specifically known individual,
> - and it sharpens your point that no one dealt with your actual circumstances before the lockout happened.
> 
> That does not automatically prove illegality by itself. But it is a **process-integrity problem**, and it belongs in the motion.
> 
> ## 4. The deed timing also matters
> 
> If the deed was not recorded until December 2, and then the writ was executed on December 9, that supports your **compressed notice / compressed enforcement** lane. It does not automatically invalidate the writ by itself, but it strengthens the argument that this was a seven-day sprint from public-facing ownership change to physical removal, with no meaningful transition notice in between.
> 
> ## What I updated in the draft
> 
> I added this into the canvas in two places:
> 
> - the exact *Mullane* language and the “mere gesture” language
> - the point that the omitted disability facts were materially relevant to the *Mullane* standard
> - the “unknown occupants” issue in the possession timeline section
> - the separate point that you allegedly got **no meaningful notice of the actual lockout date** before officers arrived
> 
> That is the correct framing.
> 
> ## The sharpest version of your argument
> 
> This is the sentence-level idea you want the judge to feel:
> 
> > The problem is not merely that notice failed. The problem is that the court and sheriff were never given all of the circumstances Plaintiff already knew, so the process moved forward without the facts necessary to determine whether the chosen method was reasonably calculated to provide actual notice under *Mullane*.
> 
> That is the knife.
> 
> The next move is to make sure the exhibits prove:
> - the disability notice trail,
> - the writ language using “unknown occupants,”
> - the deed recording date,
> - and the absence of any real pre-lockout notice of December 9.

> ## My honest comfort level
> 
> ### On the motion itself
> Yes, I’m comfortable with the **structure and theory** now.
> 
> It has a real spine:
> - service / jurisdiction,
> - *Mullane* / “under all the circumstances,”
> - lockout-date notice,
> - personal-property authority,
> - bad accounting,
> - 60(B)(1) fallback.
> 
> That is a serious motion.
> 
> ### On filing it today
> Only if the exhibit package is ready and clean.
> 
> Because without the affidavit and exhibits, too much of this remains:
> > “Defendant contends…”
> 
> You need:
> - sworn non-receipt,
> - sworn continuous residence,
> - the pre-suit disability notice trail,
> - the audit summary,
> - the nunc pro tunc filing,
> - the writ/deed/timeline documents,
> - the March 25 retrieval materials.
> 
> That is what makes this a filing instead of a draft.
> 
> ## On sanctions
> 
> This is where you need discipline.
> 
> Do **not** cram every sanctions theory into this 60(B) motion. That will dilute the main shot.
> 
> There are **three different buckets**, and they should be treated differently.
> 
> ### 1. Failure to comply with a court order to pay the clerk
> If there is an actual court order directing payment to the clerk by a certain date, that is the strongest sanctions lane. Indiana Trial Rule 70(A) expressly says the court may enforce obedience, may hold a party in contempt, and may award damages for disobedience of the order. ([rules.incourts.gov](https://rules.incourts.gov/Content/trial/rule70/current.htm)) Rule 67(B) also recognizes payment through the clerk as a post-judgment mechanism. ([rules.incourts.gov](https://rules.incourts.gov/Content/trial/rule67/current.htm))
> 
> That means:
> - if the order is clear,
> - if the deadline passed,
> - and if they still have not paid,
> 
> then the cleaner move is a **separate Motion to Enforce Order / Rule to Show Cause / for Contempt**, not a throw-in paragraph inside this motion. citeturn396646search5turn845739search4
> 
> ### 2. Litigation bad faith / frivolous continuation
> Indiana Code 34-52-1-1 allows attorney’s fees as costs if a party litigated a claim or defense that is frivolous, unreasonable, groundless, or in bad faith. ([law.justia.com](https://law.justia.com/codes/indiana/title-34/article-52/chapter-1/section-34-52-1-1/))
> 
> That is a real lane, but I would **not** lead with it in this motion unless you have a very tight record. Better move:
> - preserve it in the prayer,
> - maybe mention it lightly,
> - then bring it as a more targeted request once the court has seen the accounting contradictions and any noncompliance with the clerk-payment order. citeturn396646search0
> 
> ### 3. Compensation for your own nonstop time
> This is the bad news.
> 
> Your time matters. Your labor is real. But as a **pro se litigant**, getting paid for your own time as “attorney’s fees” is a weak ask. Indiana authority follows the general rule that pro se litigants are not entitled to attorney’s fees for representing themselves. In *Ziobron*, the Court of Appeals noted the Indiana Supreme Court had followed *Kay v. Ehrler* on that point. citeturn782981view0
> 
> So:
> - **your own time spent drafting** is not the best sanctions ask,
> - **out-of-pocket costs**, provable losses, and damages tied to order violations are much stronger.
> 
> ## What I would ask for right now
> 
> ### In this motion
> Keep the sanctions language narrow:
> - preserve the right to costs/fees “if authorized by law,”
> - preserve contempt/enforcement for order violations,
> - focus on vacatur, quash, and property preservation.
> 
> ### In a separate motion, if the facts are clean
> File a targeted motion on:
> - failure to pay the clerk as ordered,
> - contempt under Rule 70(A),
> - damages for disobedience if you can tie them clearly,
> - and possibly fee-shifting / bad-faith litigation if the court record supports it. citeturn396646search5turn396646search0
> 
> ## On equity and your property
> 
> Those are real, but I would separate them conceptually.
> 
> ### Equity / money that should have gone through the clerk
> That is tied to:
> - the accounting problem,
> - the nunc pro tunc issue,
> - and any order requiring payment into the clerk.
> 
> That is a **court-order enforcement / accounting integrity** lane.
> 
> ### Property compensation
> That is more like:
> - conversion,
> - negligent handling,
> - failure to preserve,
> - bailment-type theory,
> - or settlement leverage.
> 
> That is not the cleanest core ask inside a Rule 60 motion. In the motion, the better ask is:
> - preserve remaining property,
> - identify authority for what they did,
> - stop destruction,
> - set hearing.
> 
> Then later:
> - seek compensation or use it for settlement leverage.
> 
> ## My recommendation
> 
> ### File this motion when these are done:
> - affidavit attached,
> - exhibits attached,
> - placeholders filled,
> - clerk-payment order language confirmed.
> 
> ### Do not wait to perfect every damages theory
> This motion’s job is:
> - crack the judgment,
> - crack the writ,
> - crack the presumption of regularity,
> - force a hearing,
> - stop further harm.
> 
> ### Then file, or threaten, a second targeted motion on:
> - contempt / clerk-payment noncompliance,
> - sanctions if warranted,
> - property-preservation / damages issues.
> 
> ## Very blunt answer to your question
> 
> Do I feel comfortable filing **this motion** once the exhibits and affidavit are attached?
> 
> **Yes.**
> 
> Do I feel comfortable filing it **plus every sanctions and compensation theory at once**?
> 
> **No.** That is how good motions get bloated and easier to deny.
> 
> The clean play is:
> - **Motion to Vacate / Quash / Preserve** first,
> - **Contempt / Enforcement / Sanctions** second, if the order language supports it,
> - **Property damages / equity recovery** as a separate lane or settlement demand.
> 
> If you want, next I’ll turn this into a **final pre-filing checklist** so you can see exactly what still has to be attached before you hit file.

### User
> in the jan 9th hearing the judge ignored my argument and she said it was sufficent because there was a checkbox,

### Assistant
> That matters a lot.
> 
> It means you should **not** write this motion as if the court is seeing the checkbox issue for the first time. You need to treat it as a point the court has already leaned against you on, then show why the motion still survives anyway.
> 
> ## What the judge’s January 9 position means
> 
> If the judge already said the checkbox was sufficient, then you should assume this:
> 
> - the court may again treat the sheriff’s return as prima facie proof of service,
> - the court may again reject “I never got it” as enough by itself,
> - and the court may be reluctant to vacate solely on the Rule 4.1(B) theory. Indiana Rule 4.1(B) still requires first-class follow-up mailing after copy service and that fact must be shown on the return, but if the court has already accepted the return notation, you need to build around that reality. citeturn766558search0turn766558search5turn766558search6
> 
> So the lesson is:
> 
> ## Do not make the checkbox issue your only hill to die on
> 
> It stays in the motion, but it becomes:
> 
> - **Lane A**: preserved jurisdiction argument
> - **not**: the only engine of relief
> 
> ## How to reframe it now
> 
> Your motion should effectively say:
> 
> > The Court previously treated the return notation as sufficient. Defendant respectfully preserves his disagreement with that ruling. But even if the Court again concludes that the notation satisfies Trial Rule 4.1(B), the motion still stands because the constitutional adequacy of notice, the lack of meaningful notice of the actual writ execution date, the unexplained handling of personal property, and the plaintiff’s materially unreliable accounting remain unresolved.
> 
> That is the right move.
> 
> ## What to add to the motion
> 
> You need one short paragraph in **Procedural Posture** or the beginning of **Argument** that says, in substance:
> 
> > At the January 9, 2026 hearing, the Court indicated that the service returns were sufficient based on the return notation reflecting mailing. Defendant respectfully preserves his disagreement with that conclusion. This motion therefore presents both the jurisdictional challenge and, independently, the constitutional and equitable grounds for relief that remain even if the Court continues to view the return notation as facially sufficient.
> 
> That does three good things:
> 
> - shows respect for the court
> - preserves the issue
> - signals that you are **not just repeating yourself**
> 
> ## Why this helps strategically
> 
> Because the motion then reads like this:
> 
> ### If the court says:
> **“The checkbox is enough.”**
> 
> You answer:
> - fine, then we are now in **Mullane / under all the circumstances**
> - and in **December 9 lockout-date notice**
> - and in **what authority covered the belongings**
> - and in **your own numbers don’t reconcile**
> 
> That is much harder to brush off.
> 
> ## Also: the rule standard still matters
> 
> Even with the prior ruling, the exact standard remains important:
> 
> - Rule 4.1(B) requires that after copy service, the person making service **also must send** a first-class mailed copy, and that fact must be shown on the return. citeturn766558search0turn766558search5
> - Rule 60(B) still allows relief from a void judgment under subsection (6), and relief for excusable neglect under subsection (1). citeturn766558search2
> - Rule 70 still allows writ-based enforcement and also allows contempt/damages for disobedience of an order, which matters for the clerk-payment lane. citeturn766558search1
> 
> But because the court already signaled how it sees the checkbox, your motion is strongest when it says:
> 
> > Even if the court continues to accept the checkbox as facially sufficient, the process still failed under all the circumstances.
> 
> ## Bottom line
> 
> The January 9 hearing changes the **weighting**, not the **availability**, of your arguments.
> 
> ### Strongest structure now:
> - **Preserve** the checkbox / Rule 4.1(B) challenge
> - **Assume the judge may reject it again**
> - **Win through the stacked lanes anyway**
> 
> That means your best emphasis is now:
> 
> 1. *Mullane* — they never gave the court or sheriff all the circumstances
> 2. no meaningful notice of the actual December 9 removal date
> 3. no clear authority for the method used on your belongings
> 4. the accounting is unstable from start to finish
> 5. alternatively, 60(B)(1) excusable neglect + meritorious defense
> 
> That is the posture I would use. If you want, I’ll patch that January 9 paragraph directly into the motion in the cleanest spot.

> 
> ### 1. It proves pre-suit actual knowledge

>

> - you were in distress,
> - ordinary communication was not working well,
> - phone contact was specifically difficult,
> - and you were asking them to work with you through a more manageable channel. fileciteturn8file0
>

>

> This is where the letter gets strong.
> 
> Your argument is not:
> > this letter alone required them to do X.
> 
> Your argument is:
> > this letter was one of the circumstances they already knew, and those circumstances were material to whether later notice methods were reasonably calculated to actually reach me.
> 
> That is exactly how you should use it.
>

>

>

>

> 
> This letter does **not**, by itself, prove:
> - that FCFCU knew the formal diagnostic labels PTSD/ADHD/executive dysfunction in 2023,
> - that they had a direct ADA legal duty as a private lender,
> - or that service was automatically invalid because of the letter.
>

> - actual notice of serious hardship,
> - actual notice of communication impairment,
> - actual notice that phone communication was a barrier,
> - actual notice of preferred contact channels,
> - and actual notice that cooperative resolution was being requested. fileciteturn8file0

> - they used a **generic label** for a **known person**
> - they represented refusal to vacate

> - then they used the court’s writ process to get immediate, state-backed removal
> 
> That is not just a tone problem. That is a **foundation problem**.
> 
> ## Also important: the decree language cuts against them
> 
> The foreclosure decree language says that once the sheriff’s deed is executed, any person in possession, **upon demand and exhibition of the sheriff’s deed or a true copy**, shall surrender possession, and if possession is refused, the sheriff shall vacate the property. fileciteturn11file4
> 
> So that gives you a cleaner argument:
> 
> > Even under the decree’s own logic, there should be a demand/exhibition/refusal sequence. Plaintiff’s writ petition asserted refusal by “Unknown Occupants,” but did not submit proof showing that Cody Rice-Velasquez, the known occupant, had first been clearly and individually confronted with that demand and refused.
> 
> That is much tighter than just saying “they were lazy.”
>

>

> - added that Plaintiff **submitted no evidence with the writ request** showing a clear individualized demand to Cody followed by refusal
> - strengthened **Section IV.C** so it now says Plaintiff used the generic “Unknown Occupants” label to get the **convenience of immediate court-backed removal** without building a clear record first

### Assistant
> You are **not** confused. The record is doing three different things at once, and they are not the same thing.
> 
> Here is the clean sequence.
> 
> The **7/9 Hart service** appears to be service of the **Notice of Sheriff’s Sale** for the upcoming **8/15/2025 sale**. That document says the property “will expose at public sale” on August 15, 2025, and the bottom reflects sheriff service to Cody Rice-Velasquez at the property address with a handwritten note referencing Hart and 7/9/25. That is **sale notice**, not a demand to surrender possession, not a deed, and not a lockout-date notice. fileciteturn13file0
> 
> The **7/17 plaintiff’s bid** is also not a lockout notice. It is FCFCU’s bid submission for the sheriff’s sale. It uses the sale price around **$240,464.20** and breaks it down using the judgment amount, taxes, interest, and attorney fees. Again, that is a **bid/accounting document**, not a notice that you had to leave the house by a specific date. fileciteturn13file1
> 
> The **9/9 docket activity** is the next separate thing. The CCS shows that on **09/09/2025** the decree of foreclosure was returned as “**Fully Satisfied on 08/15/25 Sheriff Sale, Total Paid $240,464.20, Sale #032**,” and the clerk released the judgment “**By Sheriff Sale**.” That is the docket recognizing the sale result. It is still **not** the same thing as telling you the house is no longer yours on a specific possession date, and it is still **not** notice of the actual date officers will show up and remove you. fileciteturn14file17
> 
> Then on **10/29/2025**, the CCS shows a **Petition for Entry of Writ of Assistance** was filed, and a **Writ of Assistance** document was also filed. The writ document you uploaded is file-stamped **10/29/2025**, but the body says “SO ORDERED” and is dated **November 6, 2025** with the clerk’s signature, which strongly suggests it was prepared/filed with the petition and then actually issued after the court acted. The CCS supports that reading because it separately shows an **“Order for writ of assistance” signed by Judicial Officer Hannah on 11/06/2025**, with **automated paper notice issued to Cody on 11/07/2025**. fileciteturn13file2 fileciteturn14file17
> 
> So on your specific question: **yes, the clerk can issue the writ**. Indiana Trial Rule 70 says that when an order or judgment is for delivery of possession, the prevailing party is entitled to a writ of execution, assistance, or order directing the sheriff to deliver possession **upon application to the clerk**, and the rule also says the clerk shall issue a writ of assistance to compel obedience to the judgment. In other words, the clean reading is: **court order first, clerk-issued writ second, sheriff execution third**. citeturn198032view0
> 
> The part that helps you is **not** “the clerk had no authority ever.” The better argument is this: even if the clerk can issue the writ instrument, the writ process still rests on what the court was told, and the record you’ve shown raises real questions about what the court was actually told.
> 
> That is where your **“fully advised”** point lands. I would not argue, “the judge definitely would have ruled for me if she knew.” That is too absolute. I would argue this instead: the court’s finding that it was “fully advised” is vulnerable because FCFCU’s writ theory treated the property as occupied by **“Unknown Occupants”** who had **“failed to surrender possession,”** while the case had long been against **Cody Rice-Velasquez**, and the writ itself directs service on **Cody Rice-Velasquez and Unknown Occupants**. That is a process problem. They used a **generic surrender/refusal story** for a **known occupant**. fileciteturn13file2
> 
> And the foreclosure decree itself gives you a sharp hook. It says that **upon execution of the sheriff’s deed**, any person in possession, **upon demand and exhibition of the sheriff’s deed or a true copy**, must surrender possession, and **if possession is refused**, then the sheriff shall vacate the property and deliver possession to the purchaser. So the decree’s own structure is: **deed → demand/exhibition → refusal → sheriff vacates**. That is important because your attack is not just “I don’t like what happened.” It is: **where is the individualized demand-and-refusal record for Cody before the court-backed removal?** fileciteturn14file16turn14file19
> 
> That is why the “Unknown Occupants” language matters so much. The writ says: FCFCU is now owner, and **“Unknown Occupants and/or other unknown occupants have failed to surrender possession”**; then it directs the sheriff to serve **Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez, and Unknown Occupants** and deliver possession immediately. So the document itself mixes a known named defendant with a generic occupant category and asserts failure to surrender possession, but the issue you keep pressing is still there: **where is the proof that Cody, the known occupant, was first clearly told to surrender possession and then refused?** fileciteturn13file2
> 
> One correction, though: the CCS does show an **automated paper notice of the 11/6 order for writ** going to Cody on **11/07/2025**. So the clean argument is **not** “there was absolutely no court notice of any writ-related order.” The cleaner and stronger argument is: **there was no meaningful notice of the actual execution date, no individualized demand for surrender supported in the record, and no proof of the decree’s demand/exhibition/refusal sequence before the 12/9 removal.** fileciteturn14file17
> 
> And your urgency point is good. If they truly needed emergency-style possession, they still did not execute until **12/9/2025**, while the deed was not recorded until **12/3/2025**. So the timeline is: writ process in late October / early November, recorded deed on 12/3, lockout on 12/9. That supports your argument that the process was **not a true emergency in the ordinary sense**, but a delayed, convenience-driven enforcement process that still never gave you a meaningful lockout-date notice. fileciteturn14file17
> 
> So the shortest, clearest version is:
> 
> - **7/9 Hart service** = sale notice only. fileciteturn13file0  
> - **7/17 bid** = bid/accounting only. fileciteturn13file1  
> - **9/9 returned decree / clerk release** = sale result recorded, not a surrender-demand notice. fileciteturn14file17  
> - **10/29 petition + 11/6 order + clerk-issued writ** = the writ process. fileciteturn13file2turn14file17  
> - **12/3 deed recorded** and **12/9 removal executed** = separate possession timeline. fileciteturn14file17  
> - your strongest attack is: **no individualized demand, no documented refusal, no meaningful notice of the actual execution date, and “Unknown Occupants” used as a convenience label for a known occupant.**
> 
> That lane is real.

> - Page 5 shows the tax-payment receipt processed on **8/4/2025** for **$7,600.81**. fileciteturn21file0
> - Then later, Plaintiff’s nunc pro tunc position backed away from the **$10,725.27** interest figure and said the correct 30-day interest should have been only **$823.50**, reducing the credit bid by nearly **$10,000**. That is exactly the kind of instability you are talking about. fileciteturn22file2
> 
> So your strongest formulation is:
> 
> > They did not maintain one stable, contemporaneous accounting record. They used one bid number on 7/17, another grand-total structure on the 8/6 worksheet, later surfaced the publication claim only inside a 12/17 packet, stopped explaining the $8,875.81 in added charges, stopped leaning on the extra attorney-fee component, and then later disowned the $10,725.27 interest figure as a “scrivener’s error.”
> 
> That is lethal and still disciplined.
> 
> ## On sanctions
> Yes, ask for them — **narrowly**.
> 
> The safer sanctions lane is:
> 
> - **show-cause / contempt / corrective relief** for post-judgment noncompliance and materially inconsistent accounting submissions
> - **not** a giant emotional sanctions rant
> 
> Why:
> - Indiana Trial Rule **70(A)** expressly says that when a party fails to comply with a judgment directing a specific act, the court may adjudge contempt and may award damages for disobedience of the order. It also says the clerk issues a writ of assistance on application when the judgment is for delivery of possession. citeturn411344view0
> - Indiana Trial Rule **11(A)** is narrower than people think. It says an attorney’s signature certifies there is good ground to support the pleading and that it is not interposed for delay; a sham or false pleading can be stricken, and willful violations can lead to disciplinary action. citeturn411344view1
> 
> So the clean ask is:
> 
> > direct Plaintiff to show cause why sanctions, contempt remedies, cost-shifting, or other corrective relief should not be imposed for materially inconsistent sale-accounting submissions and post-judgment noncompliance.
> 
> That is better than yelling “the court is their puppet.” Do **not** put that in the motion.
> 
> ## On your time
> This part matters, but not the way you want it to.
> 
> You should absolutely **document the time** you have lost because it helps show burden, prejudice, and damages. Your **12/22/2025 declaration** already says that on **December 21, 2025 alone**, you spent approximately **16 hours** responding to the case, organizing records, preserving evidence, and maintaining compliance/access. fileciteturn21file1
> 
> Use that as:
> - burden,
> - prejudice,
> - escalation of harm,
> - and later damages support.
> 
> Do **not** make your pro se time log the centerpiece of the sanctions ask in this motion.
> 
> ## The sentence I would add to the motion
> Put this into **III.G**:
> 
> > Defendant further contends that the publication and sale-cost record did not surface cleanly or contemporaneously. Although Plaintiff’s later-filed sheriff-sale report packet asserted that publication occurred in the Court & Commercial Record and reflected a $284.28 publication payment, that same packet also introduced an added-cost worksheet dated August 6, 2025 listing $8,875.81 in additional charges and a grand total of $241,086.45, while Plaintiff’s earlier filed bid listed a sale amount of $240,464.20, included $1,275.00 in attorney fees, and separately reflected publication-related costs in a different amount. fileciteturn21file0 fileciteturn13file1 Defendant contends that these materials do not read like one stable, contemporaneous accounting chain. They read like post-hoc supplementation after challenge, with inconvenient components later ignored or abandoned.
> 
> Then add this to the **Prayer for Relief**:
> 
> > **DIRECTING**, if the Court deems appropriate, that Plaintiff show cause why sanctions, contempt remedies, cost-shifting, or other corrective relief should not be imposed for materially inconsistent sale-accounting submissions, post-judgment noncompliance, or other conduct established by the record.
> 
> ## One important honesty point

> (0:00) When an engineer looks at a collapsing building, they don't catalog the cracked windows, they check the foundation. (0:07) Today's critique focuses on a submitted draft of a pro se defendant's renewed motion to vacate a default foreclosure judgment. (0:15) Consolidating disparate legal arguments and post-judgment harms into a single motion risks diluting the core jurisdictional challenge.

> (0:24) Yeah, that is exactly the weakness here. The current draft of this renewed motion (0:28) operates as basically a kitchen sink filing. Right.

> (0:32) It blends a very strict procedural defect, which is the lack of the follow-up mailing required by Indiana trial rule (0:38) 4.1b with these highly distinct totally separate issues. Like the plaintiff's (0:44) $10,000 nunc protunc interest miscalculation. Exactly.

> (0:49) It throws that in there along with the arbitrary 9am to 11am window (0:53) they gave for property retrieval and even the tragic closure of a cohabitants federal asylum case. (0:59) Wow. Yeah, I mean by layering all those post-judgment damages and accounting disputes over what is really a threshold (1:08) jurisdictional question, the motion just loses its sharp procedural focus.

> It risks totally overwhelming the court. (1:15) It does. It absolutely does.

> I like to think about this visually, you know? (1:20) Framing the jurisdictional argument as a scalpel and the post-judgment damages as a hammer. (1:26) So if you're presenting the judge with a hammer, doesn't that make it so much harder for them to see the precision of the scalpel? (1:32) Especially since the strategy map in the source notes (1:35) explicitly advised holding back those broader grievances for separate filings. Completely.

> (1:41) You're asking a judge to perform a highly precise technical operation, (1:45) voiding a judgment entirely based on a mailing defect. (1:49) But then you're handing them this massive hammer of messy factual disputes. Right, and judges are busy.

> Exactly. (1:55) So my suggestion here is to disentangle the threshold jurisdictional arguments from the post-judgment damages and accounting disputes. (2:03) You've got to break them into carefully sequenced standalone filings.

> (2:07) Okay, so let's get into some concrete examples of how to do that. What goes where? (2:11) Well, first you file the trial rule 4.1B mailing failure and the ADA notice requirements (2:17) purely as the Rule 60B6 motion to vacate. Just those two things? Just those.

> (2:23) That single document should do absolutely nothing but argue that the court never presents personal jurisdiction, right? (2:29) Making the judgment void from day one. You isolate the scalpel. Okay, that makes sense.

> (2:34) But what about that accounting issue? The source material mentions the plaintiff admitting to a massive miscalculation. (2:40) Right, the almost $10,000 shift. You have to reserve that $9,901 interest reduction admission for a separate (2:49) targeted objection to the accounting and surplus.

> Why keep it separate if it makes the plaintiff look so bad? Because it's fundamentally an accounting dispute. (2:58) If you put it in the motion to vacate, the judge starts looking at the math instead of the missing mail. (3:03) Ah, I see.

> You don't want them conflating the two. Exactly. (3:08) And then for the property destruction and the asylum issues, you move those into a separate damages or sanctions motion.

> (3:15) Or, you know, maybe a federal ADA complaint. Just getting them out of this specific filing. Right.

> (3:21) Ensuring that the state judge is only tasked with ruling on that narrow procedural service defect first. (3:27) That sequencing makes a lot of sense. (3:30) But moving on to the actual writing, relying on highly charged emotional phrasing (3:35) can undermine the objective force of the documented factual evidence.

> (3:40) This is a huge weakness in the current draft. It utilizes adjectives and framing that heavily editorialize the opponent's intent. (3:48) Yeah, I saw phrases like catastrophic, irreparable, and ongoing harm.

> (3:54) Yep, and sloppy bad faith litigation tactics and calling their financial calculus corrupt. (4:00) To be fair, though, I mean, the frustration driving those words is entirely justified by the facts. (4:07) If a bank alters its math by 10 grand after the fact, a normal person calls that corrupt.

> (4:12) Oh, from a human perspective, it is 100% justified. But from a judicial psychology standpoint, it's a trap. (4:20) While the frustration is justified, this emotionally charged rhetoric can trigger a judge's defensive mechanisms.

> (4:27) Let's dig into that for a second. If a judge reads the word corrupt, (4:31) do they instinctively raise their guard and demand a higher burden of proof? They absolutely do. (4:36) Whereas like a cold, undeniable timeline just forces them to see the error without feeling like they're taking sides in a moral dispute.

> (4:44) That is exactly the dynamic. (4:46) When you throw around words like corrupt, you provide opposing counsel a massive opening to dismiss the filing as just, (4:52) you know, hyperbolic venting. Right.

> The opposing counsel gets to stand up and defend their character (4:57) instead of their flawed service. Exactly. They get to say, your honor, we made an administrative oversight.

> We aren't corrupt. (5:05) You've let them change the subject. (5:07) So the suggestion here is to adopt a colder, surgically objective judicial tone (5:12) that allows the timeline and the opponent's own admissions to generate the outrage naturally.

> Show. Don't tell. Precisely.

> (5:21) Let's look at some concrete examples of how to fix this in the text. (5:24) Yeah, how do we take the heat out of the service defect argument? > (5:36) You state simply, the sheriff's returns dated March 5 and April 15, 2025 (5:42) contain no record of the mailing required by trial rule 4.1b. (5:46) Oh, that's much better.
