You'd write, (6:07) plaintiff's February 24, nunk pro-tunk petition sought to retroactively reduce the credit bid by nearly 10,000 dollars, (6:15) explicitly acknowledging the original sworn figure presented to the court was inaccurate.

> Oh, wow. (6:20) That's actually devastating. You aren't calling them liars.

> I love that. (6:34) What about the pre-litigation outreach? Same principle. (6:38) Replace subjective terms like institutional non-engagement with a direct reference to the evidence.

> Frame it like, (6:45) plaintiff received the December 4, 2024 hardship letter, but proceeded with the January 16, (6:53) 2025 filing without documented response. The proximity of those dates does all the (6:59) heavy lifting for you. Exactly.

> It trusts the judge to recognize the bad behavior. (7:05) Okay, so we've cleaned up the structure and the tone, but there's a big legal argument here (7:10) we need to refine. The application of the ADA within the motion lacks a tight, (7:15) explicit bridge to the constitutional standard for due process.

> (7:56) Exactly. But wait, if they don't explicitly tie those together, what's the risk? A state court judge looking at a foreclosure docket might just say, (8:12) look, an ADA violation is a serious federal issue, but that's a matter for a different court.

> (8:18) My job today is just to see if a summons was dropped off. (8:21) Oh, I see. > Plaintiff's possession of the December 2024 (9:40) medical documentation meant they knew irregular doorknocking would fail rendering their service (9:46) method not reasonably calculated to provide notice. That binds the empirical fact of the (9:51) disability directly to the legal failure of the procedure. It does.

> And to cap it off, (9:56) use the opponent's failure to utilize alternative requested communication methods as evidence of (10:02) jurisdictional failure rather than just an ADA violation. What does that mean practically? (10:08) It means if the hardship letter requested communication via email as an accommodation (10:13) and the bank ignored that and just sent a sheriff to knock on the door, (10:16) that proves they weren't making a genuine attempt to provide notice under the Mulani standard. (10:21) So the failure to accommodate becomes the actual proof that their service wasn't (10:25) reasonably calculated.

> Exactly. It elevates it from a civil rights complaint to a fatal (10:30) jurisdictional flaw. Well, to summarize, this highly thoroughly researched motion can really (10:35) maximize its impact by implementing three key adjustments.

> Right. First, separate the arguments. (10:41) Keep the motion to vacate laser focused on the jurisdictional trial rule 4.1 B defect (10:46) while reserving those accounting errors and property damages for separate sequenced filings.

> (10:52) Keep the quarantine zone intact. Exactly. Second, cool the rhetorical temperature by (10:58) replacing emotionally charged labels with cold objective factual timelines that just let the (11:03) opponent's errors speak for themselves.

> Here’s the blunt read.
> 
> ## The critique is **partly right**, not gospel
> 
> ### What it gets right
> The critique is right about **two real vulnerabilities** in the current PDF:
> 
> #### 1. The math should hit earlier and harder
> Right now the PDF opens with the four-lane theory in the Preliminary Statement. That is coherent, but it still asks the judge to absorb a lot before seeing the easiest objective problem: **their numbers are unstable and they admitted one of the biggest errors themselves**. The current PDF still saves the hard accounting punch for Section III.G, around page 24. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> That means the critique is right that:
> - the admitted math error is a **judge-friendly anchor**
> - it is simpler than your ADA/Mullane bridge
> - and it helps establish unreliability before the court gets to your more novel theories
> 
> #### 2. The “Unknown Occupants” pivot should be treated as a central hinge
> The current PDF does discuss it well in Section III.E and Section IV.C, but it is still embedded in the possession timeline rather than being visually isolated as a **standalone act of procedural substitution**. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> That critique is right: this is not just another fact. It is one of the sharpest things in your record.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## What the critique gets wrong for *your* case
> 
> ### 1. I would **not** fully split this into three separate filings first
> That advice is too clean-room academic.
> 
> In a perfect world, yes:
> - service/jurisdiction in one motion,
> - accounting in another,
> - property harm in another.
> 
> But your judge already leaned against you on the checkbox issue. So a **pure Rule 4.1(B) scalpel motion** risks giving the court a very easy way to deny you again and never seriously confront the bigger record.
> 
> For *your* posture, the current bundled structure is strategically defensible because it says:
> 
> - even if the court rejects the mailing defect again,
> - the notice still failed under *Mullane*,
> - the writ / “Unknown Occupants” process still has a defect,
> - the property-handling lane still exists,
> - and the numbers still don’t reconcile.
> 
> That stack matters.
> 
> ### 2. I would not demote the ADA/Mullane bridge too far
> The critique is right that the ADA theory should be **tightly tied** to due process, not treated as a floating civil-rights essay.
> 
> But the current PDF already does a decent job of that in Section IV.B by using *Mullane*’s “under all the circumstances” standard and explaining that the omitted disability-related facts were material to notice adequacy. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> So the fix is not:
> - remove the ADA lane.
> 
> The fix is:
> - **tighten the bridge even more** and maybe move one punchier sentence forward.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## What the current PDF still gets wrong
> 
> This is the important part.
> 
> ### 1. It still has the **wrong deed date** in key places
> The PDF says in Procedural Posture that the Sheriff’s Deed was not recorded until **December 2, 2025**. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> It also says that again in the prayer for relief. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> But your record supports **December 3, 2025**, not December 2.
> 
> That needs to be fixed everywhere. No excuses.
> 
> ### 2. The sanctions/show-cause language is still missing
> The current PDF’s prayer asks only for ordinary costs/fees and general relief. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> The later stronger version we built included a **show-cause / sanctions / contempt / corrective-relief** ask tied to:
> - materially inconsistent sale-accounting submissions
> - post-judgment noncompliance
> - other conduct established by the record
> 
> That is not in this PDF. It should be.
> 
> ### 3. The exhibit placeholders are still sloppy
> The PDF still has:
> - `See Exs. –.`
> - `See Ex. __`
> - unresolved placeholders throughout the facts and argument sections. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> That is draft language. It cannot stay.
> 
> ### 4. The exhibit outline is still older and weaker
> The PDF still uses the older generic exhibit outline and does not reflect the later cleanup around:
> - actual sent surplus demand vs. draft analysis
> - later clerk/sheriff packet inconsistencies
> - communications/preservation-device issues
> - show-cause sanction lane
> 
> It is usable as a drafting aid, but not fully aligned with where the motion ended up. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> ### 5. The opening is still a little too abstract for this judge
> The Preliminary Statement is strong, but it is still **concept-first**, not **record-first**. fileciteturn26file0
> 
> For this judge, I think the opening should hit this sequence faster:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff later admitted its credit-bid math was wrong by nearly $10,000.
> 2. Plaintiff also used “Unknown Occupants” in the writ process despite Cody being the named defendant.
> 3. Plaintiff still cannot show a clear individualized surrender-demand / refusal path before lockout.
> 4. The court was not given all the circumstances relevant under *Mullane*.
> 
> That is a more punishing entry point.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Best revision strategy from here
> 
> Not a full rewrite. A **surgical restructuring**.
> 
> ### Keep the four-lane structure
> Do **not** blow it up.
> 
> That is still the right backbone for your posture.
> 
> ### But revise the opening order of emphasis
> Make the first page do this:
> 
> #### Opening paragraph
> State the relief.
> 
> #### Next paragraph
> Lead with **objective unreliability**:
> - admitted math error
> - shifting sale numbers
> - post-hoc accounting papers
> - “Unknown Occupants” substitution
> - no clear surrender-demand/refusal record
> 
> #### Then move into the legal architecture
> - jurisdiction
> - constitutional adequacy
> - property authority
> - alternative excusable neglect
> 
> That way the judge sees the **record rot** before the doctrinal bridge.
> 
> ### Create a standalone bolded subheading in the facts
> Something like:
> 
> **E. Plaintiff Recast a Known Occupant as “Unknown Occupants” to Obtain Immediate Writ-Based Removal Without a Clear Individualized Demand-and-Refusal Record**
> 
> That needs to stand on its own.
> 
> ### Tighten the ADA sentence
> Use this logic explicitly:
> 
> > Because Plaintiff allegedly possessed pre-suit knowledge of Defendant’s documented communication barriers, those facts were part of the “circumstances” the court was constitutionally required to consider under *Mullane*. A notice method selected and evaluated without those facts cannot be treated as reliably calculated to provide actual notice under the circumstances actually known to Plaintiff.
> 
> That is cleaner and meaner.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## My recommendation on filing posture
> 
> ### Do not file this current PDF unchanged
> It is better than the `.txt`, but still not ready.
> 
> ### Fix these first
> - deed date to **12/3/2025** everywhere
> - add sanctions/show-cause language back in
> - fill every exhibit placeholder
> - sharpen the opening so math + “Unknown Occupants” land sooner
> - isolate the “Unknown Occupants” pivot under its own subheading
> - clean the exhibit outline
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Bottom line
> 
> The transcript critique is useful as a **pressure test**, but it overstates the need to split everything apart.
> 
> For *your* case:
> - **keep the stacked lanes**
> - **front-load the admitted numerical unreliability**
> - **elevate the “Unknown Occupants” pivot**
> - **tighten the ADA-to-*Mullane* bridge**
> - and **fix the factual/drafting defects in the current PDF**
> 
> That is the right evolution.
> 
> If you want, I’ll turn that into a **five-point exact edit plan by page number** for the current PDF.

### Assistant
> I’m verifying the actual cases and rules against primary/current sources, then I’m going note-by-note and separating what should go in this motion, what should be an exhibit, and what should be saved for a later sanctions/damages lane.

### Assistant
> I checked the authorities and the note list. The short version:
> 
> - **The core citations are real.**
> - **There are a few places where the authority needs to be sharpened or dated correctly.**
> - **Several of your notes absolutely should go into this motion.**
> - **Several others should be quarantined for a later sanctions / spoliation / damages filing.**
> 
> ## 1. Citation audit
> 
> ### Confirmed and usable
> Your current authorities are real and generally on-point:
> 
> - **Indiana Trial Rules 4.1, 4.14, 4.15(F), 60(B), and 70** all exist on the official Indiana rules site. Rule 4.1(B) still requires follow-up first-class mail after copy service; Rule 4.14 allows special-order service reasonably calculated to give actual knowledge; Rule 4.15(F) is the defects/savings provision; Rule 60(B) covers void judgments and excusable neglect; Rule 70 covers writs of assistance and contempt/damages for disobedience. citeturn103614search0turn551088search0turn551088search1turn103614search2turn621952view0
> - **Barrow, LePore, Boczar, Stidham, Allstate, Swiggett, Alexander, Tennessee v. Lane, and Seleme** are real cases with the citations you are using. citeturn214040search0turn115411search0turn355421search0turn355421search1turn214040search1turn284400search1turn873709search1turn522915search3turn284400search0
> 
> ### Corrections / tightening
> These are the ones I would fix:
> 
> #### A. Use the **2025 version** of Rule 4.1(B) when quoting the service rule
> The official Indiana rules page shows Rule 4.1 was amended effective **January 1, 2026**. The current version says the server “**must** send” the first-class mailing to the “**address on the summons**.” The immediately prior version, effective **January 1, 2018 through January 1, 2026**, used the older wording about “**shall send**” to the “**last known address**.” Your service events happened in **March and April 2025**, so if you quote the rule text, quote the **pre-2026 version**, not the current one. citeturn103614search0turn103614search3
> 
> #### B. Sharpen the ADA statutes
> Your generic cite to **42 U.S.C. § 12101 et seq.** is not hallucinated, but it is too broad for the actual argument. For the Title II lane, use:
> - **42 U.S.C. § 12131(1)** for “public entity”
> - **42 U.S.C. § 12132** for the operative discrimination provision
> - optionally **28 C.F.R. § 35.160** for effective communication. citeturn522915search0turn522915search1turn522915search3
> 
> #### C. Fix the Seleme caption
> Use the fuller form:
> **Seleme v. JP Morgan Chase Bank, Nat’l Ass’n, 982 N.E.2d 299 (Ind. Ct. App. 2013).**  
> Your shortened version is close, but this is cleaner. citeturn284400search0
> 
> #### D. Exact “$10,000” figure
> Do **not** say “about $10,000” anymore. Use the exact figure:
> - original credit bid: **$240,464.20**
> - revised bid sought: **$230,562.40**
> - difference: **$9,901.80**  
> The Plaintiff also filed a petition on **February 20, 2026** agreeing that surplus funds of **$8,378.42** existed. fileciteturn23file1 fileciteturn23file2
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Note-by-note advice
> 
> A few of your note titles are too vague to map precisely without the exact sentence, so I’m giving best-fit guidance.
> 
> ### “Statutes / confirm” and “Rules / confirm cites”
> **Yes, confirm them, but refine them.**  
> Keep the Indiana Trial Rule cites. Replace the broad ADA cite with the sharper Title II cites above. And for any direct quotation of Rule 4.1(B), use the **2018–2025 version**, not the current 2026 wording. citeturn103614search0turn103614search3turn551088search0turn551088search1turn103614search2turn621952view0turn522915search0turn522915search1turn522915search3
> 
> ### “property / Should I provide an inventory and estimated value.”
> **Yes. Absolutely.**  
> But make it a **separate exhibit**, not a wall of text in the motion.
> 
> Best format:
> - item
> - category
> - condition before / after
> - estimated fair-market value
> - estimated replacement cost
> - basis for estimate
> - whether essential for work / shelter / litigation
> 
> This will help both the **property-preservation** lane and the later **damages / settlement** lane.
> 
> ### “10,000 / put the exact amount”
> **Yes. Use $9,901.80.**  
> And also use the Plaintiff’s own **$8,378.42 surplus agreement** as part of the instability story. fileciteturn23file1 fileciteturn23file2
> 
> ### “Defendant / what law supports this statement.”
> Best-fit answer:
> - if the statement is about a **void judgment**, use **Stidham**
> - if it is about **default disfavor**, use **Allstate**
> - if it is about **Rule 4.1(B) as jurisdictional**, use **Barrow / Boczar / LePore / Swiggett**. citeturn355421search1turn214040search1turn214040search0turn355421search0turn115411search0turn284400search1
> 
> ### “ultimately / what does case law state here.”
> If this is about the **personal-property disposal** section, my advice is:
> - do **not** overclaim that Indiana case law clearly forbids exactly what they did
> - keep the argument phrased as:
>   > Plaintiff has not identified clear authority for this method.
> That is stronger than pretending you already have a case saying “dump-truck disposal after foreclosure is unlawful per se.”
> 
> ### “Although / any historical cases here.”
> If this is about the default-judgment policy or equitable posture, **Allstate** is the clean one. If it is about notice defects vs. technical defects, **LaPalme** is useful as a supporting case for 4.15(F) curing technical defects, not total failure, and that idea is also echoed in recent Indiana appellate discussion. citeturn214040search1turn355421search0
> 
> ### “fair / fair or unconstitutional when considering all the facts of the case.”
> Use **both**, but in the right places:
> - **“constitutionally inadequate”** in the legal argument
> - **“fundamentally unfair”** as a descriptive bridge
> Do **not** swap “unfair” in where you need a constitutional standard.
> 
> ### “Plaintiff / full history like clerk receipt in June and credit report in June…”
> Split this.
> 
> **Put in this motion now:**
> - June clerk receipt / late-filed worksheet / sale-accounting chain problems  
> That belongs in the accounting unreliability lane.
> 
> **Probably hold for later or footnote/exhibit:**
> - credit-report deletion / reappearance  
> That is useful, but it starts drifting toward a separate reporting / damages lane unless you use it very lightly as one more sign of unstable records.
> 
> ### “D / should we not highlight hardship making disability worse”
> **Yes. One sentence, not a whole therapy block.**  
> Use it to explain why the communication barriers were not static.
> 
> ### “d / this is not exactly right, it was multiple emails and calls in 2023, a letter Dec 2023 and 2 certified notices in 2024.”
> **Yes, fix this.**  
> Your facts section should use the exact chronology you can prove:
> - multiple calls/emails in 2023
> - December 2023 letter
> - two certified notices in 2024
> 
> Do not keep it vague if you can make it precise.
> 
> ### “In / newsletters and debit card but not ‘you must leave by Dec 9’”
> **Yes, but use it sparingly.**  
> One sentence only, and only if you have exhibits. It is a good common-sense *Mullane* point:
> they could mail ordinary account materials, but the record still does not show a coherent, individualized path telling you the actual lockout date.
> 
> ### “Defendant / the 99% thing… trauma… office late… could blow away”
> **Yes, but put this in your affidavit, not the main motion body.**  
> The motion can keep the 99% line. The affidavit should explain the nuance:
> - first attempt you were at work late
> - because of trauma you sometimes did not enter all parts of the house for days/weeks
> - a copy-left paper could be removed, blown away, or never meaningfully seen
> 
> That makes the 99% statement less vulnerable.
> 
> ### “The / off-duty officer said he didn’t know / disorganized schedules”
> **Include only if the video is clear and authenticated.**  
> If yes, that is good supporting evidence for the **convenience-driven / disorganized execution** theme.  
> If not crystal clear, save it for hearing.
> 
> ### “Defendant / add David another friend.”
> Only if David is:
> - a witness,
> - a named helper in transport/retrieval,
> - or relevant to a concrete fact.
> 
> Do not add people just because they were around.
> 
> ### “followed / I don’t know if I want to include this.”
> Then don’t.  
> If you are uncertain, leave it out of the motion and keep it in reserve.
> 
> ### “Upon / dump truck almost full already… started throwing stuff as soon as I pulled up…”
> **Yes. Add this.**  
> That is stronger than a generalized property-damage sentence because it shows an **already-ongoing disposal process**, not a neutral retrieval scene.
> 
> ### “Defendant / doesn’t this show worksheet should have had about 250000 instead of 241000”
> **Do not write that unless the chart proves it cleanly.**  
> That is exactly the kind of number that will get attacked if it is approximate. Put the chart together first.
> 
> ### “interest / I searched the publication site and found other foreclosures but not mine”
> Use this **cautiously**.
> 
> The stronger line is:
> > no publisher’s affidavit and no actual published notice surfaced cleanly in the record when demanded.
> 
> Your personal search is useful as background, but not the main legal proof.
> 
> ### “Property / should this be more robust to highlight the crazy timeline in black and white”
> **Yes.**  
> Make a one-page **timeline exhibit**.  
> This will help the judge more than another paragraph.
> 
> ### “Trial / review law”
> If this note is about the writ/possession process, the right rule is still **Trial Rule 70**, and the right factual hook is still the **decree’s demand/exhibition/refusal structure** plus the later “Unknown Occupants” substitution. citeturn621952view0
> 
> ### “The / and also 30 days had not passed for all defendants”
> I would **not use this unless you can prove the exact procedural significance**.  
> Be careful here. Multiple defendants do not automatically prevent default against one, and you do not need a weak timing argument cluttering the motion.
> 
> ### “Defendant / accidents, hotels, score suppressed, still can’t get housing”
> **Mostly later.**  
> You can keep:
> - hotel costs / travel burden / housing instability  
> as prejudice.
> 
> But:
> - accident photos
> - credit-score suppression months later  
> are better as damages/sanctions/supporting exhibits, not core motion text.
> 
> ### “required / I have put in hundreds of hours already.”
> Document it in a declaration or log.  
> But do **not** turn it into a fee demand centerpiece. Indiana generally follows the rule that pro se litigants are not entitled to attorney’s fees under fee-shifting statutes in the ordinary way, especially after *Kay* and Indiana cases discussing that principle. citeturn216644search0
> 
> Use your hours to show:
> - prejudice
> - burden
> - practical exhaustion
> - why the court should stop the bleeding
> 
> ### “defending / spoliation, privacy, off-duty officer, security guards, standing order, settlement, private rooms”
> This is too much for one motion.
> 
> #### Keep in this motion if documented and directly tied:
> - standing-order noncompliance
> - post-judgment noncompliance
> - property-preservation interference
> - communications / router / camera / evidence interference
> 
> #### Hold for later sanctions / spoliation / privacy filing:
> - off-duty officer acting as state actor
> - security-guard inconsistency
> - videoing private rooms
> - broader spoliation theory
> - settlement / Rule 68 / 403-type material unless you have a very specific tactical reason
> 
> Do **not** make this motion a warehouse.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. What I would change in the canvas next
> 
> Top-priority fixes:
> 
> 1. **Replace the broad ADA cite** with:
>    - 42 U.S.C. § 12131(1)
>    - 42 U.S.C. § 12132
>    - 28 C.F.R. § 35.160 if you want effective communication citeturn522915search0turn522915search1turn522915search3
> 
> 2. **Change “nearly $10,000” to “$9,901.80”** and tie it to:
>    - original bid **$240,464.20**
>    - revised bid **$230,562.40**
>    - surplus agreement **$8,378.42** fileciteturn23file1 fileciteturn23file2
> 
> 3. **Fix the pre-suit chronology** from vague to precise:

> Isolating jurisdictional defects in foreclosure motions

> (0:00) When an engineer looks at a collapsing building, they don't catalog the cracked windows, they check the foundation. (0:07) Today's critique focuses on a submitted draft of a pro se defendant's renewed motion to vacate a default foreclosure judgment. (0:15) Consolidating disparate legal arguments and post-judgment harms into a single motion risks diluting the core jurisdictional challenge.

> (0:24) Yeah, that is exactly the weakness here. The current draft of this renewed motion (0:28) operates as basically a kitchen sink filing. Right.

> (0:32) It blends a very strict procedural defect, which is the lack of the follow-up mailing required by Indiana trial rule (0:38) 4.1b with these highly distinct totally separate issues. Like the plaintiff's (0:44) $10,000 nunc protunc interest miscalculation. Exactly.

> (0:49) It throws that in there along with the arbitrary 9am to 11am window (0:53) they gave for property retrieval and even the tragic closure of a cohabitants federal asylum case. (0:59) Wow. Yeah, I mean by layering all those post-judgment damages and accounting disputes over what is really a threshold (1:08) jurisdictional question, the motion just loses its sharp procedural focus.

> It risks totally overwhelming the court. (1:15) It does. It absolutely does.

> I like to think about this visually, you know? (1:20) Framing the jurisdictional argument as a scalpel and the post-judgment damages as a hammer. (1:26) So if you're presenting the judge with a hammer, doesn't that make it so much harder for them to see the precision of the scalpel? (1:32) Especially since the strategy map in the source notes (1:35) explicitly advised holding back those broader grievances for separate filings. Completely.

> (1:41) You're asking a judge to perform a highly precise technical operation, (1:45) voiding a judgment entirely based on a mailing defect. (1:49) But then you're handing them this massive hammer of messy factual disputes. Right, and judges are busy.

> Exactly. (1:55) So my suggestion here is to disentangle the threshold jurisdictional arguments from the post-judgment damages and accounting disputes. (2:03) You've got to break them into carefully sequenced standalone filings.

> (2:07) Okay, so let's get into some concrete examples of how to do that. What goes where? (2:11) Well, first you file the trial rule 4.1B mailing failure and the ADA notice requirements (2:17) purely as the Rule 60B6 motion to vacate. Just those two things? Just those.

> (2:23) That single document should do absolutely nothing but argue that the court never presents personal jurisdiction, right? (2:29) Making the judgment void from day one. You isolate the scalpel. Okay, that makes sense.

> (2:34) But what about that accounting issue? The source material mentions the plaintiff admitting to a massive miscalculation. (2:40) Right, the almost $10,000 shift. You have to reserve that $9,901 interest reduction admission for a separate (2:49) targeted objection to the accounting and surplus.

> Why keep it separate if it makes the plaintiff look so bad? Because it's fundamentally an accounting dispute. (2:58) If you put it in the motion to vacate, the judge starts looking at the math instead of the missing mail. (3:03) Ah, I see.

> You don't want them conflating the two. Exactly. (3:08) And then for the property destruction and the asylum issues, you move those into a separate damages or sanctions motion.

> (3:15) Or, you know, maybe a federal ADA complaint. Just getting them out of this specific filing. Right.

> (3:21) Ensuring that the state judge is only tasked with ruling on that narrow procedural service defect first. (3:27) That sequencing makes a lot of sense. (3:30) But moving on to the actual writing, relying on highly charged emotional phrasing (3:35) can undermine the objective force of the documented factual evidence.

> (3:40) This is a huge weakness in the current draft. It utilizes adjectives and framing that heavily editorialize the opponent's intent. (3:48) Yeah, I saw phrases like catastrophic, irreparable, and ongoing harm.

> (3:54) Yep, and sloppy bad faith litigation tactics and calling their financial calculus corrupt. (4:00) To be fair, though, I mean, the frustration driving those words is entirely justified by the facts. (4:07) If a bank alters its math by 10 grand after the fact, a normal person calls that corrupt.

> (4:12) Oh, from a human perspective, it is 100% justified. But from a judicial psychology standpoint, it's a trap. (4:20) While the frustration is justified, this emotionally charged rhetoric can trigger a judge's defensive mechanisms.

> (4:27) Let's dig into that for a second. If a judge reads the word corrupt, (4:31) do they instinctively raise their guard and demand a higher burden of proof? They absolutely do. (4:36) Whereas like a cold, undeniable timeline just forces them to see the error without feeling like they're taking sides in a moral dispute.

> (4:44) That is exactly the dynamic. (4:46) When you throw around words like corrupt, you provide opposing counsel a massive opening to dismiss the filing as just, (4:52) you know, hyperbolic venting. Right.

> The opposing counsel gets to stand up and defend their character (4:57) instead of their flawed service. Exactly. They get to say, your honor, we made an administrative oversight.

> We aren't corrupt. (5:05) You've let them change the subject. (5:07) So the suggestion here is to adopt a colder, surgically objective judicial tone (5:12) that allows the timeline and the opponent's own admissions to generate the outrage naturally.

> Show. Don't tell. Precisely.

> (5:21) Let's look at some concrete examples of how to fix this in the text. (5:24) Yeah, how do we take the heat out of the service defect argument? (5:29) Well, instead of writing, the plaintiff entirely failed to execute this jurisdictional prerequisite, which sounds like a personal attack. Right.

> (5:36) You state simply, the sheriff's returns dated March 5 and April 15, 2025 (5:42) contain no record of the mailing required by trial rule 4.1b. (5:46) Oh, that's much better. Opposing counsel can argue all day against an accusation of failure, (5:51) but they can't argue with a blank space on a physical record. (5:55) Exactly.

> Now, let's apply that to the accounting dispute. (5:59) Rather than calling the credit bid sloppy, bad faith litigation tactics, you describe it objectively. (6:04) How would you phrase that? You'd write, (6:07) plaintiff's February 24, nunk pro-tunk petition sought to retroactively reduce the credit bid by nearly 10,000 dollars, (6:15) explicitly acknowledging the original sworn figure presented to the court was inaccurate.

> Oh, wow. (6:20) That's actually devastating. You aren't calling them liars.

> (6:23) You're just pointing out that they formally admitted under oath that their previous sworn statement was wrong. (6:29) Right. It traps them in their own filings without you ever having to raise your voice.

> I love that. (6:34) What about the pre-litigation outreach? Same principle. (6:38) Replace subjective terms like institutional non-engagement with a direct reference to the evidence.

> Frame it like, (6:45) plaintiff received the December 4, 2024 hardship letter, but proceeded with the January 16, (6:53) 2025 filing without documented response. The proximity of those dates does all the (6:59) heavy lifting for you. Exactly.

> It trusts the judge to recognize the bad behavior. (7:05) Okay, so we've cleaned up the structure and the tone, but there's a big legal argument here (7:10) we need to refine. The application of the ADA within the motion lacks a tight, (7:15) explicit bridge to the constitutional standard for due process.

> (7:19) This is probably the most complex weakness in the draft. The current text argues that the (7:24) ADA was violated during the service process and the property retrieval. (7:29) Right.

> But it somewhat isolates the ADA claim as a standalone statutory grievance. It does not (7:35) sufficiently tie the plaintiff's knowledge of the cognitive disabilities, the ADHD, the PTSD, (7:42) the executive dysfunction directly to the Moulain constitutional standard. (7:46) Moulain being the Supreme Court standard for valid service, right? The requirement for notice (7:51) reasonably calculated under all the circumstances to apprise interested parties.

> (7:56) Exactly. But wait, if they don't explicitly tie those together, what's the risk? Is it that the (8:01) court views the ADA claim as a separate administrative issue outside their immediate purview? (8:06) Yes, completely. A state court judge looking at a foreclosure docket might just say, (8:12) look, an ADA violation is a serious federal issue, but that's a matter for a different court.

> (8:18) My job today is just to see if a summons was dropped off. (8:21) Oh, I see. So how do we trap the court into seeing the disability not as a request for (8:27) special treatment, but as the exact reason the chosen service method was legally and constitutionally (8:33) void? The suggestion is to weave the ADA obligations directly into the constitutional (8:39) due process framework.

> You use the opponent's actual knowledge of the disability to prove (8:44) exactly why their copy left service failed the fundamental constitutional test. (8:50) Copy left meaning they just taped the summons to the door or left it with someone at the house? (8:54) Right. Okay.

> So give me a concrete example of how to explicitly argue this. (8:59) You would explicitly argue that because plaintiff had actual knowledge of the (9:02) auditory and executive dysfunction impairments via the 2023 and December 2024 certified hardship (9:09) letters, relying on standard copy left service without the required mailing was constitutionally (9:14) inadequate. If you know someone is out of the country, slipping a note under their door isn't (9:19) reasonably calculated.

> If you know they have severe executive dysfunction in a regular doorknock (9:25) is objectively unreasonable. That's a brilliant pivot. How should they actually write that (9:30) sentence in the motion? Frame the argument to state due process requires notice tailored (9:35) to the known circumstances of the defendant.

> Plaintiff's possession of the December 2024 (9:40) medical documentation meant they knew irregular doorknocking would fail rendering their service (9:46) method not reasonably calculated to provide notice. That binds the empirical fact of the (9:51) disability directly to the legal failure of the procedure. It does.

> And to cap it off, (9:56) use the opponent's failure to utilize alternative requested communication methods as evidence of (10:02) jurisdictional failure rather than just an ADA violation. What does that mean practically? (10:08) It means if the hardship letter requested communication via email as an accommodation (10:13) and the bank ignored that and just sent a sheriff to knock on the door, (10:16) that proves they weren't making a genuine attempt to provide notice under the Mulani standard. (10:21) So the failure to accommodate becomes the actual proof that their service wasn't (10:25) reasonably calculated.

> Exactly. It elevates it from a civil rights complaint to a fatal (10:30) jurisdictional flaw. Well, to summarize, this highly thoroughly researched motion can really (10:35) maximize its impact by implementing three key adjustments.

> Right. First, separate the arguments. (10:41) Keep the motion to vacate laser focused on the jurisdictional trial rule 4.1 B defect (10:46) while reserving those accounting errors and property damages for separate sequenced filings.

> (10:52) Keep the quarantine zone intact. Exactly. Second, cool the rhetorical temperature by (10:58) replacing emotionally charged labels with cold objective factual timelines that just let the (11:03) opponent's errors speak for themselves.

> So don't tell. Yep. And finally, tightly bind the ADA (11:10) disability knowledge directly to the constitutional standard for due process, (11:14) proving why the service method was legally void under the circumstances.

> It's all there in the (11:19) research. It just needs this specific framing. Please submit your revised drafts back to us.

> (11:23) We would love to see how this material evolves.

> ## What we accepted
> We took the useful parts:
> 
> - **front-loaded the objective unreliability**
>   - admitted **$9,901.80** bid reduction
>   - unstable accounting
>   - shifting numbers

>   - it now has its own stronger section and subheading

>   - less “they’re corrupt”
>   - more “their own filings don’t reconcile”
> - **tightened the ADA-to-*Mullane* bridge**
>   - disability knowledge is now framed as part of the **“under all the circumstances”** analysis, not a floating side grievance
> - **added the Jan. 9 posture**
>   - so the motion no longer acts like the checkbox issue is brand new
> 
> ## What we did **not** accept
> We did **not** fully follow the critique’s advice to split everything into separate filings right now.
> 
> That advice is too clean and too academic for your actual posture.
> 
> Why I rejected that as the main strategy:
> - the judge already leaned against you on the checkbox issue
> - a pure “missing mail only” motion gives the court an easy off-ramp
> - your stronger position is the **stacked failure theory**:
>   - maybe service was defective
>   - even if the court says no, notice still failed under *Mullane*
>   - the writ process still has the **Known Defendant → Unknown Occupants** substitution problem
>   - the property-handling lane still exists
>   - the accounting still does not reconcile
> 
> That stack is what makes the motion harder to brush off.
> 
> ## Bottom line
> So yes, we reviewed it already.
> 
> The result was:
> - **keep the four-lane structure**
> - **make it colder**
> - **move the math and “Unknown Occupants” up front**
> - **tighten the constitutional bridge**
> - **do not let the whole motion live or die on Rule 4.1(B) alone**
> 
> That is the version currently reflected in canvas.

### Assistant
> Yes. That matters.
> 
> And the strongest part is **not** “they adjudicated my case” as a legal conclusion.  
> The strongest part is:
> 
> - **Ms. Abbott appears to know personal facts from your hardship history**
> - they are discussing **another known occupant** who is away at work
> - they are deciding in real time how to handle that occupant
> - they are improvising signage / contact instructions in the field
> - all of that cuts against any clean story that this was a fully orderly, previously communicated, individualized possession process
> 
> ## Why this helps
> 
> ### 1. It supports actual knowledge
> If Ms. Abbott is casually referencing that your husband was deported, that strongly supports the inference that someone on the bank side **did read or absorb** your hardship communications.
> 
> That helps your argument because it undercuts:
> > “we didn’t know enough to do anything differently.”
> 
> It supports:
> > they knew personal hardship facts, but still did not turn that knowledge into a structured, responsive notice process.
> 
> ### 2. It supports the “known occupant(s), not unknown occupants” lane
> If they are discussing another roommate who is currently at work and planning how to handle that person’s return, that is very helpful.
> 
> Because then the picture becomes:
> - they are not dealing with mysterious “unknown occupants”
> - they are dealing with a house they understand to contain **specific people**
> - but the writ/petition still used the **generic label**
> - and the field strategy is being improvised anyway
> 
> That helps your **Known Defendant / Known Occupants vs. Unknown Occupants** theory.
> 
> ### 3. It supports disorganized, ad hoc field execution
> The no-trespassing-sign discussion is useful because it suggests they were making **live operational decisions** about access and occupancy in the moment.
> 
> That supports:
> - this was not a cleanly preplanned transition
> - they were still figuring out how to manage absent occupants
> - the process was reactive and improvised
> 
> ### 4. “Call the clerk” is weird, but don’t overplay it
> You’re right that it sounds odd.
> 
> But I would not turn that into:
> > therefore they were acting illegally
> 
> Use it more carefully:
> > it reflects uncertainty or improvisation about how occupancy and access questions would actually be handled in the field.
> 
> That is safer and still useful.
> 
> ### 5. The delayed return with the no-trespassing sign matters
> If she did not return with the sign until around 1:00 p.m. or later, that helps the same theme:
> - they were still making and implementing occupancy-control decisions **after** the removal began
> - that is not what a fully structured, already-settled possession transition looks like
> 
> ## What I added to canvas
> I added a new paragraph after the “paperwork / he just got it” paragraph.
> 
> It now says, in substance:
> 
> - related video shows on-scene operational discussion inconsistent with a fully orderly, preplanned possession transition
> - Ms. Abbott is heard discussing personal background facts, including that your husband had been deported
> - they discuss another occupant who is away at work
> - they discuss placing a no-trespassing sign and telling people to contact the clerk
> - if authenticated, this supports the inference that Plaintiff had in fact absorbed substantive information from your hardship communications while still failing to convert that knowledge into a structured, disability-responsive notice process
> - and that the agents were making **ad hoc possession and access decisions in real time**
> 
> ## Best way to use this later
> When you verify the clip, do **not** summarize it loosely.
> 
> Create a short exhibit note like:
> 
> - file name
> - timestamp
> - exact verified quote
> - one-sentence relevance
> 
> That way it lands as evidence, not interpretation.

> - **actual knowledge**
> - **known occupants**

### User
> but them ignoring me was really there only way to shield against not doing an interactive process, so her confiramtion her that she new of my hardship which i also discussed my disabilities in that letter she is referencing, then they shoudl have know, and not only is it my constitutional civil right for them to have engaged in a interactive ada process, to formuly deny my accomodation request if they found it to burdensume to email me, and under NCUA regulations and some case law they are supposed to or at least encouraged to attempt mitigating forclosure talks and options but repeately told me there is nothing they can do and i just need to pay while making it harder and harder for me to pay until I just couldnt pay anything, to then forclose on my anyways, use the worst method of conduct to get default then not even try to notify me about anything after the judement they went silent until dec. 9th. this is all facts adn provable.

### Assistant
> I’m checking the bank-side legal hooks before I tell you to frame it that way, because this is exactly where one overstatement gives them a cheap win. The facts help you a lot; the bank-duty theory needs to be phrased with precision.

### Assistant
> Yes — but tighten the law, because one overstatement will hand them an escape hatch.
> 
> ## What is strong and provable
> Your fact pattern is strong if you can prove it:
> 
> - FCFCU had pre-suit notice of hardship and communication barriers through your letters/emails/calls. fileciteturn8file0
> - FCFCU later used a minimalist service method on you while using a more formal/documentable method on the corporate defendants. fileciteturn10file13
> - FCFCU then went silent on any meaningful possession-transition notice and moved into writ-based removal using “Unknown Occupants,” even though you were the named defendant. fileciteturn23file0
> - FCFCU later admitted the sale-accounting figures were wrong and sought to reduce the credit bid from **$240,464.20** to **$230,562.40**, a difference of **$9,901.80**. fileciteturn23file2
> - FCFCU also filed a separate petition on **February 20, 2026** agreeing surplus funds of **$8,378.42** existed. fileciteturn23file1
> 
> Those facts absolutely support a theme of **knowledge + nonresponse + procedural shortcut + unreliable numbers**.
> 
> ## What to **stop saying**
> Do **not** say:
> 
> > it was my constitutional civil right for the bank to engage in an interactive ADA process.
> 
> That is too broad and too easy to attack.
> 
> Why:
> - **Title II** is the ADA title that applies to **state and local governments**, including courts and sheriffs, not private banks. citeturn374560search1
> - **Title III** applies to businesses open to the public and includes effective-communication / reasonable-modification concepts, but it is not the same thing as a clean, standalone constitutional duty for a private lender to run a formal “interactive ADA process” the way employment law discussions often use that phrase. citeturn374560search0turn374560search6
> 
> ## What to say instead
> Use this framing:
> 
> > Plaintiff’s documented knowledge of Defendant’s communication barriers was materially relevant to whether the notice method it chose was reasonably calculated, under all the circumstances, to provide actual notice under *Mullane*. Plaintiff’s failure to adjust its communications, disclose those circumstances to the court-enforcement process, or use the channels Defendant had identified as workable supports the conclusion that the process was not reasonably calculated to reach this Defendant in practice.
> 
> That is tighter and harder to swat away.
> 
> ## The clean legal split
> 
> ### Against the **court / sheriff**
> You can say:
> - Title II and due process required meaningful access and constitutionally adequate notice. citeturn374560search1turn374560search6
> 
> ### Against the **bank**
> The stronger line is:
> - the bank’s prior knowledge of your barriers is evidence that its chosen notice method was **not reasonably calculated under all the circumstances** under *Mullane*
> - and, if you want an ADA-adjacent frame, effective-communication / reasonable-modification principles make its refusal to use clearer written/email communication look worse, not better. citeturn374560search0turn374560search1
> 
> That is different from claiming the bank directly owed a freestanding constitutional interactive-process duty.
> 
> ## On mortgage-loss mitigation / “they had to try to help”
> Here too, be precise.
> 
> ### What you can say
> If FCFCU was acting as the **servicer**, Regulation X imposes real loss-mitigation procedures once a borrower submits a qualifying application, but it **does not require the servicer to offer any specific option**. The rule is explicit on that point. citeturn374560search4turn374560search5
> 
> So the good version is:
> 
> > If Plaintiff or its servicer received a complete loss-mitigation application in time to trigger Regulation X, then federal servicing rules required review procedures, continuity-of-contact type obligations, and specified response duties, even though they did not require any particular workout outcome. citeturn374560search4turn374560search5
> 
> ### What you should not say
> Do **not** say:
> > NCUA regulations gave me a private right to a foreclosure workout.
> 
> That is not the clean law.
> 
> ### What NCUA guidance does help with
> NCUA guidance and official statements do support the proposition that prudent loan modifications / workouts for borrowers in hardship are often in the best interest of both the borrower and the credit union. But that is **supervisory guidance / policy support**, not a private cause of action by itself. citeturn406682search0turn406682search9
> 
> So use NCUA this way:
> 
> > NCUA guidance reinforces that prudent loan modifications and workouts for financially distressed borrowers are often in the long-term interest of both borrower and credit union, which makes Plaintiff’s alleged refusal even to meaningfully engage hardship communications more probative as to reasonableness and servicing posture. citeturn406682search0turn406682search9
> 
> That is safe.
> 
> ## Where your online-payment point fits
> This is a good fact if you can prove it:
> 
> > Plaintiff allegedly restricted online payments as a privilege for “on-time” clients while simultaneously making phone and in-person payment routes more burdensome for a borrower who had already explained that phone communication was difficult because of disability-related barriers.
> 
> That helps:
> - servicing friction
> - reasonableness
> - credibility
> - the story that they made payment harder while later blaming you for not paying
> 
> It is not, by itself, the main constitutional hook. It is a **bad-facts / reasonableness / mitigation** hook.
> 
> ## The strongest final framing
> Your best theory is not:
> 
> > they violated my civil rights because they did not formally do an ADA interactive process.
> 
> Your best theory is:
> 
> 1. **They knew the circumstances.**  
> 2. **They did not use that knowledge to communicate in a way reasonably calculated to reach you.**  
> 3. **They did not disclose those circumstances to the court-enforcement process.**  
> 4. **They made payment and communication harder, not easier.**  
> 5. **They then used the thinnest notice path, default, a generic “Unknown Occupants” writ theory, and shifting math to force the result.**
> 
> That is much stronger.
> 
> ## What I would add to the motion
> A line like this:
> 
> > Defendant does not contend that Plaintiff owed him a freestanding constitutional duty as a state actor. Defendant does contend that Plaintiff’s documented knowledge of hardship and disability-related communication barriers was materially relevant to the *Mullane* inquiry, and that Plaintiff’s refusal to communicate through the workable channels Defendant identified, combined with Plaintiff’s failure to disclose those circumstances before default and enforcement, renders the resulting notice record materially less trustworthy and less constitutionally adequate under all the circumstances.
> 
> That protects you from overclaiming while still hitting hard.
> 
> ## Bottom line
> Your **facts** are strong.  
> Your **legal framing** needs discipline.
> 
> Use:
> - **Mullane**
> - **Title II against the court/sheriff**
> - **effective-communication principles as context**
> - **Regulation X if the loss-mitigation facts fit**
> - **NCUA guidance as policy support, not an enforceable standalone right**
> 
> That is the version that lands.

### Assistant
> Yes. The court-ready version is **not** “they treated me like a criminal.”  
> The court-ready version is:
> 
> ## What these new video facts support
> 
> They support a pattern of **improvised field execution, prior-knowledge leakage, unnecessary escalation, and post-removal perimeter control**.
> 
> More specifically, if the videos verify what you described, they support these points:
> 
> ### 1. No true emergency, no orderly execution
> If they are casually saying it “went really well,” discussing vacations, billing hours, and meeting each other for the first time after the removal, that cuts against any suggestion that this was a carefully structured, necessity-driven enforcement process. It sounds operational, casual, and convenience-driven.
> 
> ### 2. They were working from more personal knowledge than they admitted
> If Ms. Abbott referenced your husband’s deportation, that supports the inference that Plaintiff had in fact absorbed the contents of your hardship communications. That matters because it reinforces your argument that Plaintiff knew specific hardship facts and communication barriers, but did not translate that knowledge into a structured, disability-responsive notice process.
> 
> ### 3. They were dealing with known people, not “unknown occupants”
> If they discussed another occupant being at work, discussed your neighbors, and discussed trespass instructions for specific people who might return, that undercuts the generic “Unknown Occupants” framing. It supports the inference that the occupancy situation was more specifically known than the writ papers suggest.
> 
> ### 4. The field process was reactive and improvised
> If they were discussing whether the paperwork was there, whether your name was on it, whether neighbors had been told to call, whether signs needed to be posted, and who should be contacted if someone came back, that supports the argument that they were making key access and enforcement decisions in real time instead of carrying out a cleanly preplanned, clearly communicated transition process.
> 
> ### 5. Perimeter and reputation control went beyond simple possession
> If Ms. Abbott says she usually talks to neighbors “to let them know what’s going on” or about “danger,” that raises a separate issue: not just possession of the property, but narrative management and social perimeter control. You do not need to call that defamatory or improper yet. The safer point is that Plaintiff’s agents were discussing your personal circumstances with third parties in ways not obviously necessary to execute possession.
> 
> ### 6. The trespass issue may expose a contradiction
> Your instinct is right. If they had not actually identified everyone, yet were discussing that “you all” had been trespassed and neighbors should call if you returned, that suggests they were not really dealing with unknowns at all. It suggests a broader effort to exclude specific people from the property and area without a clearly documented individualized process.
> 
> ## What not to say
> 
> Do **not** say:
> - they “adjudicated” your case on the lawn
> - they “conspired”
> - they “treated me like a criminal”  
> even if that is how it felt
> 
> Say instead:
> - they engaged in **on-scene operational discussion**
> - they made **ad hoc access and enforcement decisions in real time**
> - they demonstrated **knowledge of personal hardship facts**
> - they treated the occupancy situation as more specifically known than their generic writ language suggests
> - they implemented **post-removal access-control measures** through neighbors, signage, and trespass instructions
> 
> That lands better.
> 
> ## Best place for this in the motion
> 
> This belongs mainly in **Section III.E**, as support for:
> - no meaningful pre-removal notice
> - disorganized, convenience-driven execution
> - known occupants versus “Unknown Occupants”
> - real-time decision-making rather than a clean prior notice sequence
> 
> A smaller part can go into **Section V**, but only as support for practical harm and overreach.
> 
> ## Court-ready draft language
> 
> Use this as a working insert:
> 
> > Defendant further contends that contemporaneous video from the December 9, 2025 removal event reflects an improvised and operationally ad hoc execution process inconsistent with any claim of a fully orderly, previously communicated possession transition. According to Defendant, on-scene participants, including a sheriff’s deputy, an off-duty security person, and Plaintiff’s representative Ms. Abbott, are heard discussing whether the operative paperwork was present, whether Defendant’s name appeared on it, and whether Defendant had only then received it. If authenticated, Defendant relies on that exchange as evidence that the on-scene participants were focused not on a clearly completed prior notice sequence, but on whether operative paperwork had only then been placed in Defendant’s hands.
> >
> > Defendant further contends that related video reflects Ms. Abbott discussing personal hardship facts, including that Defendant’s husband had been deported, and discussing the presence of another occupant who was then away at work. In Defendant’s view, those exchanges support the inference that Plaintiff had in fact absorbed substantive information from Defendant’s hardship communications while still failing to translate that knowledge into a structured, disability-responsive notice process. Defendant further alleges that the participants discussed post-removal access-control measures, including signage, neighbor notification, and instructions concerning what should occur if an occupant returned. If authenticated, Defendant relies on this evidence not to establish every motive of the participants, but to show that Plaintiff and its agents were making ad hoc possession, access, and perimeter-control decisions in real time, with knowledge of specific personal circumstances and additional occupants, rather than executing a cleanly documented surrender-and-transition process previously communicated to those affected.
> 
> ## My advice on the neighbor piece
> 
> Keep it, but frame it narrowly.
> 
> Not:
> > why is it my neighbors’ business
> 
> Instead:
> > Defendant further contends that Plaintiff’s agents discussed involving neighbors in post-removal monitoring or notification, which reinforces the impression that post-removal perimeter control was being improvised in the field rather than carried out through a clearly documented and individualized legal process.
> 
> That is stronger.
> 
> ## What to verify before it goes in
> 
> For each clip, confirm:
> - exact timestamp
> - who is speaking
> - exact wording
> - whether the audio clearly supports the inference
> 
> Then we can convert each into a proper exhibit excerpt.
> 
> The useful themes here are:
> **knowledge, improvisation, known occupants, perimeter control, and convenience-driven execution.**
> 
> That is how this becomes court-ready.

> - burst pipe / broken glass / emergency sound alerts
> 
> then your strongest point is:
> 
> > by disconnecting that system, Plaintiff’s agents did not remove a decorative or incidental setup; they disabled a live monitoring and emergency-alert infrastructure tied to property safety, preservation, and documentation.
> 
> That is a serious point.
> 
> ### 4. Foreseeability of later damage
> This is where the argument sharpens.
> 
> Do **not** say:
> > damage was guaranteed
> 
> Say:
> > loss of monitoring made damage or undetected problems substantially more foreseeable
> 
> That is stronger and more defensible.
> 
> Court-ready version:
> 
> > Defendant contends that once Plaintiff’s agents disconnected the communications and backup-router infrastructure that supported camera feeds, environmental monitoring, sump-pump alerts, smoke or oxygen-related alerts, and emergency-notification functions, the property was left materially less protected against exactly the kinds of deterioration, interruption, or unnoticed events those systems were designed to detect. In Defendant’s view, later loss or damage was not random in that setting; it became substantially more foreseeable once that monitoring infrastructure was disabled.
> 
> That lands.
> 
> ## What not to emphasize
> I would **not** make the main point:
> - she laughed
> - she mentioned your Tesla
> - she was snarky
> 
> That’s background.
> 
> I **would** emphasize:
> - she formed and expressed financial assumptions unsupported by the actual arrangement
> - she was evaluating your belongings while controlling the property
> - she disabled protective infrastructure
> - she did so while the bank was taking possession and before any orderly retrieval/preservation process existed
> 
> ## Best place in the motion
> This belongs mostly in:
> 
> ### Section III.E
> for:
> - Ms. Abbott’s on-scene conduct
> - prejudgment assumptions
> - property/control behavior
> - communications/safety infrastructure disruption
> 
> ### Section V.C
> for:
> - litigation impairment
> - communications impairment
> - foreseeable property harm
> - loss of monitoring and safety functions
> 
> ## Court-ready draft language
> Use this as a working paragraph:
> 
> > Defendant further contends that contemporaneous video reflects Plaintiff’s representative making informal assumptions about Defendant’s finances and household structure based on outward appearances, including the existence of a vehicle and the presence of other occupants, while disregarding the actual instability of Defendant’s circumstances. According to Defendant, those assumptions materially oversimplified the reality that prior occupancy arrangements had been tied to a failing business structure, that one later occupant had only recently moved in, and that the household did not in fact reflect stable shared income sufficient to cure the mortgage default. Defendant relies on this not to litigate tone or personality, but to show that Plaintiff’s on-scene conduct was shaped by an inaccurate narrative of Defendant’s finances and hardship.
> >
> > Defendant further alleges that the same representative characterized Defendant’s communications and monitoring setup as a hazard while disabling internet, backup-router, and related connected systems. According to Defendant, those systems supported not merely ordinary internet access, but camera connectivity, environmental and emergency monitoring, and alerts relating to smoke, oxygen, water intrusion, sump conditions, broken glass, burst pipes, and other urgent conditions. Defendant contends that once those systems were disabled, the home and its contents were left materially less protected and less observable, making later loss, deterioration, or unnoticed damage substantially more foreseeable.
> 
> ## What would make this strong as evidence
> If you have them, these are gold:
> 
> - photos of the setup before removal
> - app screenshots showing the sensors / devices / alerts
> - receipts or product pages for the systems
> - video of her tracing and disconnecting lines
> - any later evidence of damage or unmonitored failure
> - T-Mobile / router replacement notices
> - notes showing what each device monitored
> 
> That would turn this from a story into a documented chain.
> 
> ## My blunt read
> Her comments about your Tesla and roommates help show **prejudgment**.  
> But the real legal leverage is the **disabling of the monitoring infrastructure** while the bank took control of the property.
> 
> That is the part to press.

### Assistant
> I’m pulling the Indiana primary sources so we can compare your facts against a clean, court-defensible baseline instead of just saying “this felt wrong.” Then I’ll fold the new camera facts into the strongest lane without overclaiming.

### Assistant
> I’ve got enough from the Indiana sources to give you a real baseline: what a clean foreclosure-to-possession sequence should look like versus what your record shows. I’m also adding the timing detail that the camera/monitoring system was disconnected hours before they fully left, plus the unidentified man and the missed battery camera.

### Assistant
> Yes. Here is the clean **comparison standard** you can use.
> 
> Not “what perfect kindness looks like.”  
> What a **legally and operationally clean Indiana foreclosure-to-possession process** should look like, compared against your facts.
> 
> ## A. What a clean Indiana process should look like
> 
> ### 1. Service of the foreclosure complaint
> If Plaintiff uses **copy service** at a residence, Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B) required, in 2025, that the person making service **also send** a first-class mailed copy, and that fact had to be shown on the return. That is the baseline for residential copy service in your timeframe. citeturn353530search6
> 
> A clean process therefore looks like:
> - residential service attempt
> - required follow-up mailing
> - return showing that mailing
> - and, if the plaintiff knows something unusual about notice reliability, no casual shrug about it
> 
> ### 2. If the usual method may not reliably reach the person
> Indiana Trial Rule 4.14 allows the court to authorize another method of service when the usual methods are impracticable, so long as the order uses a method **reasonably calculated to give actual knowledge**. citeturn353530search2
> 
> A clean process therefore looks like:
> - if the plaintiff knows the normal method may fail,
> - it either uses a more reliable method itself,
> - or asks the court for a special-order method,
> - instead of quietly proceeding with the weakest method and hoping default happens
> 
> ### 3. Constitutional notice standard
> Under *Mullane*, notice must be **“reasonably calculated, under all the circumstances”** to apprise the person and allow objections. That is still the constitutional backbone. Your current motion already uses that correctly. The ADA Title II materials reinforce that courts and sheriffs, as state/local government actors, must provide meaningful access and effective communication to people with disabilities. citeturn353530search0turn353530search1
> 
> A clean process therefore looks like:
> - notice method chosen with the actual circumstances in mind
> - not a generic box-check if the actor already knows the person has communication barriers
> 
> ### 4. Possession enforcement after foreclosure
> Indiana Trial Rule 70 provides the mechanism for delivery of possession through writ process. In plain English: once the court has entered the right judgment, the **clerk can issue** the writ of assistance and the sheriff can deliver possession. citeturn353530search2
> 
> A clean process therefore looks like:
> - clear underlying judgment/decree
> - clean writ issuance
> - clear identification of who is being dispossessed
> - a documented transition from ownership papers to actual possession enforcement
> - not generic occupancy language used as a shortcut for a known person
> 
> ### 5. Personal property handling
> Indiana’s landlord property-removal statute is not a perfect one-to-one foreclosure rule, but it is still useful as a benchmark because it shows Indiana law recognizes a difference between taking possession of real estate and immediately tossing personal property. Under Indiana Code 32-31-4-2, once possession is awarded, the landlord may seek a court order allowing removal of personal property, and if the tenant does not remove it by the ordered date, it may then be removed to a warehouseman or court-approved storage. citeturn353530search3turn353530search7
> 
> So the **ethical/legal benchmark** looks like:
> - clear notice of possession status
> - clear date or procedure for removal
> - inventory or documented handling
> - structured access
> - storage or at least a documented custody process
> - not ad hoc dumping and field improvisation
> 
> ### 6. Effective communication by public actors
> The DOJ’s ADA guidance says covered entities must communicate effectively and consider the **nature, length, complexity, and context** of the communication and the person’s normal communication methods. citeturn353530search1turn353530search8
> 
> A clean public-actor process therefore looks like:
> - not just “we handed him a paper”
> - but communication that works in context for that person
> 
> ---
> 
> ## B. What your record looks like by comparison
> 
> Using that benchmark, your theory becomes much cleaner:
> 
> ### Instead of a clean service sequence:
> You have a disputed copy-service record, no clearly corroborated mailing proof beyond notation, and prior hardship/communication facts that you say were never surfaced to the court-enforcement process.
> 
> ### Instead of a clean possession transition:
> You have a known defendant later converted into **“Unknown Occupants”**, no clear individualized demand-and-refusal record, and no meaningful advance notice of the actual December 9 execution date.
> 
> ### Instead of orderly property handling:
> You have a compressed retrieval window, no inventory, no clear coordinator, active disposal, and disputed damage.
> 
> ### Instead of preserving communications/safety infrastructure while the bank controlled the scene:
> You now have an additional fact pattern that I added to the draft:
> - Ms. Abbott allegedly traced and disconnected cameras/router/internet lines
> - that disabling occurred **hours before** Plaintiff’s agents fully vacated the home
> - another unidentified man was seen leaving the house during that period
> - at least one **battery-powered front-door camera** remained active and still recorded
> - which supports the inference that parts of the monitoring system were selectively disabled while people continued moving through and controlling the premises
> 
> That is now in canvas.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## C. The strongest comparison language for the motion
> 
> This is the frame I’d use, either as a paragraph in the argument or as a timeline/issue chart:
> 
> > A clean foreclosure-to-possession process in Indiana would reflect a reliable service record, a notice method reasonably calculated under the actual circumstances, a documented and individualized transition from judgment to possession, and a structured procedure for handling any remaining personal property. Defendant contends the present record reflects the opposite: disputed copy-service adequacy, a generic “Unknown Occupants” writ theory for a known defendant, no meaningful advance notice of the actual lockout date, improvised field execution, and unstructured control and disposal of personal property. To the extent public actors participated in that process, Defendant further contends the record reflects no meaningful effort to ensure effective communication under the circumstances actually known.
> 
> That is a powerful compare-and-contrast.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## D. What I changed in canvas just now
> 
> I added the new monitoring/camera detail to the post-removal handling paragraph:
> 
> - camera/router/internet disabling occurred **hours before** the agents fully left
> - another **unidentified man** was seen leaving the house
> - at least one **battery-powered front-door camera** kept recording
> - this supports the inference of **selective disabling** of monitoring infrastructure while the premises were still under active control
> 
> That is now part of the draft.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## E. My advice on using this comparison in court
> Do **not** title it “what ethical foreclosure should look like.” That sounds preachy.
> 
> Title it something like:
> - **Benchmark for a Reliable Possession Transition**
> - **What a Documented Possession Process Ordinarily Requires**
> - **Contrast Between a Structured Possession Process and the Present Record**
> 
> That sounds judicial instead of emotional.
> 
> If you want, next I’ll turn this into a **two-column compare chart** for the motion or exhibits:
> **Clean Process** vs **This Record**.

### User
> hey i sent them a litigation hold and my property was in their posession and they dispose of it in a land fill so didnt they kinda mess up

### Assistant
> I’m checking the preservation / spoliation standards so I can tell you exactly what lane this is, not just “yeah that sounds bad.” The answer is probably yes, but the legal label matters.

### Assistant
> Yes. Potentially **in a serious way**.
> 
> This is not just “they were rude” or “they made cleanup decisions.”  
> If your property was in their control, you sent preservation / litigation-hold notices, and they still disposed of it in a landfill, that creates at least **three real lanes**:
> 
> ### 1. Spoliation / failure to preserve evidence
> Indiana’s current spoliation standard is that a party to pending or reasonably foreseeable litigation has a duty to preserve evidence in its control that it knows, or reasonably should know, is discoverable. Spoliation means destroying, materially altering, or concealing evidence that should have been preserved. citeturn455275search3
> 
> Your record helps you here because:
> - on **February 3, 2026**, you sent FCFCU and related recipients a **second notice of immediate preservation of evidence and litigation hold**, expressly demanding preservation and warning that failure to comply could support findings of spoliation and adverse inference. fileciteturn28file0
> - on **February 12, 2026**, you again sent a formal demand directing FCFCU, its agents, and counsel to preserve relevant documents and ESI without alteration, deletion, or modification. fileciteturn28file3 fileciteturn28file13
> - then on **March 26, 2026**, Plaintiff filed that the house had been cleaned, **all property had been removed**, and it was **“no longer available.”** fileciteturn28file1
> 
> That is not a good record for them.
> 
> ### 2. Disposal of property while it was in their possession
> Separate from evidence preservation, this is also a **property-control problem**.
> 
> Your current theory is already the right one: once they took control of the premises while your belongings remained inside, they were not just bystanders anymore. They were exercising control over property that was still yours. If they then disposed of it without inventory, without structured retrieval, and after preservation notice, that supports arguments sounding in:
> - wrongful disposal / conversion-type conduct
> - failure to preserve property under their control
> - and stronger sanctions/show-cause relief
> 
> Indiana Trial Rule 70 also matters here. If a judgment/order directs possession or a specific act, the court can use writs, contempt, and even damages for disobedience of the order. citeturn429869view0  
> So if any order or court-directed preservation obligation was in play, their position gets worse.
> 
> ### 3. ESI / device-preservation exposure
> This is especially true for:
> - cameras
> - routers
> - monitoring devices
> - logs
> - recordings
> - backup systems
> 
> Indiana Trial Rule 37(D) now expressly allows sanctions when relevant ESI that should have been preserved is lost because a party failed to take reasonable preservation steps, cannot be restored or replaced, and prejudice results or there was intent to deprive. Available sanctions can include presumptions, adverse inferences, dismissal/default, or other just orders. citeturn429869view1
> 
> So if they:
> - disconnected cameras,
> - disposed of router/internet equipment,
> - and thereby wiped out recordings/logs/alerts or made them unavailable,
> 
> that is not just a “stuff” problem. It is also potentially an **ESI preservation** problem.
> 
> ## The honest limit
> A litigation hold does **not automatically mean** they were forbidden from touching anything at all.  
> But it **does** mean they were on notice that they had to preserve relevant evidence and relevant property in their control. And once they later told the court all property had been removed and was no longer available, after those notices, they created a real preservation issue. fileciteturn28file0 fileciteturn28file1
> 
> ## The clean way to say it
> Use this framing:
> 
> > Plaintiff’s position materially worsened once Defendant served formal preservation and litigation-hold notices and Plaintiff nevertheless proceeded to remove and dispose of property in its control. At that point, the issue ceased to be mere cleanup or possession. It became a preservation problem, an evidence problem, and a property-handling problem.
> 
> ## What this strengthens in your motion
> This supports, at minimum:
> 
> - the **show-cause / sanctions** request
> - the **property-preservation / wrongful disposal** lane
> - the **communications / router / camera / ESI** lane
> - the argument that Plaintiff’s post-judgment conduct is not trustworthy and may justify adverse inferences
> 
> ## My blunt read
> So yes — **they may have seriously messed up**.
> 
> Especially because your record already shows:
> - formal hold notice,
> - repeated preservation demands,
> - and then their own filing saying everything was removed and no longer available. fileciteturn28file0 fileciteturn28file3 fileciteturn28file1
> 
> That is exactly the kind of sequence courts look at when deciding whether sanctions, adverse inference, or other corrective relief should be on the table.
> 
> The next smart move is to build a short **preservation timeline exhibit**:
> - date of hold notice
> - date of second hold notice
> - date of property-preservation motions
> - date of cleanup / disposal
> - Plaintiff’s filing admitting property is no longer available
> 
> That would make this much harder for them to shrug off.

At that point, the issue ceased to be simple cleanup. It became a preservation issue, a property-control issue, and a potential sanctions issue.

> anything in here we should add. Your 10_docket tree is outstanding — it is already perfectly structured for appeal.
> You have every single docket entry, the original PDF scans from IEFS, and clean .md summaries with exact filenames. This is exactly what the Indiana Court of Appeals expects in an Appendix under Appellate Rules 50 and 51 (current as of 2026). No gaps, no duplicates, chronological by event, and everything is source-native from the clerk’s system. Most lawyers don’t organize this cleanly.
> When the judge eventually rules on your April 9, 2026 Motion to Vacate (and/or the Rule-to-Show-Cause motion), you will simply add one new folder and compile the Appendix in under an hour. Here is exactly how to turn this tree into a compliant, ready-to-file Appendix.
> 1. Quick Prep Steps (Do These Before the Denial Order Arrives)
> 
> Add today’s filings (right now, while the tree is fresh):
> Create new folder:
> 2026-04-09_00_motion_to_vacate_default_judgment
> Inside it, place:
> Your filed Motion to Vacate PDF (the full document you just e-filed)
> All exhibits you attached (numbered as Ex. A, Ex. B, etc.)
> A new index.md with a one-line summary
> 
> Do the same for the Rule-to-Show-Cause motion if filed separately: 2026-04-09_01_motion_rule_to_show_cause
> 
> Create an “Appendix” master folder next to 10_docket (e.g., 11_appendix_for_appeal).
> Decide on volumes (Rule 51(D)):
> Volume 1 = Table of Contents only (stand-alone).
> Volumes 2+ = the actual documents (max 250 pages or 50 MB per volume).
> You will probably need 3–5 volumes total (your key docs are not huge).
> 
> Pagination (mandatory):
> Each volume starts at page 1 at the bottom center.
> Use free tools: Adobe Acrobat (add header/footer), Smallpdf.com, or ILovePDF (batch “Number Pages”).
> Do not change the original PDFs — just overlay the new page numbers.
> 
> Verification page (last page of the final volume):
> Copy this exactly (Rule 50(A)(2)(i) and pro se handbook):VERIFICATION OF ACCURACY
> I, Cody Rice-Velasquez, verify under penalties of perjury that the documents contained in this Appendix are accurate copies of parts of the Record on Appeal in Marion Superior Court Cause No. 49D03-2501-MF-002559.
> /s/ Cody Rice-Velasquez
> Cody Rice-Velasquez, Pro Se
> Date: [insert date]
>

> I. Strategic Case Overview and Core Facts
> The litigation initiated by Financial Center First Credit Union (FCFCU) has shifted from a standard residential foreclosure into a high-stakes dispute characterized by systemic procedural failures, constitutional due process violations, and extreme accounting volatility. What began as a routine enforcement action has devolved into a strategic indictment of the Plaintiff's conduct, involving the intersection of disability rights under the Americans with Disabilities Act (ADA), regulatory non-compliance with NCUA standards, and a series of forensic accounting discrepancies that render the initial judgment a jurisdictional void.
> Case Identity Table
> Field
> Details
> Cause No.

> Defendant

> Key Legal Filings
