> Chronology of Enforcement and Dispossession
> 
> January 2025: FCFCU initiates foreclosure proceedings.
> March 5, 2025: First disputed residential copy service attempt.
> April 15, 2025: Second disputed residential copy service attempt; Defendant asserts no summons/complaint were received via mail.
> May 28, 2025: Court enters Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure.
> August 15, 2025: Sheriff’s Sale occurs; FCFCU enters a credit bid of $240,464.20.
> November 6, 2025: Writ of Assistance issued against "Unknown Occupants."
> December 3, 2025: Sheriff’s Deed recorded, accelerating possession just six days prior to removal.
> December 9, 2025: Execution of Writ; Defendant, co-habitants Zai and David, and dog Gigi are displaced into freezing conditions.
> February 24, 2026: Plaintiff admits to a "scrivener’s error" in interest calculations via nunc pro tunc petition.
> March 25, 2026: Property disposal event; Defendant observes an active disposal operation involving a dump truck.
> 
> The initial default judgment serves as an unstable foundation for every subsequent enforcement action. By obtaining a judgment through the least reliable service method and supporting it with an accounting record now admitted to be inaccurate, the Plaintiff has rendered the entire enforcement sequence—from sale to physical dispossession—legally precarious.
> II. Analysis of Procedural and Jurisdictional Defects
> Valid service of process is the non-negotiable prerequisite for personal jurisdiction; its absence renders a judgment void ab initio. The record in this matter demonstrates a calculated decision by the Plaintiff to utilize the most minimal service methods available, which, when combined with strategic naming substitutions, created a total failure of notice.
> Trial Rule 4.1(B) Violation: The Jurisdictional Void
> Under Indiana Trial Rule 4.1(B), residential copy service is only valid if a second copy is sent via first-class mail. The Plaintiff’s reliance on shorthand notations in the Sheriff’s return is a fatal defect.
> 
> Absence of Corroboration: The record contains no independent proof of dispatch—such as a certificate of mailing or affidavit—to substitute for the bare return notation.
> Disparate Standards: Plaintiff demonstrated it understood the necessity of reliable service by utilizing formal, documented service for corporate co-defendants (American Express and JP Morgan) in April 2025. The decision to then use "bare residential copy service" for an individual known to have communication barriers suggests a pretextual effort to secure a default.
> 
> The "Unknown Occupants" Substitution
> In a strategic maneuver to bypass individualized notice, the Plaintiff shifted from naming "Cody Rice-Velasquez" to the generic "Unknown Occupants" in the Writ of Assistance.
> 
> The "So What?": This was a procedural shortcut designed to avoid presenting the Court with a documented "demand for surrender" specifically directed to the Defendant. By converting a known defendant into an anonymous "occupancy problem," the Plaintiff accelerated dispossession while stripping the Defendant of the transition procedures inherent in his status as a named party.
> 
> III. Quantitative Forensic Analysis: Accounting Instability and Surplus
> Financial accuracy is the heartbeat of judicial bidding; "scrivener’s errors" in sworn filings are not merely mistakes—they are judicial admissions of unreliability.
> Comparative Accounting Table

> The "Missing Ledger" Defect
> The Plaintiff’s failure to produce the underlying loan servicing payoff ledger is a critical forensic failure. Without this foundational record, the Plaintiff's nunc pro tunc filing is a unilateral assertion rather than a verified correction. The Court cannot mathematically verify interest calculations or total debt satisfaction without the ledger that tracks every debit and credit throughout the loan's history.
> Shifting Judicial Admissions
> The Plaintiff’s accounting is defined by three critical takeaways:
> 
> Sworn Volatility: Plaintiff initially swore the bid produced no surplus, only to later admit to a nearly $10,000 interest miscalculation.
> Unilateral Recalculation: Plaintiff attempted to retroactively shrink its bid to eliminate a surplus that its own revised math established.
> Material Inconsistency: The $8,875.81 in "Added Costs" from the August 6, 2025 worksheet was never reconciled with the judgment or subsequent filings, proving a broad pattern of accounting instability.
> 
> This volatility has caused ongoing harm to the Defendant’s consumer credit reporting, as the Plaintiff continues to report unverified and inconsistent balances to national agencies.
> IV. Evaluation of Enforcement Misconduct and Property Disposal
> A Plaintiff assumes a strict duty of care when taking possession of real estate containing personal property. The "unstructured" disposal observed here constitutes a significant enforcement liability.
> Critique of the March 25, 2026 Retrieval Event
> The retrieval event was an "active disposal operation" rather than a coordinated recovery.
> 
> The Dump Truck: Defendant arrived to find personal belongings being loaded directly into a "substantially filled" dump truck. The truck "departed and returned" during the window, indicating a high-volume disposal rather than an orderly retrieval.
> Pretextual Access: Plaintiff imposed a strict 2-hour window (9:00 AM – 11:00 AM), yet representatives remained on-site for several additional hours. This suggests the window was a pretextual limit rather than an operational necessity.
> 
> Interference with Evidence: Disabling the Digital Eye
> Evidence demonstrates an intentional effort by Plaintiff representative Ms. Abbott to impair evidence preservation.
> 
> Selective Disabling: Ms. Abbott disabled security cameras and removed internet/router equipment hours before vacating the home.
> The "So What?": By remaining on-site for hours after disabling the "digital eye," the Plaintiff’s agents ensured a period of unmonitored control. This conduct prevented the Defendant from monitoring the handling of his property and destroyed the digital record of the removal process.
> 
> V. Assessment of Plaintiff Exposures and Regulatory Risk
> ADA and "Meaningful Access" Exposure
> The Defendant has documented cognitive disabilities (PTSD, ADHD, executive dysfunction). The Plaintiff had pre-suit knowledge of these conditions via 2023-2024 outreach.
> 
> Regulatory Red Flag: As an NCUA-regulated Credit Union, the Plaintiff is subject to the "Prudent CRE Loan Accommodations" policy. The failure to perform a "comprehensive review of a borrower’s financial condition" before resorting to a "minimalist" and inaccessible foreclosure process constitutes a significant regulatory violation. Proceeding with unstructured notice for a defendant with known processing delays is a failure to provide "meaningful access."
> 
> Collateral Damages and Prejudice
> 
> Economic Injury: Loss of the Indianapolis client network and total collapse of rideshare income.
> Survival-Based Vulnerability: Displacement into freezing conditions without transition notice created emergency conditions leading to "coercive sexual pressure" from third parties.
> Legal Prejudice: The communication instability and loss of internet equipment directly caused the closure of Zai’s federal asylum case.
> 
> VI. Conclusion and Requested Remedies
> The systemic procedural and mathematical failures identified herein necessitate the vacatur of the judgment to restore constitutional fairness.
> Remedies Checklist
> 
> Vacatur of the May 28, 2025 Judgment.
> Quashing of the Writ of Assistance.
> Immediate Deposit of $10,701.51 in surplus funds with the Clerk.
> Credit Reporting Relief: Immediate temporary deletion of mortgage tradelines.
> Structured Retrieval Order: A court-mandated, disability-responsive process including a full inventory.
> Mandatory Disclosure: Requirement for the Plaintiff to identify the specific legal authority used for the chosen property disposal method.
> 
> The "Broad Pattern of Procedural Instability" documented throughout this analysis demonstrates a Plaintiff that prioritized enforcement speed over jurisdictional and regulatory integrity. Only comprehensive vacatur can correct this material prejudice.This analysis is excellent — sharp, evidence-based, and strategically layered. It transforms your April 9, 2026 Motion to Vacate from a strong procedural filing into a comprehensive indictment of the entire case. It does not create a private right of action, but it powerfully supports your existing claims of bad-faith notice, prejudice, and failure to provide meaningful access under Mullane + ADA Title II.

> VI. Conclusion and Requested Remedies
> The systemic procedural, jurisdictional, and accounting defects documented above — including the void judgment under Trial Rule 4.1(B), constitutionally inadequate notice under Mullane, Plaintiff’s own admitted accounting volatility, and ongoing enforcement misconduct — compel immediate and ultimate relief.

> A. Immediate Coercive Relief (Enforceable Within 7 Days)
> 
> Enforcement of the March 2, 2026 Surplus Order
> Pursuant to the Court’s order of March 2, 2026 (Docket Folder 2026-03-02), and in light of Plaintiff’s continued non-compliance, the Court is respectfully requested to ORDER Plaintiff to pay the full surplus amount of $10,701.51 (or the precise amount confirmed by the Court’s records) directly to Defendant or into the Clerk’s registry for immediate release to Defendant within 7 calendar days of this order. In the alternative, the Court is requested to issue a Rule to Show Cause directing Plaintiff and its counsel to appear and show cause why they should not be held in civil contempt for violating the March 2, 2026 order.
> Immediate Preservation of Remaining Personal Property
> Pursuant to Trial Rule 70 and the Court’s inherent authority to prevent irreparable harm, the Court is respectfully requested to ORDER Plaintiff, its agents, contractors, and representatives to (a) cease any further removal, disposal, transfer, or destruction of Defendant’s personal property, and (b) maintain the current location and condition of all remaining items until further order of the Court or until a structured retrieval process is completed.
> Structured, Disability-Responsive Retrieval Process
> The Court is respectfully requested to ORDER a court-supervised property retrieval process that includes: (i) a written inventory prepared by Plaintiff within 14 days, (ii) a minimum 8-hour retrieval window on a date convenient to Defendant, (iii) presence of a neutral third-party coordinator, and (iv) disability accommodations consistent with Defendant’s documented impairments (PTSD, ADHD, executive dysfunction).
> 
> B. Ultimate Relief on the Merits (Vacatur and Restoration)
> 
> Vacatur of the Default Judgment
> The Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure entered May 28, 2025 (Docket Folder 2025-05-28) is vacated as void under Indiana Trial Rule 60(B)(6) for lack of personal jurisdiction, or in the alternative set aside under Trial Rule 60(B)(1) for excusable neglect and substantial meritorious defense.
> Unwinding of Derivative Enforcement Actions
> The Court is respectfully requested to ORDER that the following be set aside to the extent they depend upon the vacated judgment:
> The Sheriff’s Sale held August 15, 2025;
> The Sheriff’s Deed recorded December 3, 2025;
> The Writ of Assistance issued November 6, 2025 and executed December 9, 2025.
> 
> Credit-Reporting Relief
> The Court is respectfully requested to ORDER Plaintiff to (a) immediately request temporary deletion of all mortgage-related tradelines from Defendant’s credit reports at Equifax, Experian, and TransUnion, and (b) refrain from any further adverse reporting until final resolution of this matter.
> Mandatory Disclosure of Authority
> If Plaintiff disputes Defendant’s allegations regarding the March 25, 2026 property-disposal method, the Court is respectfully requested to ORDER Plaintiff to file, within 14 days, a verified statement identifying the specific court order, statute, rule, or other legal authority that purportedly authorized the compressed 2-hour window, monitored access, lack of inventory, and dump-truck disposal process.
>

### Assistant
> Yes, but not with the fake-diamond line. That’s too theatrical and gives them an excuse to dismiss you as dramatic.
> 
> Use something like this instead:
> 
> > The defects in this record are not isolated. They are cumulative, recurring, and mutually reinforcing. The service record is unstable, the notice sequence is incomplete, the possession process was improvised, the accounting figures shifted materially, and later filings introduced additional inconsistencies rather than resolving them. Taken together, these defects make the record unusually difficult to reconcile and materially undermine confidence in the validity of both the judgment and its enforcement. At some point, the issue ceases to be whether any single irregularity can be explained away in isolation. The issue becomes whether the Court can continue to rely on a process so marked by inconsistency, omission, and post hoc correction. Defendant respectfully submits that it cannot.
> 
> A slightly sharper version:
> 
> > This case is no longer marked by one disputed mailing, one accounting error, or one post-judgment disagreement. It is marked by an accumulation of discrepancies, omissions, shifting explanations, and procedural shortcuts that, taken together, render the record materially unreliable. The Court is not required to treat a pattern of instability as though it were ordinary process merely because each defect, viewed in isolation, can be minimized. At some point, the cumulative weight of the inconsistencies becomes the issue. Defendant respectfully submits that this record has reached that point.
> 
> And the clean one-line closer:
> 
> > The cumulative instability of this record is itself now a material fact.
> 
> That’s the legal version of what you’re trying to say. It hits harder because it sounds judicial, not wounded.

> - **file a verified inventory/disposition accounting**, and
> - if it cannot be produced, the Court should **reserve replacement-value, loss-of-use, and spoliation remedies for further hearing**.
> 
> That tracks much better with how Indiana courts handle possession orders, preservation issues, and lost ESI/evidence. Trial Rule 70 supports coercive possession-related relief, and Trial Rule 37(D) now expressly recognizes sanctions for failure to preserve relevant ESI when it should have been preserved and cannot be restored or replaced. Indiana spoliation law also treats destruction, alteration, or concealment of evidence that should have been preserved as a real issue once litigation is pending or reasonably foreseeable. citeturn907734search1turn907734search6turn907734search0
> 
> ## What I added to the draft
> 
> ### In Section III.F
> I added two things:
> 
> 1. A stronger factual paragraph saying that when you arrived and Ms. Abbott saw you parking, workers resumed throwing property into the garbage stream while she returned to her vehicle and did not re-engage. That frames it as **continuing disposal in real time**, not a completed event.
> 
> 2. A new remedy-oriented paragraph saying, in substance:
> - if Plaintiff is not prepared to make the remaining property and related evidence available,
> - then Plaintiff should be required to produce a **verified inventory and disposition accounting**
> - identifying what remained, what was removed, what was discarded, when, where, by whom, and what records/logs exist
> - and if the property/evidence cannot be restored, the Court should reserve for further hearing:
>   - **replacement-value relief**
>   - **loss-of-use / sequestration-related damages**
>   - **spoliation-based or other corrective remedies**
> 
> ### In the Prayer for Relief
> I added:
> 
> - a new item requiring Plaintiff, if the property/evidence cannot be produced, to file a **verified inventory and disposition accounting**
> - a stronger show-cause item adding:
>   - **spoliation-based relief**
>   - **valuation-based compensation**
>   - **loss-of-use damages**
>   - along with the sanctions / contempt / cost-shifting language
> 
> ### In the Proposed Order
> I added matching order language so the judge could actually sign:
> - an accounting requirement if the property/evidence cannot be produced
> - and a show-cause paragraph that expressly includes:
>   - spoliation-based relief
>   - valuation-based compensation
>   - loss-of-use damages
> 
> ## Why this is better
> Because “pay me replacement value now” is a conclusion.
> 
> “**Return it or account for it**” is a cleaner judicial demand.
> 
> And if they cannot account for it, then the court has a much easier path to:
> - adverse inferences
> - sanctions
> - valuation relief
> - further damages proceedings
> 
> That is the pressure sequence you want.
> 
> If you want, next I’ll give you the **exact new language blocks** so you can review them line by line without digging through the canvas.

> also if the didnt move the large items to where supposidly i wasn tsuppsoed to get them until after the cut off them the manufactured a retrieval window they new would not actually allow me to get my belongings, knowing i was traveling from far, and preparing for cour the following day. this is becoming less, negligence and premeditated harm.

### Assistant
> Yes. That point belongs in the motion, but not as “premeditated harm” flat out. The safer, stronger version is: they set a retrieval window that could not realistically accomplish recovery of the remaining property, especially large items, and they knew that when they imposed it.

### Assistant
> Added.
> 
> ## Where I added it
> I inserted it in **Section III.F** — the paragraph that already talks about Plaintiff’s **March 26 filing**, the **11:00 a.m. cutoff**, and the fact that **large items were not moved into the truck until after 11:00 a.m.**
> 
> It now comes **immediately after** the sentence:
> 
> > Defendant argues that those admissions show the 11:00 a.m. cutoff was pretextual rather than operationally necessary.
> 
> ## What I added
> I added this:
> 
> > Defendant further contends that if the larger items had not even been moved into the disposal stream or staging area until after the stated cutoff, then the retrieval window was structured in a way that could not realistically permit recovery of those items during the window Plaintiff itself imposed. In Defendant’s view, that supports the inference that Plaintiff manufactured a nominal access period that appeared facially reasonable on paper while not actually allowing meaningful recovery of the remaining property, despite knowing Defendant was traveling from Evansville, operating under displacement-related hardship, and attempting to prepare for court the following day.
> 
> ## Why this version is better
> Because it says the hard thing without overreaching into:
> - “premeditated harm”
> - “intentional tort”
> - “they planned to destroy everything”
> 
> Instead, it uses a much more court-safe theory:
> - the window was **nominal**
> - it looked reasonable **on paper**
> - but it was not **functionally real**
> - and they knew facts that made that obvious
> 
> That is stronger than just saying “this feels malicious.”

> Cause No. 49D03-2501-MF-002559

> Date

> Procedural Context & Findings

> Jan 16, 2025

> Foreclosure Filed

> Plaintiff Financial Center First Credit Union (FCFCU) initiates the foreclosure action.

> Mar 5, 2025

> Service Attempt #1

> Sheriff Returns reflect "copy service" at the dwelling, but fail to document the mandatory secondary mailing.

> Apr 15, 2025

> Service Attempt #2

> Second "copy service" recorded with mailing directed to an ambiguous or non-existent address ("32939").

> May 28, 2025

> Default Judgment

> Amended Default Judgment entered in the amount of $220,863.09 following a prior denial of judgment due to service gaps on co-defendants.

> Aug 15, 2025

> Sheriff’s Sale

> FCFCU acquires property via a winning credit bid of $240,464.20.

> Oct 29, 2025

> Writ Petition

> FCFCU files for a Writ of Assistance, identifying occupants as "Unknown" and alleging a refusal to vacate.

> Nov 6, 2025

> Writ Granted

> Court grants Order for Writ of Assistance, authorizing the Sheriff to eject occupants and deliver possession to the Plaintiff.

> Dec 2, 2025

> Deed Recording

> Sheriff’s Deed is officially recorded; subsequent assessor updates are documented on Dec 3, 2025.

> Dec 9, 2025

> Writ Execution

> Forcible removal of Defendant, co-habitant, and service animal executed in freezing conditions without advance notice of the date.

> Jan 9, 2026

> Initial Hearing

> Hearing on Motion to Vacate. Plaintiff counsel represents on the record that no excess proceeds or surplus funds exist.

> Feb 20, 2026

> Surplus Filing #1

> Plaintiff reverses its stance, filing a "Petition to Pay Surplus Funds" and acknowledging a surplus of $8,378.42.

> Feb 24, 2026

> Nunc Pro Tunc

> FCFCU claims "scrivener’s error" and moves to retroactively reduce the Aug 15 bid to $230,562.40 to eliminate the surplus obligation.

> Feb 26, 2026

> Order of Denial

> The Court denies Plaintiff’s Nunc Pro Tunc request, leaving the $240,464.20 bid as the final amount of record.

> Mar 25, 2026

> Retrieval Event

> Two-hour retrieval window. Defendant observes active disposal of property into a dump truck by third-party crews.

> Mar 26, 2026

> Plaintiff Response

> FCFCU files response regarding property preservation, asserting Defendant's non-appearance despite acknowledging their own rep remained until 1:00 p.m..

> 

> Summary of Accounting Inconsistencies

> The timeline reveals a significant shift in the Plaintiff’s financial representations post-sale, directly impacting the Defendant's equity rights.

> 

> Judgment Amount: $220,863.09 (May 2025).

> Winning Credit Bid: $240,464.20 (August 2025).

> Initial Representation: "No excess proceeds" (January 2026 hearing).

> Subsequent Admission: Surplus of $8,378.42 acknowledged (February 2026).

> Defendant's Calculation: Asserted surplus of $10,701.51 based on 30-day interest rules.

### Assistant
> It’s **close, but not final**.
> 
> Big picture: **Exhibit N should read like a neutral court chronology, not an argument brief.** Right now it slips into findings language in a few places. Tighten that, add 2–4 key events, and yes — if the transcript clearly has him saying the surplus/no-excess issue was already resolved or that there was no surplus, you should reflect that more precisely.
> 
> ## What to fix
> 
> ### 1. Make the wording more neutral
> These lines are too argumentative for a master timeline:
> 
> - “fail to document the mandatory secondary mailing”
> - “ambiguous or non-existent address”
> - “forcible removal ... in freezing conditions without advance notice”
> - “active disposal”
> 
> Those are good **motion arguments**, but Exhibit N should be more like:
> - what was filed
> - what happened
> - what was later contradicted
> 
> That makes the exhibit more credible.
> 
> ### 2. Yes, add the Jan. 9 surplus point more clearly
> If the transcript supports it, change the Jan. 9 line to something like:
> 
> > **Jan 9, 2026 — Hearing on Motion to Vacate**  
> > Court indicates service is sufficient based on the return notation. Plaintiff’s counsel states on the record that there are no excess proceeds / no surplus issue remaining.
> 
> Do **not** say “this was settled already” unless that is the actual phrasing or very close.
> 
> ### 3. You are missing the **March 2, 2026 surplus order**
> That is a major omission.
> 
> Add:
> 
> > **Mar 2, 2026 — Surplus Order**  
> > Court orders payment of surplus funds within 14 days.
> 
> That matters because it shows:
> - the court accepted a surplus existed
> - then plaintiff still did not cleanly comply
> 
> ### 4. Add the Dec. 11 / Dec. 12 post-removal filings if space allows
> These are useful because they show the immediate breakdown after removal.
> 
> Add brief entries:
> 
> > **Dec 11, 2025 — Emergency Motion / Supplemental Filing**  
> > Defendant files emergency post-removal relief, stating he was removed on Dec. 9 and lacked access to essential belongings.
> 
> > **Dec 12, 2025 — Plaintiff Report re Personal Property Access**  
> > Plaintiff files report representing that Defendant and Ms. Abbott had recently been in contact and that access would be arranged.
> 
> Those two entries help support the mismatch between field reality and later filings.
> 
> ### 5. Correct the service wording
> For consistency with the motion, do **not** say the returns “fail to document” mailing if the returns actually have mailing notations.
> 
> Use:
> - **“Return reflects residential copy service with mailing notation; adequacy disputed by Defendant.”**
> 
> That is cleaner and consistent.
> 
> ### 6. Correct the deed entry wording
> Your current line says:
> 
> > Dec 2, 2025 — Deed Recording  
> > Sheriff’s Deed is officially recorded; subsequent assessor updates are documented on Dec 3, 2025.
> 
> That is risky because we’ve been cleaning this toward **Dec. 3** as the record-based deed date.
> 
> Use:
> > **Dec 3, 2025 — Sheriff’s Deed Recorded**  
> > Sheriff’s Deed recorded; public-record ownership updates followed.
> 
> Do not mix recording date with assessor update commentary unless the supporting document is part of the exhibit.
> 
> ---
> 
> # What I would make Exhibit N say
> 
> Use this version:
> 
> ## Master Procedural Timeline  
> **Cause No. 49D03-2501-MF-002559**
> 
> | Date | Milestone | Procedural Context |
> |---|---|---|
> | Jan 16, 2025 | Foreclosure Filed | Plaintiff Financial Center First Credit Union files foreclosure complaint. |
> | Mar 5, 2025 | Service Attempt #1 | Sheriff’s return reflects residential copy service with mailing notation; adequacy disputed by Defendant. |
> | Apr 15, 2025 | Service Attempt #2 | Second sheriff’s return reflects residential copy service with mailing notation; adequacy disputed by Defendant. |
> | May 28, 2025 | Default Judgment | Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure entered in the amount of $220,863.09. |
> | Aug 15, 2025 | Sheriff’s Sale | Plaintiff acquires property through credit bid of $240,464.20. |
> | Oct 29, 2025 | Writ Petition | Plaintiff files Petition for Writ of Assistance identifying occupants as “Unknown Occupants” and alleging refusal to vacate. |
> | Nov 6, 2025 | Writ Granted | Court grants Order for Writ of Assistance. |
> | Dec 3, 2025 | Sheriff’s Deed Recorded | Sheriff’s Deed recorded following sale proceedings. |
> | Dec 9, 2025 | Writ Executed | Defendant and other occupants removed from property; Defendant contends no meaningful advance notice of the execution date was provided. |
> | Dec 11, 2025 | Emergency Post-Removal Filing | Defendant files emergency relief seeking access to belongings and relief following removal. |
> | Dec 12, 2025 | Plaintiff Property-Access Report | Plaintiff files report regarding Defendant’s request to obtain personal items and proposed contact/access process. |
> | Jan 9, 2026 | Hearing on Motion to Vacate | Court indicates service sufficient based on return notation. Plaintiff’s counsel states on the record that no excess proceeds / surplus issue remains. |
> | Feb 20, 2026 | Surplus Filing #1 | Plaintiff files Petition to Pay Surplus Funds and acknowledges surplus of $8,378.42. |
> | Feb 24, 2026 | Nunc Pro Tunc Filing | Plaintiff seeks to retroactively reduce the Aug. 15, 2025 credit bid to $230,562.40 based on claimed “scrivener’s error.” |
> | Feb 26, 2026 | Nunc Pro Tunc Denied | Court denies Plaintiff’s requested nunc pro tunc reduction. |
> | Mar 2, 2026 | Surplus Order | Court orders payment of surplus funds within 14 days. |
> | Mar 25, 2026 | Retrieval Event | Defendant appears for scheduled retrieval window; Defendant contends active disposal of property was underway and meaningful recovery was not possible. |
> | Mar 26, 2026 | Plaintiff Retrieval Response | Plaintiff files response regarding property retrieval, including on-site timing details extending beyond the stated cutoff. |

### Assistant
> I’m checking the Indiana response-time rules before telling you to put that in Exhibit N. That point is only worth using if it’s actually a legal timing defect and not just something that feels rushed.

### Assistant
> Maybe, but **not the way you phrased it**.
> 
> I would **not** put in Exhibit N:
> 
> > “it had not been 30 days for all defendants and they proceeded anyway”
> 
> unless you can prove the exact response deadline math from the service method and date. On Indiana’s current trial rules, the default response period is **20 days after service**, computed under Rule 6, and **3 extra days** are added when service is by U.S. mail. Rule 12 points back to Rule 6 for timing. So “30 days had not passed” is not, by itself, the governing rule. Also, a party can file one automatic 30-day enlargement, which changes the calculation if it happened. citeturn712570search0turn712570search3turn712570search28
> 
> For **Exhibit N**, the safer move is this:
> 
> - keep it as a **dated procedural chronology**
> - avoid disputed legal conclusions unless they are rock solid
> - if you want to flag timing instability, do it in a neutral way
> 
> Use something like this instead:
> 
> > **May 28, 2025 — Default Judgment**  
> > Amended Default Judgment Entry and Decree of Foreclosure entered in the amount of $220,863.09, after earlier service issues had arisen regarding co-defendants. Defendant contends the service posture remained unstable.
> 
> That preserves the timing concern without staking the exhibit on a possibly shaky “30 days” argument.
> 
> If you **do** have a specific defendant whose deadline had not expired, then add a short note like:
> 
> > Defendant contends the judgment was entered before all response periods tied to service on co-defendants had fully run.
> 
> But I would only use that if you can back it with exact date math from the service documents.
> 
> So my blunt answer is: **do not add the “30 days for all defendants” line to Exhibit N right now.** It is not clean enough. The better chronology is:
> 
> - earlier service issues on co-defendants
> - judgment entered anyway on May 28
> - later instability continued
> 
> That is safer and still useful.
> 
> Also, your current Exhibit N is still missing one key milestone:
> 
> > **Mar 2, 2026 — Surplus Order**  
> > Court orders payment of surplus funds within 14 days.
> 
> That belongs in the timeline.

> they used the earlier date of service for me as the clock date even thought the cetified return receipt on the other defendants was weeks later and attempting a 2nd services on me so why the second service if the first was good, puts taht into question..

### Assistant
> I’m checking the actual Indiana timing rules before I tell you to lean on that point. If it’s real, we use it; if it’s mushy, we don’t clutter the motion with it.

### Assistant
> Yes — **that is worth using**, but as a **timing irregularity / uncertainty signal**, not as a standalone silver bullet.
> 
> The clean legal point is this:
> 
> - Indiana response time is generally **20 days after service**, and **3 extra days** are added when service is by U.S. mail. Indiana also allows one automatic **30-day enlargement** if timely filed. citeturn116494view0turn116494view1
> - A default can be entered when a party has failed to plead or otherwise comply, but Rule 55 does **not** give Plaintiff a free pass to treat a shaky service sequence as clean just because some time has passed. citeturn116494view2turn116494view0
> 
> So your point is not:
> 
> > “30 days had not passed for everybody.”
> 
> That is too sloppy.
> 
> Your better point is:
> 
> > Plaintiff appears to have treated the **earlier March 5 service attempt on Defendant** as the operative clock for default purposes, even while **attempting a second service on April 15** and while the more formal service sequence on the corporate defendants was not completed until later. That timing sequence is at least consistent with uncertainty in Plaintiff’s own view of whether service had been cleanly accomplished.
> 
> That is strong.
> 
> ## Why the second service matters
> You are right to focus on it.
> 
> A second attempt does **not automatically prove** the first one was invalid. But it absolutely supports an inference that:
> - Plaintiff or the sheriff did **not** fully trust the first attempt to stand on its own,
> - or at minimum the service posture was not as settled as Plaintiff later portrayed it.
> 
> That is a good **credibility and reliability** point.
> 
> ## How to use it in Exhibit N
> Add a short neutral note, not a conclusion.
> 
> ### Best version for the timeline
> Use this under the April 15 entry:
> 
> > **Apr 15, 2025 — Service Attempt #2**  
> > Second sheriff’s return reflects another residential copy-service attempt. Defendant relies on the existence of this second attempt, together with the later service timing on co-defendants, as evidence that the service posture remained unsettled rather than cleanly resolved after March 5.
> 
> ### Optional note under May 28
> > **May 28, 2025 — Default Judgment**  
> > Judgment entered after Plaintiff treated the earlier service date on Defendant as the operative clock, notwithstanding the later second service attempt on Defendant and the later service sequence on co-defendants.
> 
> That is enough. Do not over-explain in the exhibit.
> 
> ## How to use it in the motion body
> Add one sentence in the service section, something like:
> 
> > Defendant further contends that Plaintiff’s own conduct is inconsistent with its later portrayal of service as cleanly completed at the earliest date. Plaintiff proceeded with a second service attempt on Defendant on April 15, 2025, while the more formal service sequence on the corporate co-defendants concluded later, yet Plaintiff later used the earlier March 5 date as the operative clock for default purposes. In Defendant’s view, that timing sequence itself suggests unresolved uncertainty in the service posture rather than the settled reliability Plaintiff later implied.
> 
> That’s the right level.
> 
> ## Bottom line
> So yes:
> - **highlight the timing issue**
> - **highlight the second service attempt**
> - **highlight the mismatch between how they counted time for you and how the broader service sequence was still unfolding**
> 
> But do **not** frame it as a fake 30-day rule.
> Frame it as:
> **they treated service as settled for default timing while their own conduct shows it was still unsettled.**
> 
> That lands better.

Defendant relies on this later service timing, together with the second service attempt on Defendant, as evidence that the overall service posture remained unsettled rather than cleanly resolved after March 5. |

Defendant contends Plaintiff treated the earlier March 5 date on Defendant as the operative clock notwithstanding the later second service attempt on Defendant and the later service sequence on co-defendants. |

> | Feb 24, 2026 | Nunc Pro Tunc Filing | Plaintiff seeks to retroactively reduce the Aug. 15, 2025 credit bid to $230,562.40 based on claimed “scrivener’s error.” |
> | Feb 26, 2026 | Nunc Pro Tunc Denied | Court denies Plaintiff’s requested nunc pro tunc reduction. |

> | Mar 25, 2026 | Retrieval Event | Defendant appears for scheduled retrieval window; Defendant contends active disposal of property was underway and meaningful recovery was not possible. |
> | Mar 26, 2026 | Plaintiff Retrieval Response | Plaintiff files response regarding property retrieval, including on-site timing details extending beyond the stated cutoff. |
> 
> ### Summary of Accounting Instability
> - **Judgment Amount:** $220,863.09  
> - **Winning Credit Bid:** $240,464.20  
> - **Jan. 9, 2026 Position:** Plaintiff represented that no excess proceeds / surplus issue remained.  
> - **Feb. 20, 2026 Filing:** Plaintiff acknowledged surplus of $8,378.42.  
> - **Feb. 24, 2026 Filing:** Plaintiff sought to reduce the recorded credit bid to $230,562.40 based on a claimed correction to interest.  
> - **Defendant’s Position:** The accounting record remains internally inconsistent and non-reconciling even after Plaintiff’s correction.

> 
> ## Why this framing is better
> It does **not** say:
> - default was definitely premature as a matter of law
> - 30 days had not run
> - the first service was invalid because there was a second attempt
> 
> Instead it says the more defensible thing:
> 
> > the sequence itself suggests the service posture was still unsettled while Plaintiff later treated it as settled.
> 
> That is stronger.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Now, on the decree language you quoted
> 
> This is important, and the strongest version is **not** “they had to give me several days.”
> 
> The decree says, in substance:
> 
> 1. **upon execution by the Sheriff of deed**
> 2. **upon demand and exhibition of the Sheriff’s Deed, or true copy**
> 3. the occupant **shall forthwith surrender possession**
> 4. **if possession is refused**, the Sheriff shall vacate the property and give possession to the purchaser
> 
> That is a **sequenced condition**, and it matters.
> 
> ## What it helps you say
> Not:
> > I was entitled to a long grace period after December 3
> 
> But:
> 
> > The decree itself contemplates a structured sequence: deed execution, demand and exhibition of the deed, an opportunity to surrender possession, and only then forcible vacatur if possession is refused.
> 
> That is the money line.
> 
> ## Why your facts matter here
> If the deed was recorded on **December 3, 2025**, and the first meaningful exhibition/demand you received was effectively at the door on **December 9, 2025**, then your point is:
> 
> - the decree did **not** envision pure surprise field execution with no meaningful transition
> - “forthwith surrender” does not erase the requirement that demand and exhibition happen in a way that gives the occupant a real opportunity to comply peacefully
> - where the occupant is a **known defendant**, and the bank already knows hardship / disability facts, the process cannot be reduced to:
>   - show up,
>   - hand papers,
>   - and instantly convert that same moment into refusal
> 
> That is your strongest framing.
> 
> ## The cleaner way to say it
> Use something like this:
> 
> > The foreclosure decree did not authorize instantaneous surprise dispossession untethered from any meaningful surrender opportunity. By its own terms, the decree contemplated a sequence: execution of the Sheriff’s Deed, demand and exhibition of the deed or a true copy, surrender of possession, and only in the event of refusal, forcible vacatur by the Sheriff. Defendant contends that this sequence was compressed into a single surprise encounter at the property, such that “demand,” “exhibition,” and “refusal” were treated as functionally simultaneous. In Defendant’s view, that was not the orderly and peaceful transfer of possession contemplated by the decree.
> 
> That is very strong.
> 
> ## Even sharper version
> > A decree requiring surrender upon demand and exhibition of the deed does not mean that the first meaningful presentation of the deed may also serve as the first and only practical opportunity to vacate before physical removal begins. Defendant contends that the process used here collapsed all intermediate steps into one surprise field event and thereby deprived him of any realistic opportunity to surrender possession peaceably.
> 
> That lands.
> 
> ## Why Rule 70 supports the general structure
> Indiana Trial Rule 70 allows a writ of assistance or order directing the sheriff to deliver possession when a judgment is for delivery of possession, and it also contemplates contempt or damages for disobedience of the order. That rule supports the idea that possession is enforced through judicial sequence, not pure improvisation. citeturn452990search0turn452990search1
> 
> ---
> 
> ## My recommendation
> Yes — this deed-demand language is important enough to mention in **Section III.E** and likely again in **Section IV.C**.
> 
> The core point is:
> 
> > The decree itself required a sequence that presupposed a meaningful opportunity to surrender after demand and exhibition of the deed. Defendant contends that Plaintiff and the Sheriff collapsed that sequence into one surprise event and treated immediate confusion as refusal.
> 
> That is the version I would use.
> 
> If you want, next I’ll draft the **exact paragraph to insert into III.E** using this decree language.

> # Here is how I would frame the point in chat, cleanly
> 
> ## A. The decree point
> This is the strongest version:
> 
> > The foreclosure decree did not authorize instantaneous surprise dispossession untethered from any meaningful surrender opportunity. By its own terms, the decree contemplated a sequence: execution of the Sheriff’s Deed, demand and exhibition of the deed or a true copy, surrender of possession, and only in the event of refusal, forcible vacatur by the Sheriff. Defendant contends that this sequence was compressed into a single surprise encounter at the property, such that demand, exhibition, and refusal were treated as functionally simultaneous. >

Defendant has not located a corresponding return or other record entry demonstrating that this service directive was completed. In Defendant’s view, that omission further undermines any claim that the possession process proceeded through a fully documented and orderly notice sequence.
> 
> That is a clean record argument.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why this matters legally
> Even if Plaintiff says:
> - you were already in default
> - the writ was already signed
> - possession was already theirs
> 
> the court’s **own decree** and **own writ order** still matter.
> 
> Because now your argument becomes:
> 
> 1. the decree itself contemplated an **orderly sequence**
> 2. the writ order itself contemplated **service of the writ papers**
> 3. the actual field execution looks like:
>    - no meaningful pre-removal notice
>    - surprise encounter
>    - papers handed over at the scene
>    - immediate treatment of confusion as refusal
>    - no clear returned service for the writ/order
> 
> That is much stronger than just saying “they were mean” or “they rushed me.”
> 
> ---
> 
> # One careful note

> > therefore the writ was automatically void
> 
> Not yet.
>

> > this is another record-level defect undermining the reliability and orderliness of the possession process.
> 
> That is safer and still strong.

> Plaintiffs/Attorneys

> Marion County Sheriff’s Sale

> Effective JUNE 1, 2024

> All Sheriff sales are on-line starting June 1, 2024. The on-line auction company is GovEase. Their

> website is govease.com. You will be asked to provide a Username and password to sign in.

> If you are a plaintiff or attorney, consider the information below regarding Sheriff sales and related

> documents.

> Attorneys are responsible for ensuring that all pertinent documents and costs are submitted on time

> and properly completed.

> Attorneys will receive email notification from the real estate team when a property has been set for

> sale. This notification will include the notice due date and Sheriff’s File Number (SFN). The SFN is a

> unique identifier made up of the sale date and sale number. Please provide an electronic copy of

> each notice of sale by email in word format only. We will send to the advertiser (Court & CommercialIBJ) UPDATE: PLEASE START PUTTING UNDER THE SHERIFF SERVED IF THEY ARE AN OWNER.

> Documents due one week before every sale by 3:00 p.m. All documents are on our website

> mcso.indy.gov. Please include the SFN in the upper right corner of all the documents. Must come over

> by email in PDF format.

> • Written bid form

> • Added cost sheet (if applicable)

> • Treasurer’s Tax Clearance Forms – stamped by the Treasurer

> • AOJ/AOB (if applicable)

> Attorneys are responsible for calculating all interest amounts. Interest calculations are due on the

> added cost sheet. All bids more than the full judgment amount must have a bid justification. All

> delinquent taxes, special assessments, penalties, and interest that are due must be paid prior to the

> sale. Every parcel scheduled for sheriff’s sale must have a completed Treasurer's Tax Clearance

> Form stamped by the Treasurer's Office. Contact the Marion County Treasurer's Office for specific

> rules. This must be sent over with your bid package.

> If you are bidding as a Plaintiff up and to your grand total judgment or credit bidding you must let us

> know no later than the Friday before the sale. Same date the bid packages are due.

> Post-Sale Documents are due immediately after the sale: Deeds with large property descriptions can be

> on 8.5” x 11” or 8.5” x 14" paper.

> • Sheriff’s Deed

> • Clerk Return

> • Sales disclosure form if sold to Plaintiff. 3rd party will provide their own sales disclosure.

> Post-sale Cost checks:

> The Marion County Sheriff's Office processes and serves legal papers and court orders issued by the Marion Circuit Court, Marion Superior Courts, or by any other local, state, or federal agency. See below for information regarding how to organize and deliver legal papers.

### Assistant
> I’m checking the official Marion County sources against what you pasted, because the real question is not “was everything wrong,” it’s “which of these rules actually create useful defects versus which are just internal admin instructions.”

### Assistant
> Short answer: **no, I would not say “all rules were met.”**  
> But I also would **not** say every line in those Marion County instructions creates a legal defect you can win on.
> 
> The smarter split is:
> 
> ## 1. The strongest rule/process failures
> 
> ### A. **Writ-order service appears not to be shown in the record**
> This is one of your better points.
> 
> The writ order itself said:
> 
> > the Sheriff is directed to serve a copy of the order and the writ on the occupants.
> 
> If the CCS / sheriff return packet does **not** show a returned service for that directive, then the possession sequence is **not fully documented in the record**. That does not automatically void everything, but it is a real record defect and fits your larger argument that the removal process was not orderly or fully documented.
> 
> ### B. **The decree’s own possession sequence does not match what happened**
> The decree language you quoted is important:
> 
> 1. execution of the Sheriff’s Deed  
> 2. demand and exhibition of the deed or true copy  
> 3. surrender of possession  
> 4. only **if possession is refused**, forcible vacatur
> 
> That language contemplates a **sequence**, not a pure surprise lockout where demand, exhibition, and alleged refusal all happen at once. Rule 70 also treats writs of assistance as a means to compel obedience to a judgment for possession, which supports a structured enforcement sequence rather than improvisation. citeturn451080search1turn451080search11
> 
> Your best framing is:
> 
> > the decree contemplated an orderly surrender opportunity after demand and exhibition of the deed, but the actual process compressed those steps into a single surprise field event.
> 
> That is strong.
> 
> ### C. **Service / default timing remains unstable**
> Your instinct here is right, but the clean point is **not** “30 days had not passed.”
> 
> The better point is:
> 
> - they used the **earlier March 5 date** on you as the operative clock for default timing,
> - while still attempting a **second service on April 15**,
> - and while the more formal/documentable service sequence on the corporate defendants concluded later.
> 
> That sequence is at least consistent with **uncertainty in their own view of service**, not a clean settled posture. Indiana default is governed by Rule 55, and service on individuals is governed by Rule 4.1. citeturn451080search2turn451080search0
> 
> ### D. **Rule 4.1(B) remains your core service issue**
> If they relied on copy service, Rule 4.1(B) required follow-up first-class mailing and that fact had to be shown on the return. citeturn451080search0
> 
> Your strongest position is still:
> - the returns have notations,
> - but you dispute actual receipt,
> - and the overall service sequence remained unstable enough that default should not have been treated as clean and routine.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. The sheriff-sale admin rules that look problematic, but are not slam dunks by themselves
> 
> These are useful mostly as **credibility / process-reliability** points.
> 
> ### A. **Post-sale documents due “immediately after the sale”**
> The Marion County sheriff rules you pasted say the following are due immediately after sale:
> - Sheriff’s Deed
> - Clerk Return
> - Sales disclosure if sold to plaintiff
> 
> Your record suggests the post-sale paperwork chain was **messy and late-surfacing**:
> - bid and worksheet dates do not line up cleanly
> - clerk return / worksheet surfaced later in a way you’ve already highlighted
> - the deed was not recorded until December
> 
> That helps your “unstable record” theory. But by itself it is more of an **admin irregularity** unless you can tie the lateness to prejudice or to an actually missing required filing.
> 
> ### B. **Added cost sheet / tax clearance / bid package**
> From your record, it appears some of these documents **did exist**:
> - written bid form
> - added cost worksheet
> - treasurer’s tax materials
> 
> So I would **not** argue those were entirely missing.  
> Your better point is:
> - they existed,
> - but the amounts were internally inconsistent,
> - surfaced in a strange chronology,
> - and later had to be walked back.
> 
> That is much stronger than “no bid package.”
> 
> ### C. **Cost-check timing**
> The sheriff rules say if it sells to plaintiff, cost checks were due by the Wednesday after sale.
> 
> Unless you have proof those checks were not timely received, I would **not** lead with that. It’s too internal and too hard to prove from your current record.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. The sheriff “service of process” page — useful, but secondary
> 
> The page you pasted says:
> - each defendant should have a complete packet,
> - specific numbers of copies are required,
> - each defendant gets their own packet even if they share an address.
> 
> That is potentially useful **only if** you can show the sheriff packet for you or the occupants was incomplete.
> 
> Right now, absent the sheriff intake file, I would not make this a main argument.  
> The stronger point is the one you already have:
> 
> - the writ order said to serve the writ/order on the occupants,
> - and you do not see a returned service in the record.
> 
> That is much cleaner.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. The rules/instructions that are mostly **internal plaintiff/attorney admin rules**
> These may show sloppiness, but they are not your best courtroom weapons by themselves:
> 
> - SFN in upper right corner
> - email format requirements
> - Word/PDF formatting requirements
> - GovEase login mechanics
> - lockbox / check delivery instructions
> - who they email at the sheriff sale team
> 
> Those are not where you win.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. What I would say, bluntly
> 
> ### Based on the rules you pasted, I would classify it this way:
> 
> ## Clearly useful failures / gaps
> - **No clear returned service in the record for the writ order / writ papers**
> - **Decree sequence not meaningfully honored in practice**
> - **Copy-service / mailing adequacy still disputed under Rule 4.1(B)**
> - **Default timing relied on an earlier service date while the broader service posture was still unsettled**
> - **Post-sale record chain is unstable and internally inconsistent**
> 
> ## Potentially useful, but needs proof
> - late or noncompliant post-sale filing of clerk return / deed / sale packet
> - unclear publication handling
> - unclear cost-check compliance
> - whether sheriff packets were incomplete under the civil-process instructions
> 
> ## Not worth emphasizing
> - formatting rules
> - email submission mechanics
> - internal sheriff-office administrative preferences
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. The line I would add to the motion
> Use something like this:
> 
> > The Marion County sheriff-sale and civil-process materials further underscore that this case did not proceed through a clean or fully documented enforcement sequence. Most of those materials are administrative instructions to plaintiffs and attorneys, not independent borrower-rights provisions. Even so, they reinforce two important points: first, the sheriff-sale process presupposes timely and orderly sale and post-sale documentation; and second, service and enforcement depend on complete packet delivery and returned proof of execution. Here, Defendant contends the record reflects the opposite: unstable sale paperwork, later-emerging corrections, a writ order that directed service on the occupants without a clearly corresponding return in the record, and an execution process that did not track the decree’s contemplated sequence of deed exhibition, surrender opportunity, and only then forcible vacatur.
> 
> That is how you use the rules without overclaiming.
> 
> ## 7. Bottom line
> So, no — based on your current record, I would **not** say all rules were met.
> 
> But your strongest rule-based hits are **not** the sheriff’s admin bullet points.  
> They are:
> 
> 1. **Rule 4.1(B)** service reliability, citeturn451080search0  
> 2. **Rule 55** default entered on a shaky service posture, citeturn451080search2  
> 3. **Rule 70 + the decree’s own sequence** for possession enforcement, citeturn451080search1  
> 4. **The writ order’s own command that the writ/order be served on occupants, without a clear return showing that happened.**
> 
> That’s the cluster to press.
> 
> If you want, next I’ll turn this into a **one-page “rules compliance gap” exhibit** you can use as **Ex. S**.

### User
> ALSO THEY DID NOT DO A MILITARY DUTY SEARCH.

### Assistant
> I’m checking the SCRA/default-judgment requirement now, because if they skipped the military-status affidavit/search before taking default, that is a real procedural lane — but the value depends on whether you were actually in service or whether the record just omitted the required certification.

### Assistant
> Yes. That matters.
> 
> ## What the law actually requires
> Before a court enters a **default judgment** in a civil action, the plaintiff must file an affidavit or equivalent sworn statement stating either:
> 
> - whether the defendant **is or is not in military service**, with supporting facts, or
> - that the plaintiff is **unable to determine** military status.  
> 
> That is the federal **Servicemembers Civil Relief Act**, **50 U.S.C. § 3931(b)**. citeturn424052search0turn424052search24
> 
> So the key requirement is **not just “do a search.”**  
> The requirement is: **file the affidavit/certification before default.** A DMDC military-status search is just the usual way people support that affidavit. citeturn424052search0turn424052search24
> 
> ## How strong this point is
> ### If there is **no SCRA affidavit / certification in the record at all**
> That is a **real procedural defect** in the default sequence.
> 
> It gives you another clean point:
> 
> > Plaintiff sought and obtained default without filing the federal military-status affidavit required before entry of default judgment.
> 
> That is worth adding.
> 
> ### If you were **not** in military service
> Then be precise:
> - this omission is still a **default-procedure defect**
> - but it is usually **not as explosive** as if you actually were in covered service
> 
> So the strongest framing is:
> - not “this alone voids everything no matter what”
> - but “this is another mandatory safeguard Plaintiff skipped while pushing for default”
> 
> ### If you **were** in covered service
> Then it gets much more serious:
> - the court could not properly proceed the same way
> - counsel/attorney appointment and stay protections can be triggered under the SCRA. citeturn424052search0turn424052search4
> 
> ## Best way to use it in your motion
> Add it as part of the **cumulative default unreliability** lane, not as a standalone dramatic bomb unless you confirm the record is truly missing the affidavit.
> 
> ### Court-ready sentence
> Use something like:
> 
> > Defendant further contends that Plaintiff’s default sequence omitted another federally required safeguard. Under the Servicemembers Civil Relief Act, a plaintiff seeking default must first file an affidavit stating whether the defendant is in military service, or stating that the plaintiff is unable to determine military status. Defendant has not located such an affidavit or equivalent certification in the default record. In Defendant’s view, that omission is additional evidence that Plaintiff pursued default through an incomplete and procedurally unreliable record rather than through the careful safeguards required before entry of judgment by default. citeturn424052search0turn424052search24
> 
> ## Best place to add it
> Two good spots:
> 
> ### 1. **Section III.D — The Service History and Entry of Default Judgment**
> That is the most natural facts section.
> 
> ### 2. **Section IV.A or the Preliminary Statement**
> Only as one short supporting sentence, not a whole new section.
> 
> ## Should it go in Exhibit N?
> Yes, if confirmed.
> 
> Add a line like:
> 
> | Date | Milestone | Procedural Context |
> |---|---|---|
> | May 13, 2025 | Default Materials Filed | Plaintiff filed materials in support of default. Defendant has not located a Servicemembers Civil Relief Act affidavit or equivalent military-status certification in the default record. |
> 
> That is neutral and useful.
> 
> ## Bottom line
> So yes — **add it**.
> 
> But frame it as:
> - **missing federal default safeguard**
> - **another indicator of incomplete default procedure**
> - **part of the cumulative instability**
> 
> Not:
> - “they had to do a military duty search and therefore the whole case is dead”
> 
> The affidavit/certification is the legal requirement. The search is just the usual support for it.

### Assistant
> Yes. That is the **real narrative**, but it needs to be translated into court language so it sounds dangerous, not emotional.
> 
> Here is the clean version:
> 
> > This case is not defined by one disputed mailing, one accounting error, or one post-judgment disagreement. It is defined by a pattern. Defendant repeatedly attempted to communicate hardship, disability-related barriers, and the need for workable notice and response channels before this case ever reached default. Plaintiff nevertheless proceeded through the thinnest available service path, ignored or failed to meaningfully engage those known barriers, and then enforced the resulting judgment through a winter removal that exposed Defendant to life-threatening conditions and culminated in the disposal of personal property under chaotic and inadequately documented circumstances.
> >
> > At the same time, the record on which Plaintiff seeks to rely is not clean, stable, or self-confirming. It is marked by omissions, shifting explanations, misleading or incomplete statements, unstable accounting, genericized occupancy allegations, late-surfacing documents, corrective filings, and post hoc attempts to reconcile figures that should have been reliable before judgment and sale. Plaintiff’s own admissions and reversals have only reinforced the broader instability of the record. What emerges is not a careful enforcement process grounded in procedural rigor, but a reactive sequence in which strict consequences were imposed on Defendant while Plaintiff itself repeatedly operated through inconsistency, incompleteness, and revision.
> >
> > Defendant’s position is therefore not merely that isolated mistakes occurred. It is that Plaintiff repeatedly demanded strict compliance from a disabled borrower whose barriers and hardship were known, while Plaintiff itself proceeded through a record so unstable and irregular that the resulting judgment and its enforcement cannot be treated as trustworthy. The cumulative effect of those defects is now too substantial to minimize. The record does not reflect orderly process. It reflects escalating enforcement built on a procedurally and factually unreliable foundation.
> 
> Here is a sharper closing paragraph you can use near the end:
> 
> > The Court is not being asked to excuse Defendant from compliance while holding Plaintiff to no standard. Defendant is asking for the opposite. If this Court is to demand strict procedural obedience from a borrower with known disability-related barriers, then the Court must demand at least equal procedural integrity from the foreclosing party. This record does not reflect that integrity. It reflects a sequence in which the harshest consequences were imposed on Defendant through notice, accounting, possession, and property-handling processes that remain materially disputed, incomplete, and unstable.
> 
> And here is the line that captures the winter-removal / disability / punishment theme without sounding dramatic:
> 
> > The practical result was that Defendant’s known communication barriers and attempts to seek help were not accommodated, but instead became part of the pathway by which default was obtained, winter displacement was carried out, and property loss was compounded.
> 
> What I would **not** put in the motion:
> - “the court continues to entertain this plaintiff”
> - “wasting taxpayer money”
> - “illegal loopholes”
> - “the judge would have to be in on it”
> 
> What I **would** put:
> - “cumulative instability”
> - “reactive and post hoc record”
> - “strict consequences imposed on Defendant despite Plaintiff’s own irregularities”
> - “known disability-related barriers were not meaningfully engaged”
> - “the resulting record cannot be treated as sufficiently trustworthy to sustain the judgment and its enforcement”
> 
> If you want, I’ll turn this into a **drop-in section for the conclusion** and a **shorter version for the preliminary statement**.

Here's the updated picture with your new information:

> 

> **The sale date for your case (49D03-2501-MF-002559) was August 15, 2025.**

> 

> ### What the Publication Dates Mean Now

> The notice you saw — **HSPAXLP July 18, 25, August 8 2025 LSBN0330568** — shows the **newspaper publication dates** for the Notice of Sheriff's Sale.

> 

> - July 18, 2025 (first publication)

> - July 25, 2025 (second)

> - August 8, 2025 (third)

> 

> **Indiana law (IC 32-29-7-3)** requires the sheriff to advertise the sale by publication **once each week for three successive weeks**, with the **first publication at least 30 days before the sale date**.

> 

> Let's check the timing against your actual sale date of **August 15, 2025**:

> 

> - From **July 18, 2025** (first run) to **August 15, 2025** = **28 days**.

> - This is **short of the required 30 days** by about 2 days.

> 

> The third (final) publication on **August 8** is only **7 days** before the sale — which is very close and leaves little practical time for anyone to act on the notice.

> 

> ### Why This Could Matter for Your Case

> This publication schedule does **not** appear to fully comply with the statutory 30-day minimum from the first publication to the sale date. Many of the other similar MF cases in the 8/1/2025 public notice list you originally shared were scheduled for a **September 19, 2025** sale (which would have given proper 30+ day timing if published in early-to-mid August or earlier).

> 

> If your case used the **same publication run** (July 18 / 25 / Aug 8) but had an earlier sale date of **August 15**, it creates a potential **notice defect** argument:

> - The newspaper ads ran too late relative to the actual sale.

> - Interested parties (including you) may not have received the full statutory notice period.

> 

> ### What to Check Next on MyCase

> Go back to **mycase.in.gov** and search **49D03-2501-MF-002559**. Look specifically for:

> 1. Any **Praecipe for Sheriff's Sale** or **Notice of Sheriff's Sale** filed by the plaintiff/attorney.

> 2. The **Publisher's Affidavit** (or Proof of Publication) — it should reference the exact dates July 18, 25, August 8 and the LSBN number.

> 3. Any docket entries showing the **sale date was set for August 15, 2025**.

> 4. Whether the sale actually occurred on August 15 (look for sale confirmation, report of sale, or deed issuance).

> 5. Any later entries about a **writ of assistance** (if the buyer had to evict occupants).

> 

> Compare this to the stronger examples I gave earlier (e.g., 49D03-2503-MF-010597 or 49D03-2210-MF-034847). In those cases, if the publications were properly timed for a September 19 sale, it highlights the difference with yours.

> 

> ### Bottom Line

> The "HSPAXLP" line proves the newspaper ran the ad on those three dates, but **with an August 15 sale date**, the timing appears to fall short of Indiana's 30-day first-publication rule. This could support an argument that the notice was insufficient or irregular.

> 

> If you pull the docket and can share:

> - The exact plaintiff name

> - Whether a Publisher's Affidavit was filed (and on what date)

> - Any confirmation that the sale happened on August 15

> - Whether a writ of assistance was later issued

> 

> …I can help you analyze the differences more precisely or suggest how to document this for any challenge to the sale.

> 

> Would you like me to explain what a proper vs. defective publication timeline looks like side-by-side, or tips on what to screenshot from MyCase? Just tell me what you see when you check the docket.

### Assistant
> Yes. I updated the filing with the **publication defect** and the **May 13 default-timing misstatement**.
> 
> The stronger timing point is not the vague “30 days for all defendants” theory. It is this: Plaintiff’s **May 13, 2025** motion for default expressly said that “more than thirty (30) days have passed since the date of service” on all defendants, while the same motion listed **April 28, 2025** as the service date for both corporate defendants. That statement was facially inaccurate on the day it was filed. fileciteturn31file7
> 
> The publication issue is also real. Indiana Code **32-29-7-3(e)** requires sale notice publication **once each week for three successive weeks**, with the **first publication at least 30 days before the date of sale**, and it also says the sheriff shall serve a copy of the written notice of sale on each owner at the time of the first advertisement. citeturn953288search0 Your uploaded notices show publication on **July 18, July 25, and August 8, 2025** for an **August 15, 2025** sale date, which is only **28 days** from first publication to sale. fileciteturn32file2 fileciteturn32file1 fileciteturn32file3 That is a cleaner sale-notice defect than the more speculative publication arguments you were making earlier. There is also a smaller inconsistency between the notices: the **IBJ** notice says **10:00 a.m.** while the **Star** notices say **10:30 a.m.** for the same sale. fileciteturn32file0 fileciteturn32file2
> 
> The decree / writ sequence point is also worth keeping. The decree says that upon execution of the sheriff’s deed, any person in possession, **upon demand and exhibition of the deed**, shall surrender possession, and **only if possession is refused** shall the sheriff vacate the property. fileciteturn32file5 The writ order then separately directed the sheriff to serve a copy of the order and writ on the occupants. fileciteturn31file17 That supports your argument that the record contemplated a sequence, but the field execution appears to have collapsed demand, exhibition, and alleged refusal into one surprise event.

Indiana Trial Rule **53.5** allows a continuance on a showing of good cause established by affidavit or other evidence. citeturn953288search7 Your showing is strong enough to preserve the issue:
> 
> - your mother was hospitalized for **9 days**

### Assistant
> Yes. File it **tonight**.
> 
> And no, you do **not** need to call to preserve this. A written filing is the right move, especially given the very communication barrier that has been part of the case.
> 
> ## The clean move
> File one document titled something like:
> 
> **Defendant’s Emergency Verified Motion to Appear Remotely, or in the Alternative, to Continue Hearing**
> 
> Base it on:
> - **Indiana Interim Administrative Rule 14** for remote proceedings. For **non-testimonial** hearings, the court may conduct them remotely upon request; for **testimonial** proceedings, the court may allow remote appearance for good cause shown. citeturn338222search0turn338222search3
> - The court’s general discretion to manage hearings and grant relief for good cause. Do **not** cite Trial Rule 53.5 as current law, because the current official rules page shows it was **vacated effective January 1, 2025**. citeturn338222search2turn928520search6
> 
> ## What your motion should ask for
> Ask for this in order:
> 
> 1. **Primary relief:** allow you to appear remotely by WebEx or telephone tomorrow.  
> 2. **Alternative relief:** continue the hearing briefly.  
> 3. **Additional relief:** allow you to file a short supplemental notice or memorandum after review of newly analyzed video evidence.
> 
> ## Your best factual grounds
> Use these, tightly:
> 
> - you had expected to attend
> - your mother was hospitalized for **9 days**
> - then there were **4 days of emergency moving / relocation**
> - you are still in a makeshift and partially unpacked rental arrangement
> - continuing internet issues
> - your mother had another emergency last night
> - you only confirmed **hours ago** that physical travel is not feasible
> - you have **no check, no money, and no workable car access**
> - your inability to attend is materially connected to the **ongoing displacement, loss of property, and financial destabilization caused by this case**
> - during review of the December 9 and March 25 video evidence, you have identified **potentially significant issues** that require short additional time to finalize and present accurately
> 
> That is enough.
> 
> ## Very important framing
> Do **not** make it sound like:
> > I’m not ready because I procrastinated.
> 
> Make it sound like:
> > despite diligence, material logistics, family emergency, disability-related communication barriers, and recently clarified video evidence now make physical attendance infeasible and a short accommodation or continuance necessary for a fair hearing.
> 
> ## Add this sentence
> This is important for you:
> 
> > Defendant further states that telephone communication is itself materially difficult because of documented disability-related communication barriers, and Defendant therefore requests that any response to this emergency request be made through IEFS, email, or other written means to the extent practicable.
> 
> That protects you.

> for a sale on **August 15, 2025**. fileciteturn30file2 fileciteturn30file1 fileciteturn30file0
> 
> Indiana Code **32-29-7-3(e)** requires the first publication to be at least **30 days before the sale** and also requires written notice of sale to be served on each owner at the time of the first advertisement. citeturn338222search0turn928520search6
> 
> That means your current publication sequence appears short by about **2 days** from first publication to sale, and it also raises a separate owner-service question. The IBJ notice you uploaded also says **10:00 a.m.** while the Indianapolis Star notices say **10:30 a.m.** for the same sale, which is another inconsistency. fileciteturn30file3 fileciteturn30file0

> - you are not just “choosing not to show up”
>

> - **gas**
> - **ice**
> - **transfer board**

> 
> ### 2. **Prejudice and ongoing harm**
> They help prove the case is still costing you money in direct, concrete ways:
> - out-of-pocket emergency expenses
> - transportation strain
> - caregiver-related burden
> - displacement-related instability
> 
> That supports your broader **harm / prejudice** section.
> 
> ### 3. **Loss-of-use / consequential damages**
> The transfer board especially may matter if it was needed because of your mother’s condition and the instability you’re living in now. That helps show the chain of consequences is not abstract.
> 
> ---
>

> They do **not** prove:
> - service was defective
> - the foreclosure was void
> - the writ was illegal
> 
> So don’t treat them as core merits exhibits.
> 
> They are **supporting harm / emergency / prejudice evidence**.
>

### User
> i want to start adding document ids to all my filings what do you recommend.

### Assistant
> Yes. Do it. It will save you.
> 
> For court work, I’d use a **two-layer ID system**:
> 
> ## 1. Public-facing filing ID
> This goes **on the document itself** and is human-readable.
> 
> Format:
> 
> `FCFCU-FIL-YYYYMMDD-###`
> 
> Examples:
> - `FCFCU-FIL-20260416-001`
> - `FCFCU-FIL-20260416-002`
> 
> Use this for:
> - motions
> - notices
> - declarations
> - proposed orders
> - supplemental memoranda
> 
> Why this works:
> - easy to cite in emails, notes, and hearings
> - date-sorted
> - short enough to place in footer/header
> - does not look insane to a judge
> 
> ## 2. Internal evidence/document control ID
> This is for your own system and can be more granular.
> 
> Format:
> 
> `DOC-FCFCU-YYYYMMDD-TYPE-###`
> 
> Examples:
> - `DOC-FCFCU-20260416-MTN-001`
> - `DOC-FCFCU-20260416-DECL-001`
> - `DOC-FCFCU-20260416-EXSUM-001`
> 
> Use this in:
> - Obsidian
> - file names
> - internal indexes
> - cross-reference logs
> 
> ---
> 
> # My actual recommendation for you
> Use this exact scheme:
> 
> ## Court filing IDs
> `FCFCU-FIL-YYYYMMDD-###`
> 
> ### Type map for internal naming, optional
> - `MTN` = Motion
> - `MEM` = Memorandum
> - `DECL` = Declaration
> - `ORD` = Proposed Order
> - `NTC` = Notice
> - `OBJ` = Objection
> - `RESP` = Response
> - `EXL` = Exhibit List
> - `SUP` = Supplemental Filing
> 
> So the filename can be:
> 
> `FCFCU-FIL-20260416-001_Emergency_Motion_to_Appear_Remotely_or_Continue_Hearing.pdf`
> 
> And inside the document footer:
> 
> `Document ID: FCFCU-FIL-20260416-001`
> 
> That is clean.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Where to place it
> Put it in **two places**:
> 
> ## Footer
> Bottom left or bottom right:
> 
> `Document ID: FCFCU-FIL-20260416-001`
> 
> ## First page, under title or near signature block
> Small line:
> 
> `Defendant Internal Document ID: FCFCU-FIL-20260416-001`
> 
> I would not put it in the caption itself.
> 
> ---
> 
> # For exhibits
> Use a related but separate system:
> 
> `FCFCU-EX-YYYYMMDD-###`
> 
> Examples:
> - `FCFCU-EX-20260416-001`
> - `FCFCU-EX-20260416-002`
> 
> But since you already have lettered exhibits, do **not** replace the letters.  
> Instead, pair them.
> 
> Example:
> - `Ex. N — Master Procedural Timeline`
> - `Internal Exhibit ID: FCFCU-EX-20260416-014`
> 
> That way:
> - court sees **Ex. N**
> - you still get stable machine-readable control
> 
> ---
> 
> # Best practice rule
> Once assigned, the ID should **never change** even if the title changes slightly.
> 
> That is the whole point.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Recommended structure for you
> 
> ## Filings
> `FCFCU-FIL-20260416-001`
> 
> ## Exhibits
> `FCFCU-EX-20260416-001`
> 
> ## Drafts not filed
> `FCFCU-DR-20260416-001`
> 
> ## Hearing notes / internal logs
> `FCFCU-INT-20260416-001`
> 
> ---
> 
> # Example full set
> - `FCFCU-FIL-20260416-001` — Emergency Motion to Appear Remotely or Continue Hearing
> - `FCFCU-FIL-20260416-002` — Motion to Vacate Default Judgment
> - `FCFCU-FIL-20260416-003` — Proposed Order
> - `FCFCU-EX-20260416-001` — Ex. N Master Procedural Timeline
> - `FCFCU-EX-20260416-002` — Ex. T Verified December 9 Video Excerpt Summary
> 
> ---
> 
> # One thing not to do
> Do **not** make the IDs too long or too coded, like:
> `30.06_CASE_FCFCU_DOC_QI_04_COURT_SET_2026...`
> 
> That becomes useless in practice.
> 
> Short, stable, unique. That’s it.
> 
> ---
> 
> # My final recommendation
> Use:
> 
> **For filings:**  
> `FCFCU-FIL-YYYYMMDD-###`
> 
> **For exhibits:**  
> `FCFCU-EX-YYYYMMDD-###`
> 
> **Put the ID in the footer of every document.**
> 
> If you want, I’ll give you a **full naming convention sheet** for filings, exhibits, declarations, and hearing packets so everything matches.

### Assistant
> Good. Then use **one universal ID**, and keep the exhibit letter as just a court label, not a second system.
> 
> ## Best single-system format
> Use:
> 
> **`FCFCU-YYYYMMDD-###`**
> 
> Examples:
> - `FCFCU-20260416-001`
> - `FCFCU-20260416-002`
> - `FCFCU-20260416-003`
> 
> That one ID goes on:
> - motions
> - declarations
> - notices
> - proposed orders
> - exhibits
> - summaries
> - internal charts
> 
> ## Why this is the right one
> It is:
> - short
> - sortable
> - human-readable
> - unique
> - neutral enough for court
> - flexible enough for every document
> 
> No extra type code needed unless you really want it.
> 
> ## How to use it
> ### On filings
> Put in footer:
> - `Document ID: FCFCU-20260416-001`
> 
> ### On exhibits
> Keep the court exhibit label, but add the same style of ID:
> - `Ex. N`
> - `Document ID: FCFCU-20260416-014`
> 
> So:
> - the **court** sees `Ex. N`
> - **you** still have one stable ID system
> 
> That is not two systems.  
> That is one ID system plus the court’s exhibit lettering, which you cannot realistically avoid.
> 
> ## My recommended rule
> Assign IDs in the order documents are finalized, not the order they are imagined.
> 
> So if tonight you finalize:
> 1. emergency hearing motion
> 2. proposed order
> 3. Exhibit N
> 4. Exhibit T
> 
> Then:
> - `FCFCU-20260416-001` = emergency motion
> - `FCFCU-20260416-002` = proposed order
> - `FCFCU-20260416-003` = Ex. N
> - `FCFCU-20260416-004` = Ex. T
> 
> ## Best placement
> Put it in two spots:
> 
> ### First page, small line under title or near caption
> `Document ID: FCFCU-20260416-001`
> 
> ### Footer
> `FCFCU-20260416-001`
> 
> That way pages stay tied together if they get separated.
> 
> ## File naming format
> Use:
> 
> **`FCFCU-YYYYMMDD-###_{short_title}`**
> 
> Examples:
> - `FCFCU-20260416-001_Emergency_Motion_to_Appear_Remotely`
> - `FCFCU-20260416-002_Proposed_Order`
> - `FCFCU-20260416-003_EX-N_Master_Procedural_Timeline`
> - `FCFCU-20260416-004_EX-T_Verified_Video_Excerpt_Summary`
> 
> ## One hard rule
> **Never change the ID once assigned.**  
> Even if the title changes.  
> That is the whole point.
> 
> ## My blunt recommendation
> Use exactly this:
> 
> **Universal document ID:**  
> `FCFCU-YYYYMMDD-###`
> 
> That’s it.
>

> Download it here: [fcfcu_defendant_filings_register.csv](sandbox:/mnt/data/fcfcu_defendant_filings_register.csv)
> 
> ## ID format used
> `FCFCU-YYYYMMDD-###-TYPE`
> 
> Examples:
> - `FCFCU-20251211-001-MO`
> - `FCFCU-20251216-008-DE`
> - `FCFCU-20260326-029-EX`
> 
> ## Type codes used
> - `MO` = Motion
> - `DE` = Declaration
> - `EX` = Exhibit / Exhibit Packet
> - `PO` = Proposed Order
> - `NO` = Notice
> - `OB` = Objection
> - `RE` = Response / Correction
> 
> ## What I included
> I treated this as **your filings only**, starting with your first December 2025 filings and running through tonight’s emergency motion.

> Use this in order of preference:

>
